The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Condition that the conclusion must be other than the premises
Commentary explains why the conclusion must be different from the premises to avoid nonsense or circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a syllogism commits the fallacy of petition of principle (musadara) or nonsense (hadhyan) by checking if the conclusion is identical to a premise.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism must produce a new judgment, not repeat a premise.

In any valid syllogism, the conclusion must be different from both premises. This passage from the commentary on the Tadhheeb explains why: if the conclusion were identical to a premise, the argument would either be meaningless (hadhyan) or circular (musadara). Understanding this condition is key to sound reasoning in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion and premises
Given a syllogism, clearly separate the minor premise, major premise, and conclusion. Write them out as full propositions.
This step is essential before you can compare them for identity.
2
Check for identity with each premise
Compare the conclusion word-for-word with each premise. If it matches exactly (both matter and form), then the condition of otherness is violated.
Partial overlap in terms is allowed; the entire proposition must not be identical.
3
Distinguish the two errors
If the identity is unintentional, the argument is hadhyan (nonsense). If intentional, it is musadara (petition of principle). Both are invalid because they produce no new knowledge.
Intent matters in classifying the error, but the logical fault is the same.
4
Watch for exceptive syllogisms
In conditional or disjunctive syllogisms, the conclusion may appear to be mentioned in the premises, but the particle (e.g., 'if') removes the ruling, so the conclusion is not actually stated. This does not violate the condition.
The author notes this nuance to prevent a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ لولاها لكانَ إمَّا هذياناً، أو مصادرة على المطلوب مشتملاً على الدور المهروب منه
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : لَكَانَ) أي : كون النتيجة عينَ إحدى المقدِّمتين (إمَّا هذياناً)؛ أي : عبئاً عند عدم القصدِ، (أو مُصادرةٌ) مع قصدِ ذلك، والمصادرة : جعلُ الدَّعوى جزءاً من الدليل . (قَوْلُهُ: مُشْتَمِلاً) حال من (مصادرة)؛ أي حالَ كونها مشتملة على الدور الحكمي . المطار
الصُّغرى ومحمولَها محمولُ الكُبرى، وأمَّا القياس الاستثنائي؛ فإنَّ المذكور فيه صورةُ النَّتيجة؛ لأنَّ النَّتيجةَ قضيَّةٌ مُشتملةٌ على الحكم، والمذكور في القياس مُقدَّماً أو تالياً لا محكّم فيهِ ؛ لأنَّ الأداةَ أخرجَتْهُ عَنِ التَّمَامِ، وهذا سِرُّ قول المصنف الآتي: (فإنْ كانَ مَذكوراً فيهِ بِمادَّتِهِ وصورتِهِ)، ولم يقل : فإِنْ كَانَ غَيرُ النَّتيجة مذكوراً بالفعل، وفي الحاشية نقلاً عن الهروي أنَّه يرد على التعريف قولنا : كُلُّ إنسان حيوانٌ وَكُلُّ حيوان حيوان، فإِنَّهُ ينتجُ الصُّغرى ا.هـ. وليس بشيء؛ لأنَّ الكُبرى لَغْوُ مِنَ القول.
(قَوْلُهُ: إِذْ لَوْلَاهَا) تعليلٌ لِقَولِهِ : وإِنَّما اشترط ... إلخ، لكن الصواب: لولاه، بتذكيرِ الضَّميرِ ؛ لأنَّ المرجع هو اشتراط الآخريَّة، وحاصل المعنى : إِنَّما اشتراط الآخَريَّةِ، إِذْ لولاهُ لَكَانَتِ النَّتيجةُ إمَّا عينَ المقدِّمتَيْن جَميعاً أو عينَ إحداهما، وأيَّا مَا كانَ فهوَ باطل ؛ لأنَّهُ يؤدّي إلى الهذيان أو المصادرة. (قَوْلُهُ : لَكَانَ إمَّا هَذَيَانًاً) أي : كلاماً غير مقصودٍ فيما إذا كانَتِ النَّتيجة عين المقدمتين .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مُصَادَرَةٌ) وهيَ أخذُ المدَّعى جزءاً من الدَّليل فيما إذا كانَتِ النَّتيجةُ عينَ المقدمتين، وكونُ المدَّعى جزءاً من الدَّليل ؛ لا يفيد المطلوب للزوم الدَّورِ؛ لأنَّ معرفة المدَّعَى موقوفةٌ على معرفةِ الدَّليلِ، فلو كان المدَّعى جزءاً مِنَ الدَّليل؛ لَلَزِمَ أَنْ تكونَ معرفةُ الدَّليل موقوفةً على معرفة المدَّعى؛ لِتَوقُفِ معرفةِ الكُلِّ على معرفة الجزء، وأما إذا كانَتِ النَّتيجة غير المقدِّمتَيْنِ؛ فَلَا يلزمُ شَيءٌ مِن هَذَيْنِ المحذورَيْنِ،
For if not for that [the condition of otherness], the conclusion would be either nonsense or a question-begging [circularity] involving the very circularity from which we flee. Dassuqi: (His statement: 'it would be') i.e., the conclusion being identical to one of the two premises (either nonsense) i.e., a meaningless utterance when there is no intent, (or a petition of principle) with that intent. And petition of principle is making the claim a part of the proof. (His statement: 'involving') a state from (petition of principle); i.e., it being involved in the circularity of judgment. al-Matar: the minor and its predicate is the predicate of the major. As for the exceptive syllogism, the form of the conclusion is mentioned in it because the conclusion is a proposition containing the judgment, and what is mentioned in the syllogism as antecedent or consequent has no ruling because the particle removed it from completeness. And this is the secret of the author's coming statement: (if it is mentioned in it with its matter and form), and he did not say: if the conclusion is other than that mentioned in fact. And in the gloss, quoting al-Harawi, that an objection to the definition is our statement: every human is an animal and every animal is an animal, for it yields the minor [as conclusion]. End quote. And it is nothing because the major is superfluous speech. (His statement: 'for if not for that') an explanation of his statement: and he only conditioned... etc., but the correct [pronoun] is 'loolahu' with masculine pronoun because the referent is the condition of otherness. The summary: he only conditioned otherness because if not for it, the conclusion would be either identical to both premises or to one of them. And whichever it is, it is invalid because it leads to nonsense or petition of principle. (His statement: 'it would be either nonsense') i.e., speech not intended when the conclusion is identical to the premises. (His statement: 'or petition of principle') and it is taking the claim as a part of the proof when the conclusion is identical to the premises. And the claim being part of the proof does not yield the required due to circularity, because knowledge of the claim depends on knowledge of the proof. So if the claim were part of the proof, it would necessitate that knowledge of the proof depends on knowledge of the claim, because knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of the part. But when the conclusion is other than the premises, none of these two harmful consequences follow.
Source pages p. 776

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn statement that explains why the conclusion in a syllogism must be different from the premises. The matn says: 'For if not for that [condition], the conclusion would be either nonsense or a petition of principle (musadara) involving the very circularity we sought to avoid.' The commentator Dassuqi unpacks this. He first explains that 'it would be' refers to the conclusion being identical to one premise. If this happens unintentionally, it is nonsense; if intentional, it is a petition of principle (begging the question). He then clarifies that 'involving the circularity' means the argument contains a vicious circle. He discusses a potential objection regarding the exceptive syllogism, where the conclusion's form is explicitly mentioned, but the particle (e.g., 'if') removes the ruling from the antecedent/consequent, so the conclusion is not actually stated. He then corrects the pronoun in the matn from 'loolaha' (feminine) to 'loolahu' (masculine) because the referent is the condition of otherness. The crux: if the conclusion is identical to a premise, the argument either yields no new information (nonsense) or assumes what it tries to prove (circularity). This condition is essential for a valid syllogism to produce knowledge.

Key Terms
الهذيان
المصادرة
الدور
الآخريَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a syllogism has the premises 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant', and the conclusion is 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant', what error does this commit?
A) Nonsense (hadhyan)
B) Petition of principle (musadara)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's conclusion must be different from both premises to yield new knowledge; otherwise it is either nonsense or circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Is the conclusion identical to either premise? Does it violate the condition of otherness? Justify your answer.
?
Construct a syllogism (on a topic from fiqh or kalam) that violates the condition of otherness by having the conclusion be identical to the minor premise. Classify the error as hadhyan or musadara.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Two Types of Qiyas: Conjunctive and Exceptive
Commentary introduces and explains the fundamental division of syllogism based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a conjunctive (iqtirani) and an exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism based on whether the conclusion is explicitly mentioned with its matter and form.

Core Idea
The division of qiyas hinges on whether the conclusion is stated within the argument or left implicit.

In this lesson, we explore the fundamental division of qiyas (syllogism) as explained by the commentator al-Dassuqi. The classification is based on a simple yet crucial criterion: whether the conclusion (al-qawl al-ākhar) is explicitly mentioned in the syllogism with its matter (subject and predicate) and form (arrangement). Understanding this division is essential for analysing logical arguments in Islamic sciences, where both conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms are used in proofs for theological and legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the division criterion
The matn states that qiyas is divided into conjunctive and exceptive because the conclusion either is mentioned in the syllogism with its matter and form, or not. This is the central distinction: in conjunctive, the conclusion is not expressed; in exceptive, it is.
Note: 'mentioned with its matter and form' means the conclusion appears exactly as it would stand alone, with its subject and predicate and in the appropriate logical form.
2
Define matter and form
Matter (mādda) refers to the content parts of a proposition: the subject and predicate. Form (hay'a) is the structure resulting from the composition and order of those parts. For the conclusion to be 'mentioned,' both must be present explicitly.
In the exceptive syllogism, the form is not uttered as a separate sentence but is implied by the structure (e.g., as the consequent of a conditional).
3
Identify the two types in practice
In conjunctive qiyas (e.g., 'All A are B; all B are C; therefore all A are C'), the conclusion is not stated among the premises. In exceptive qiyas (e.g., 'If A then B; A; therefore B'), the conclusion (B) is explicitly mentioned as the consequent of the conditional premise.
Remember: even if the conclusion is implied, in exceptive it is linguistically present in the premises.
4
Understand the condition of otherness
The commentator explains that the conclusion must be 'other' than the premises. If it were identical to a premise, the argument would be circular (petition of principle). This condition ensures that the syllogism provides new knowledge.
The glosses elaborate that if the conclusion is a premise, the argument is either nonsense (if knowingly used) or a petition of principle (if unknowingly).

(قَوْلُهُ : بِمَادَّتِهِ) أي: أجزائه من الموضوع والمحمول. (قَوْلُهُ: وَهَيْئَتِهِ) أي : صورتِهِ الحاصلة من تركيب أجزائه، وتقديم بعضها على بعض . المطار

(قَوْلُهُ : مَذْكُوراً ) أي : بالذِّكْرِ اللّساني في القياس الملفوظ، وبالذِّكْرِ القلبي في المعقول .
(قَوْلُهُ : بِمَادَتِهِ وَهَيْئَتِهِ ذِكْرُ النَّتيجة ليسَ إلَّا ذِكْرُ أجزائها الماديَّةِ؛ لأنَّ الهيئة ليسَتْ بِمَلفوظةٍ، لكنَّ ذِكْرَها قد يكونُ مُلتبساً بحال كونها بالفعل، وقد يكونُ مُلتَبِساً بحال كونها بالقوَّةِ، وحينئذٍ؛ فالمراد بذكرِ القولِ الآخر بمادَّتِهِ وهيئتِهِ؛ ذِكْرُهُ بالفعل، والمرادُ بِذِكرِهِ بمادَّتِهِ فقط؛ ذِكْرُهُ بالقوَّةِ؛ فَتَدَبَّرُ ا.هـ.
Chapter: On the Syllogism. Division of qiyas into conjunctive and exceptive. Then the qiyas is divided into: conjunctive and exceptive; because the other statement either is mentioned in the qiyas with its matter and form, or not. Dassuqi. And its explanation: that if it [the conclusion] is not other, but rather is identical to one of the two premises of the proof, then it would be a petition of principle because it is not known except from it and the proof depends on it because it is a part of it, and the whole depends on its part. (His statement: 'with its matter') i.e., its parts from subject and predicate. (His statement: 'and its form') i.e., its form resulting from the composition of its parts and the ordering of some before others. al-Matar: For this reason, he conditioned its otherness. And in some glosses, his statement: (it would be either nonsense) i.e., if he knows that it does not refute the opponent and he throws it to him, or petition of principle i.e., if he does not know that, and both are when the conclusion is both premises or only one of them. And their statement: petition of principle is making the claim a part of the proof; i.e., or its very self. End quote. So it is of the type that the commentator said first. And for al-Fadil al-Mar'ashi in Taqrir al-Qawanin there is a precious discussion on the meaning of petition of principle. (His statement: 'then the qiyas... etc.') is the beginning of the division of qiyas after its definition into its primary divisions. And he mentioned the exceptive qiyas first because its concept is existential, and because its production is self-evident in all its signs, and he placed it later in the rulings out of concern for the conjunctive due to the abundance of its topics. (His statement: 'mentioned') i.e., by verbal mention in the uttered qiyas, and by mental mention in the intelligible. (His statement: 'with its matter and form' the mention of the conclusion is only the mention of its material parts, because the form is not uttered, but its mention may be associated with its actuality or potentiality. So then, the meaning of mentioning the other statement with its matter and form is its mention in actuality, and the meaning of mentioning it only with its matter is its mention in potentiality. So ponder. End quote.
Source pages p. 777

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that opens a new chapter on qiyas (syllogism) and introduces its primary division. The matn states: 'Then the qiyas is divided into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i), because the other statement either is mentioned in the qiyas with its matter and form, or not.' The commentator Dassuqi first clarifies that the term 'the other statement' refers to the conclusion. The division is based on whether the conclusion is explicitly expressed (mentioned) in the argument with both its matter (subject and predicate) and form (the specific arrangement). In a conjunctive syllogism, the conclusion is not expressed; in an exceptive syllogism, it is. Dassuqi then explains that if the conclusion were identical to a premise, it would be a petition of principle. He quotes al-Matar on the condition of otherness. He then discusses a gloss that reinterprets the 'nonsense' and 'petition of principle' based on knowledge of the opponent. Finally, he explains that the author mentioned the exceptive first in the division because its concept is existentially clearer and its production is self-evident, but he treats it later in detail to give more attention to the conjunctive, which has more topics. The last part clarifies that 'mentioned with matter and form' means explicit mention (actual), while 'mentioned with matter only' means potential mention.

Which of the following best describes a conjunctive (iqtirani) qiyas?
A) The conclusion is explicitly mentioned as a premise.
B) The conclusion is not mentioned in the syllogism at all.
C) The conclusion is mentioned but with different wording.
D) The conclusion is identical to one of the premises.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of qiyas into conjunctive and exceptive depends on whether the conclusion is explicitly mentioned with its matter and form, and the condition of otherness prevents circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following syllogism as conjunctive or exceptive: 'If a prayer is invalid due to impurity, it must be repeated. This prayer is invalid due to impurity. Therefore it must be repeated.' Explain your answer with reference to matter and form.
?
Identify the petition of principle in this flawed argument: 'The Quran is the word of Allah because it says so in the Quran.' Rewrite it as a valid syllogism.

The Syllogism

Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Defense against Circularity
Definition and defense of conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a conditional syllogism and explain why including the conclusion in the premises does not entail circularity.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism includes the conclusion potentially, not as a premise; no circularity.

This passage from the Tadhheeb Sharh defines the conditional (istithna'i) syllogism and addresses a common objection: if the conclusion is already mentioned in the premises, the argument seems circular. The author clarifies that the conclusion is present only potentially—its terms and relation appear in the conditional premise, but the conclusion itself is a separate proposition. We will walk through the definition and the logical refutation of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature
A syllogism is called istithna'i if the conclusion (or its contradictory) is mentioned in it, either actually or potentially. The passage says 'if the other statement is mentioned in it in its matter and form.'
Matter = two terms; form = arrangement and relation.
2
Distinguish matter from form
The author explains that 'matter' means the two terms of the conclusion (subject and predicate), and 'form' means the detailed relation between them in the order they appear, including whether the relation is affirmative or negative.
This precision avoids confusion: the conclusion must be present in structure, not just as a sentence.
3
Understand potential vs. actual mention
The author refines the definition: the conclusion is not actually mentioned as a premise, but potentially, close to actuality. For example, in 'If the sun is rising, daytime exists; but the sun is rising; so daytime exists,' the conclusion's terms and relation are in the first premise, but the conclusion itself is a new proposition.
This resolves the concern that the conclusion might be a premise.
4
Analyze the circularity objection and response
The objection is: if the conclusion is in the premises, the syllogism begs the question. The response: the first premise is the conditional statement itself, not the consequent. The second premise is the antecedent. The conclusion is a different proposition derived from both. Thus, no circularity.
The example with 'body' and 'extended' illustrates this clearly.

Source Text

فإن كان القول الآخر أي: النتيجة مذكوراً فيه أي: في القياس بمادته أي: طرفيه وهيئته أي: صورته فاستثنائي كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، فالقول الآخر - وهو النهار موجود - مذكور في القياس بمادته وهيئته.

(قَوْلُهُ : أي: صُوْرَتِهِ) تَفسيرٌ للهيئة، والمراد : الهيئةُ التَّأليفيَّةُ، كذا قال الفاضل الرَّازِيُّ، وقال بعضُ الشُّرَّاحِ : أرادَ بمادَّةِ القول الآخر: طرفَي النتيجة، وبهيئته: النّسبةَ التَّفصيليَّةَ بينهما على التّرتيب الذي وقعا عليه في النتيجة؛ سواء كانت الكيفية التي عليها في النتيجة مِنَ الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ أو لا، وحاصلُهُ : أَنَّ النَّتيجةَ إِنْ كانت مذكورةً أي: موجودةً في القياس بطرفَيها، والنسبة التفصيلية بينهما؛ سواء كانت عينَ النِّسبةِ التَّفصيليَّةِ الَّتي في النتيجةِ مِن الوقوع أو اللاوقوع أو نقيضها، وإنْ لم تكن متعلّقاً للإيقاع أو الانتزاع؛ فهو الاستثنائيُّ، فَلَا يرد عليهِ أنَّ القول الآخر بمادَّتِهِ وهيئته هو عين النتيجة ، فَلَا يجوز أن يكون مذكوراً في القياس، وإلا لزمَتِ المصادرة، وَلَا أنَّ هذا وإن كان صادقاً على القياس الاستثنائي بوضع المقدَّم، لكنَّه لا يصدقُ على رفع التّالي ؛ إذ المذكورُ فيهِ نقيضُ النَّتيجةِ لَا غيرُه، ولهذا اشتُهِرَ تفسيره بما كانَتِ النَّتيجةُ أو نَقيضُها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل، لكن الأظهر في تفسيره أن يُقال: هو ما كانَتِ النَّتيجةُ أو نقيضُها مذكوراً فيهِ بالقوَّةِ القريبةِ مِنَ الفعل.

قال المصنِّفُ : فإِنْ قِيلَ : اشتمال القياس على النتيجة بالفعل ينافي وجوب مغايرة النتيجة لمقدّماتِ القياس؛ قُلْنَا : لَا منافاة، فإنَّ النَّتيجةَ في مثل قولنا : إن كان هذا جسماً ؛ فهوَ مُتحيَّر، لكنَّه جسم، هي القضيَّةُ المحتملةُ لِلصِّدقِ والكذب؛ أعني قولنا : هذا متحيَّر، وهوَ مُغايرُ لِكُلِّ مِن مُقدِّمتَي القياس؛ لأنَّ المقدّمة الأُولى هي الشَّرطيَّةُ المشتملة على الحكم بلزوم التّالي للمقدَّم؛ أعني قولنا : إن كان هذا جسماً فهو مُتحيَّز؛ لَا نفسَ التَّالي والمقدّم؛ لأنَّهُ ليس بقضيَّةٍ، والمقدِّمةُ الثَّانية هي قوله : لكنَّهُ جسم. ا.هـ. فَعُلِمَ مِن هذا سقوط البحثِ الآتي في الشَّارح، فَلَا سَهوَ وَلَا تَسامح.
If the other statement, i.e., the conclusion, is mentioned in it, i.e., in the syllogism, in its matter, i.e., its two terms, and its form, i.e., its figure, then it is an exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism, like our statement: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists, but the sun is rising, so daytime exists.' Thus the other statement—namely, 'daytime exists'—is mentioned in the syllogism in its matter and form.

(His statement: 'i.e., its figure') is an interpretation of 'form', and the intended meaning is the compositional form, as stated by al-Fadl al-Razi. Some commentators said: By 'matter of the other statement' he meant the two terms of the conclusion, and by its 'form' he meant the detailed relation between them in the order they occur in the conclusion, whether the quality of affirmation or negation in the conclusion is the same or not. The upshot is: If the conclusion is mentioned, i.e., exists in the syllogism with its two terms and the detailed relation between them—whether that relation is identical to the detailed relation in the conclusion in terms of occurrence or non-occurrence or its contradictory, and even if it is not subject to affirmation or abstraction—then it is the exceptive syllogism. Thus it is not objected that the other statement in its matter and form is identical to the conclusion, so it cannot be mentioned in the syllogism, otherwise circularity would follow; nor that this definition, though true of the exceptive syllogism when the antecedent is posited, does not apply when the consequent is negated, since what is mentioned there is the contradictory of the conclusion and nothing else. Hence the famous definition: it is that in which the conclusion or its contradictory is actually mentioned. But the more evident definition is: it is that in which the conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned potentially, close to actuality.

The author said: If it is said: The inclusion of the conclusion actually in the syllogism contradicts the necessity of the conclusion being different from the premises of the syllogism—we say: There is no contradiction, for the conclusion in our statement, for example, 'If this is a body, then it is extended; but it is a body' is the proposition that admits truth and falsehood, namely our statement 'This is extended', and it is different from each of the two premises of the syllogism. Because the first premise is the conditional statement that contains the judgment that the consequent is necessarily entailed by the antecedent—namely our statement 'If this is a body, then it is extended'—not the consequent and antecedent themselves, since they are not a proposition. The second premise is the statement 'but it is a body'—end. So from this it is known that the following investigation by the commentator is refuted; there is neither error nor negligence.
Source pages p. 778

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional (istithna'i) syllogism and defends it against an objection of circularity. The author begins by stating that a syllogism is exceptive (istithna'i) if the conclusion (the 'other statement') is mentioned in the syllogism both in its matter (the two terms) and its form (the arrangement and relation). He gives an example: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but the sun is rising; therefore daytime exists.' In this example, the conclusion 'daytime exists' clearly appears in the first premise (as the consequent) and in the second premise? Actually, the second premise is 'but the sun is rising', not the conclusion. The point is that the conclusion's terms and relation are present in the premises: the first premise contains both terms ('sun rising' and 'daytime exists') as antecedent and consequent. But the conclusion itself is not explicitly stated as a premise; it is derived. The author then expands on the definition: some commentators distinguish 'matter' (the terms) from 'form' (the detailed relation). The key point is that the conclusion or its contradictory must be potentially present in the premises, close to actuality. The objection is that if the conclusion is actually present, the syllogism would be circular (the conclusion would be a premise). The response shows that the conclusion is not a premise: the first premise is the conditional (if-then) statement, which is not the same as the conclusion; the second premise is the affirmation of the antecedent; the conclusion is a separate proposition. Thus there is no circularity. This refines the definition to avoid a misunderstanding.

A) Because the conclusion is more general than the premises.
B) Because the conditional premise is a conditional statement, not the conclusion itself; the conclusion is a separate derived proposition.
C) Because the conclusion is actually identical to the major premise.
D) Because the conclusion is derived by induction, not deduction.

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is valid because the conclusion is only potentially present in the premises, not as a premise itself, thus avoiding circularity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Definition and Conditions of the Exceptional Syllogism
Authors critique and refine the definition of the exceptional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid and invalid forms of the exceptional syllogism and apply the condition that the conclusion or its opposite must be explicitly mentioned.

Core Idea
The exceptional syllogism is defined by explicit mention of the conclusion or its opposite.

In this passage, commentators refine the definition of the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). They clarify that the only valid forms are assuming the exact antecedent (yielding the exact consequent) or assuming the opposite of the consequent (yielding the opposite of the antecedent). They also stress a key condition: the conclusion or its opposite must be explicitly present in the syllogism. This lesson helps you internalize these rules and recognize common errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of an exceptional syllogism
An exceptional syllogism has a conditional premise (e.g., 'If it intoxicates, then it is forbidden') and an assumption that affirms or negates one part. The conclusion must be derivable from these two premises.
The conditional can be conjunctive (mutasil) or disjunctive (munfasil); our focus is on the conjunctive type.
2
Learn the two valid forms
Valid forms: (1) Assume the exact antecedent (muqaddam) → conclude the exact consequent (tali). (2) Assume the opposite of the consequent (naqid al-tali) → conclude the opposite of the antecedent. These correspond to modus ponens and modus tollens.
Memorize: ayn al-muqaddam → ayn al-tali; naqid al-tali → naqid al-muqaddam.
3
Recognize the invalid forms
Assuming the opposite of the antecedent (naqid al-muqaddam) or the exact consequent (ayn al-tali) generally yields no necessary conclusion, unless the material (al-madda) forces equivalence. The passage warns against this trap.
Example: 'If it is a body, it is determinate.' Assuming 'it is not a body' does not lead to 'it is not determinate' unless body and determinate are equivalent.
4
Apply the explicit mention condition
The definition requires that the conclusion or its opposite be explicitly mentioned (bil-fi'l) as a single sentence in the syllogism. This means the parts and their relation must appear, not just scattered terms.
Without this condition, a syllogism could be incomplete or ambiguous.

(قَوْلُهُ : فَفِي الْعِبَارَةِ سَهْوٌ مِنَ النَّاسِخِ ... إِلَخ) قِيلَ : في هذه العبارة سهو من الشَّارحِ أو تسمح منه لما أنَّ ذِكْرَ القولِ بهيئته ومادَّتِهِ معناه أنْ تُذكرَ الأطرافُ مع الرَّبْطِ بينَهُمَا ؛ لَا بدونِهِ، بأنْ يُذكر موضوع في مقدّمةٍ ومحمولٌ في أُخرى، ولا شكَّ في وجودِ ذلك في القياس الاستثنائي ولو استثنى منه نقيضُ المقدَّم، وهذا تحقيق مِنَ المصنفِ، وشرح لقول القومِ : ما تكونُ عينُ النَّتيجة أو نقيضُها مذكوراً فيهِ بالفعل الموهم وجودُ الحُكْمِ في مقدَّم أو تالي الشَّرطيَّةِ، يُومِيُّ إلى ذلك كلامُهُ في شرح الرّسالةِ حيثُ قالَ : وَمَعنى كونِ النَّتيجة مذكورةً بالفعل في القياس؛ أنَّها بأجزائها الماديَّةِ وهيئتها التّاليفيَّةِ مَذكورةٌ فيه ؛ وإن طرأ عليها ما أخرجَهَا عَنْ كونها
Section: On the Syllogism.
And in the expression there is a discussion; because if we say in the example: 'But the sun is not rising' it yields: 'the day does not exist', and then the definition does not apply to it. Al-Dasuqi said: And his statement: 'So the day exists' – this is the conclusion; because what comes after 'fa' is the conclusion. So it is known that it is the reverse of the conjunctive syllogism, so understand it and apply analogously to what resembles it. End of commentary. (His statement: 'And in the expression there is a discussion') – its summary: that the author's statement 'If it was... etc.' does not cover from the exceptional syllogism except that in which the exact antecedent is assumed, yielding the exact consequent, not that in which the opposite of the consequent is assumed, yielding the opposite of the antecedent. And it may be answered that the author's expression covers that by saying: it is mentioned or its opposite. (His statement: 'But the sun is not rising' – the correct thing is to take the opposite of the consequent; because the opposite of the antecedent does not produce a valid product, and it only produces here because of the specificity of the matter; because assuming the exact antecedent is determinate, and it is different from both premises of the syllogism; because the first premise is the conditional that contains the judgment of the consequent's entailment by the antecedent – meaning our statement: 'If this is a body, then it is determinate' – not the actual consequent and antecedent; because it is not a proposition, and the second premise is his statement: 'But it is a body.' So it is known from this that the following discussion in the commentator is invalid, so there is no mistake and no laxity. (His statement: 'And in the expression there is a discussion') – you already know its refutation. (His statement: 'Because if we said in the example: But the sun is not rising... etc.') – this is a mistake from him; the correct thing is: 'The day does not exist' yields 'The sun is not rising'; said by some. Because the conclusion is not mentioned with its matter and form in the syllogism, rather what is mentioned in it is the opposite of the conclusion. For this reason, it occurs in all logic books: that the exceptional syllogism is that in which the exact conclusion or its opposite is explicitly mentioned. So in the expression there is a mistake from the copyist or laxity from the author. Al-Dasuqi said: It yields the exact consequent, and the opposite of the consequent yields the opposite of the antecedent, unlike the opposite of the antecedent or the exact consequent, which does not yield, reflect. (His statement: 'And for this') – meaning: and because it mentions the opposite of the conclusion; it occurs... etc. (His statement: 'Explicitly') – by the conclusion or its opposite being mentioned as one sentence. Al-'Attar said: And the reason it is a mistake is that assuming the opposite of the antecedent is non-productive; and it may be said that his purpose is merely giving an example, even if assuming the opposite of the antecedent is sterile. (His statement: 'So in the expression there is a mistake from the copyist... etc.') – it is said: in this expression there is a mistake from the commentator or laxity, because when the statement is mentioned with its form and matter, it means that the parts are mentioned with the connection between them, not without it, such that a subject is mentioned in one premise and a predicate in another; and there is no doubt of that existing in the exceptional syllogism even if one assumes the opposite of the antecedent. And this is a verification from the author and an explanation of the people's statement: that the exact conclusion or its opposite is explicitly mentioned (which gives the impression that there is a judgment in the antecedent or consequent of the conditional), and his statement in his commentary on the Risala indicates that: 'And the meaning of the conclusion being explicitly mentioned in the syllogism is that it is mentioned with its material parts and its compositional form, even if something occurs that removes it from being a conclusion.'
Source pages p. 779

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The discussion begins with an objection (bahth) to the author's phrasing: if we take the example 'But the sun is not rising' (which assumes the opposite of the antecedent, i.e., negating the antecedent), the conclusion 'the day does not exist' follows, but the definition does not cover this case because the conclusion is not explicitly mentioned. The commentator explains that the correct move is to assume the opposite of the consequent (nagid al-tali) to produce the opposite of the antecedent, not vice versa. However, in this example, the assumption of the opposite of the antecedent still yields a result due to the specific material (al-madda) – the antecedent and consequent are equal (musawat), so the negation of one implies the negation of the other. But generally, the opposite of the antecedent is non-productive. The discussion then affirms that the definition of the exceptional syllogism should require that the exact conclusion or its opposite be explicitly mentioned (bil-fi'l) in the syllogism. This is the standard definition found in other logic books. The commentator notes that the author's example seems flawed because it omits the conclusion, but this could be a copyist error or authorial laxity. The passage concludes with a justification that the definition is about the form and matter (hay'a wa madda) of the conclusion being present, even if the conclusion itself is not stated explicitly, because the parts and their relation are there. This is further explained by reference to the commentary on al-Risala. Throughout, the text moves from objection to resolution, defending the definition and clarifying conditions for valid exceptional syllogisms.

نتيجة

تالي

In an exceptional syllogism with the conditional 'If something is intoxicating, then it is forbidden', which assumption yields the conclusion 'It is not intoxicating'?
A) Assume: It is intoxicating.

C) Assume: It is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The exceptional syllogism is valid only when the assumption matches the exact antecedent or the opposite of the consequent, and the conclusion must be explicitly mentioned in the syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Refining the Definition of Istithna'i: What Does 'Actually Mentioned' Mean?
Critical refinement on the definition of istithna'i syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the refined definition of an istithna'i syllogism and explain why the conclusion need not appear verbatim.

Core Idea
The conclusion's matter and form are distributed across premises — that is 'actually mentioned'.

Some logicians define the istithna'i syllogism as one where the conclusion or its opposite is actually mentioned. Yet in many valid examples, the conclusion is not stated as a single sentence. The commentators resolve this tension by refining what 'actually mentioned' means: the material parts (subject, predicate) and the compositional form (affirmation/negation) must be present across the premises, even if not as a single proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
Notice that in an istithna'i, the conclusion (or its opposite) is not always stated verbatim. For example, in modus tollens, the conclusion 'not A' is not explicitly in any premise, but its opposite 'A' appears in the conditional.
The text says: 'Because the conclusion is not mentioned with its matter and form in the syllogism, rather its opposite is mentioned.'
2
Understand the refined interpretation
The commentators explain that 'mentioned with its matter and form' means the subject, predicate, and logical connection are present across the premises. The conditional premise provides the relation; the exceptive provides one part. Together they constitute the conclusion's matter and form.
Al-Attar: 'The meaning of the conclusion being actually mentioned is that it is mentioned with its material parts and its compositional form.'
3
Exemplify the distinction
Take the conditional: 'If it is khamr, then it is forbidden.' Exceptive: 'It is not forbidden.' Conclusion: 'It is not khamr.' The conclusion's matter (khamr, forbidden) and form (negation) are distributed: 'khamr' appears in the antecedent, 'forbidden' in the consequent, and the negation in the exceptive.
The conclusion is not a separate sentence, but its logical components are all present.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
In usul al-fiqh, a mujtahid uses conditional reasoning. For instance: 'If the sale is invalid due to gharar, then it is void. This sale is not void.' The conclusion 'The sale is valid' (or 'not invalid'?) Actually careful: the valid conclusion from modus tollens is 'The sale is not invalid due to gharar' – but the material parts are present.
This refinement prevents a too-literal reading of the definition.

لعدم ذكر النتيجة بمادتها وهيئتها في القياس، بل المذكور فيه نقيضُ النتيجة . ولهذا وقع في سائر الكتب المنطقيَّة : أنَّ القياس الاستثنائي هو ما يكون عين النتيجة أو نقيضُها مذكوراً فيه بالفعل، ففي العبارة(۱) سهو من النَّاسخ ، أو تسامح من المصنّف . الدسوقي ينتج عينَ التَّالي، ونقيض التّالي ينتج نقيض المقدَّم، بخلافِ نقيضِ المقدَّمِ أو عينِ التالي، فلا ينتج، تأمَّل. (قَوْلُهُ: وَلِهَذَا ) أي : وَلأجل كونه يذكرُ فيه نقيض النتيجة؛ وقع ... إلخ. (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْفِعْل بأنْ تكونَ النَّتيجة أو نقيضُها مذكورة فيه جملة واحدة. العطار ووَجْهُ كونِهِ سهواً ؛ أنَّ استثناء نقيض المقدَّم غيرُ منتج، وقد يُقالُ: غرضُهُ مجرَّدُ التَّمثيل وإنْ كانَ استثناء نقيض المقدَّم عقيماً . (قَوْلُهُ : فَفِي الْعِبَارَةِ سَهْوٌ مِنَ النَّاسِخِ ... إِلَخ) قِيلَ : في هذه العبارة سهو من الشَّارحِ أو تسمح منه لما أنَّ ذِكْرَ القولِ بهيئته ومادَّتِهِ معناه أنْ تُذكرَ الأطرافُ مع الرَّبْطِ بينَهُمَا ؛ لَا بدونِهِ، بأنْ يُذكر موضوع في مقدّمةٍ ومحمولٌ في أُخرى، ولا شكَّ في وجودِ ذلك في القياس الاستثنائي ولو استثنى منه نقيضُ المقدَّم، وهذا تحقيق مِنَ المصنفِ، وشرح لقول القومِ : ما تكونُ عينُ النَّتيجة أو نقيضُها مذكوراً فيهِ بالفعل الموهم وجودُ الحُكْمِ في مقدَّم أو تالي الشَّرطيَّةِ، يُومِيُّ إلى ذلك كلامُهُ في شرح الرّسالةِ حيثُ قالَ : وَمَعنى كونِ النَّتيجة مذكورةً بالفعل في القياس؛ أنَّها بأجزائها الماديَّةِ وهيئتها التّاليفيَّةِ مَذكورةٌ فيه ; وإن طرأ عليها ما أخرجَهَا عَنْ كونها - إلى قوله: المناطقة.
Because the conclusion is not mentioned with its matter and form in the syllogism, rather its opposite is mentioned. And for this reason it occurs in the other books of logic that the conditional syllogism is that in which the conclusion itself or its opposite is actually mentioned. So in the expression there is an oversight by the copyist or a leniency by the author. Al-Dassuqi: It produces the same consequent, and the opposite of the consequent produces the opposite of the antecedent, contrary to the opposite of the antecedent or the same consequent, so it does not produce. Reflect. His statement: 'and for this' means: because of his mentioning the opposite of the conclusion it occurred... etc. His statement: 'actually' that the conclusion or its opposite is mentioned in it as one sentence. Al-Attar: And the reason it is an oversight is that excepting the opposite of the antecedent is not productive, and it may be said: his purpose is merely exemplification even if excepting the opposite of the antecedent is sterile. His statement: 'so in the expression there is an oversight by the copyist... etc'. It is said: in this expression there is an oversight by the commentator or a leniency from him because the mention of the statement with its form and matter means that the terms are mentioned with the connection between them, not without it, by mentioning a subject in one premise and a predicate in another, and there is no doubt about the existence of that in the conditional syllogism even if he excepts from it the opposite of the antecedent. And this is a verification by the author and an explanation of the statement of the people: that which is the conclusion itself or its opposite is actually mentioned in it, which gives the impression that there is a judgment in the antecedent or consequent of the conditional. His words in the commentary on al-Risala allude to that where he said: The meaning of the conclusion being actually mentioned in the syllogism is that it is mentioned in it with its material parts and its compositional form, even if something occurs that removes it from being... to his statement: the logicians.
Source pages p. 780

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the definition of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The author of the hashiya (likely Dassuqi or al-Attar) addresses an apparent issue in the main text (matn) regarding whether the conclusion or its opposite must be explicitly mentioned. The text explains that because in a conditional syllogism the conclusion (or its opposite) is not explicitly stated with its matter and form, some logicians define it as that in which the conclusion or its opposite is actually mentioned. The author notes that if the expression suggests otherwise, it is either a scribal error or authorial leniency. Dassuqi then clarifies the productive moods: if you affirm the antecedent, the consequent follows; if you deny the consequent, the antecedent is denied; but denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent is not productive. The commentary then argues that mentioning the 'statement' with its form and matter means the terms are linked together; in a conditional syllogism, this is present even if the opposite of the antecedent is excepted, because the subject and predicate appear across the premises. Thus the definition is refined: the conclusion is actually present when its material parts and compositional form are distributed across the premises. The discussion underscores the precision required in logical definitions and the risk of confusing the form of a syllogism with its actual expression.

B) The conclusion's material parts and compositional form are present across the premises.
C) The conclusion is not mentioned at all, only its opposite.
D) The conclusion must be derived from the premises by the reader.

Key Takeaway
The 'actual mention' of the conclusion in an istithna'i means its material parts and form are present in the premises, not a single sentence.

The Syllogism

The Four Moods of Istithna'i: Which Are Productive?
Critical refinement on the definition of istithna'i syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which moods of the istithna'i syllogism are valid and which are invalid, and apply this to Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Only affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are valid; the other two moods are sterile.

Dassuqi's note in the passage succinctly states: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent; denying the consequent yields the opposite of the antecedent; but denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent are not productive. Understanding these four moods is essential for correct conditional reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
List the four possible exceptions
In a conditional proposition 'If A then B', you can either affirm or deny the antecedent (A) or affirm or deny the consequent (B). These are the four possible exceptions in an istithna'i.
The text calls these possibilities the 'moods' of the istithna'i.
2
Recall the two valid moods
Affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) leads to the consequent being true. Denying the consequent (modus tollens) leads to the opposite of the antecedent being true. These are the only productive moods.
Dassuqi: 'It produces the same consequent, and the opposite of the consequent produces the opposite of the antecedent.'
3
Identify the two invalid moods
Denying the antecedent does not lead to the opposite of the consequent; the consequent could still be true for other reasons. Affirming the consequent does not lead to the antecedent; the antecedent could be false while the consequent is true for other reasons.
The text says these 'do not produce' (la yuntij).
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Conditional: 'If the sale is invalid due to gharar, then it is void.' Denying the antecedent: 'The sale is not invalid due to gharar' – does this mean it is not void? No, it could be void for another reason. Affirming the consequent: 'The sale is void' – does this mean it is invalid due to gharar? No, it could be void for another reason.
This shows the importance of recognizing invalid moods in ijtihad.

The Syllogism
beginner
6 min
The Istithna'i Syllogism: Conditional with an Exceptive Particle
Author defines the istithna'i syllogism by its exceptive particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key feature of an istithna'i syllogism—the presence of an exceptive particle and an explicitly stated conclusion—and distinguish it from the iqtirani.

Core Idea
An istithna'i syllogism includes an exceptive particle and states its conclusion explicitly.

Not all syllogisms are straightforward categorical deductions. In Islamic logic, a special type called istithna'i (conditional) is defined by the presence of an exceptive particle—specifically 'lakinna' (but)—and the explicit mention of the conclusion or its opposite. This lesson unpacks that definition and explains why logicians count 'lakinna' as an exceptive particle, even though grammarians do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptive particle
Look for a particle like 'lakinna' (but) in the syllogism. The passage states that the exceptive particle is 'lakinna' according to logicians, not grammarians.
Logicians broaden the definition of exceptive particles to include words that carry an exceptive meaning, even if not traditionally classified as such.
2
Check for explicit conclusion
In an istithna'i syllogism, the conclusion (or its opposite) is explicitly stated as a separate statement, not just implied. The passage contrasts this with iqtirani where the conclusion is not mentioned with its full form.
3
Understand the logical distinction
The term 'istithna'i' is derived from the Arabic word for exception (istithna'). The syllogism is so named because it includes an exceptive particle, which signals a conditional or exceptive relationship between the premises.
This distinguishes it from categorical syllogisms where no such particle appears.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
In usul al-fiqh, conditional arguments are common. For instance, 'If the guardian gives permission, then the marriage is valid; but if not, then the marriage is invalid' can be structured as an istithna'i syllogism with an explicit conclusion.
Identifying the exceptive particle here helps classify the reasoning form correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ وَإِنَّما سُمِّي استثنائيا ; لاشتماله على أداة الاستثناء، وَهِيَ : «لكن». (وَإِلَّا ) ( ) أي : وإن لم يكن القولُ الآخَرُ مذكوراً فيه بمادَّتِهِ وهيئته؛ (فَاقْتِرَانِيُّ، كَقولنا : كلُّ جسم مؤلَّفٌ ، وكلُّ مؤلَّفٍ مُحدَثٌ - أي : عند أهل المنطقِ لَا عندَ النَّحويِّينَ - فكلّ جسمِ محدث. فالقولُ الآخَرُ وهو : كلُّ جسمٍ مُحدَةٌ»، ليس مذكوراً في القياس بهيئته، ويُسمى اقترانيا; لاقتران الحدودِ فيه، وستَعرِفُ الحدود بعد ذلك . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى أَدَاةِ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ) أي: عند المنطقيّينَ لا عند النَّحويين. (قَوْلُهُ : مُؤَلَّفٌ) أي : مركَّب تركيباً فيه أُلفة . (قَوْلُهُ : لَيْسَ مَذْكُوراً . . . إلخ) أي : وإِنَّما هو متفرّقُ فيه . المطار قضيَّةٌ وعن احتمالِ الصِّدْقِ والكذب .ا.هـ. وهو بمعنى ما نقلْنَاهُ عن بعض الشَّارحينَ، لكنَّ هذا القائِلَ وقعَ في سهو نَبَّهَ عليه قبلَ ذلكَ حيثُ قالَ: وَلو استثنى منه نقيض المقدَّمِ ; فقد وقع فيما اعترض به . (قَوْلُهُ: لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى أَدَاةِ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ وَهِيَ : لَكِنْ) فَإِنَّهُ في معنى إلَّا» في الاستثناء المنقطع، فَعدَّه الميزانيُّونَ النَّاظرون إلى المعنى حرف استثناء، كما عدَّ «إلا في المنقطع حرف استثناء؛ أفاده العصام. (قَوْلُهُ : لَيْسَ مَذْكُوْرَاً فِي الْقِيَاسِ بِهَيْئَتِهِ) بل الجسم مذكور في المقدمة الأولى والمؤلَّفُ فى الثَّانيةِ، وهذا الذِّكْرُ ليسَ ذِكْراً للهيئة المجموعة، وهو ظاهرُ، ثمَّ إِنَّ مصبَّ النَّفي جميعُ قولِهِ : بمادَّتِهِ وهيئتِهِ، فَلَا يُنافي ذكره بمادَّتِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : لِاقْتِرَانِ الْحُدُودِ فِيْهِ أي : لاقتران حدودِ القياس مِنَ الأصغرِ والأكبر والأوسط، قال العصام والأظهرُ أنْ يُقالَ : سُمِّيَ اقترانيا; لاشتماله على أداة الجمع والاقترانِ وهي الواوُ الواصلة .
Chapter: On Syllogism. It is called 'istithna'i' because it includes an exceptive particle, which is 'lakinna'. (And if not) i.e., if the other statement is not mentioned in it with its matter and form, then it is 'iqtirani', like our statement: Every body is composed, and every composed is originated (i.e., according to the logicians, not the grammarians), so every body is originated. So the other statement, which is 'every body is originated', is not mentioned in the syllogism with its form, and it is called 'iqtirani' due to the conjunction of the terms in it, and you will know the terms after this. Al-Dassuqi: His statement: 'on an exceptive particle' i.e., according to the logicians, not the grammarians. His statement: 'composed' i.e., compounded with a composition that has harmony. His statement: 'is not mentioned... etc.' i.e., rather it is scattered in it. Al-Attar: A proposition and about the possibility of truth and falsehood. He said: it is in the meaning of what we transmitted from some commentators, but this speaker fell into an oversight which he pointed out before where he said: 'and if he excepts from it the opposite of the antecedent, then he fell into what he objected to.' His statement: 'because it includes an exceptive particle, which is 'lakinna'' for it is in the meaning of 'illa' in the disjunctive exception, so the Mizan scholars who look at meaning counted it as a particle of exception, just as they counted 'illa' in the disjunctive exception as a particle of exception; as stated by al-Isam. His statement: 'is not mentioned in the syllogism with its form' rather, 'body' is mentioned in the first premise and 'composed' in the second, and this mention is not a mention of the collective form, and that is clear. Then the object of negation is all of his statement: 'with its matter and form', so it does not negate its mention with its matter. His statement: 'due to the conjunction of the terms in it' i.e., due to the conjunction of the terms of the syllogism from the minor, major, and middle. Al-Isam said: and the more apparent is that it is called 'iqtirani' because it includes a particle of conjunction and connection, which is the connective 'waw'.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the two main types of syllogism: istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (categorical). The author begins by stating that a syllogism is called istithna'i when it includes an exceptive particle, specifically 'lakinna' (but). Then he explains that if the conclusion (the 'other statement') is not mentioned with its matter and form, the syllogism is iqtirani. He gives an example: 'Every body is composed, and every composed is originated, therefore every body is originated.' Here, the conclusion 'every body is originated' is not stated as a separate statement with its full form; it is distributed across the premises, hence it is iqtirani. The name iqtirani comes from the conjunction (iqtiran) of the terms within the syllogism. The commentators expand on these points: Al-Dassuqi clarifies that 'composed' means having a composition with harmony. Al-Attar notes that 'lakinna' is considered an exceptive particle by logicians because it has the meaning of 'illa' in disjunctive exception. He also points out a potential oversight in someone's earlier remark about excepting the opposite of the antecedent. The commentary further explains that 'body' is mentioned in the first premise and 'composed' in the second, so the conclusion's form is not explicitly present as a whole. The naming 'iqtirani' is due to the conjunction of the minor, major, and middle terms, or alternatively because it contains a conjunction particle (the 'waw' connecting premises). This lesson establishes the foundational classification of syllogisms in Aristotelian logic as adapted by Muslim logicians.

The Syllogism
beginner
7 min
The Iqtirani Syllogism: Categorical with Conjoined Terms
Author defines the iqtirani syllogism and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the iqtirani (categorical) syllogism by its lack of an exceptive particle and its conjoined terms, and construct a simple iqtirani syllogism using the example from the text.

Core Idea
An iqtirani syllogism is a categorical syllogism where the conclusion is implicit, and the terms are conjoined across the premises.

The second major type of syllogism in Islamic logic is the iqtirani, or categorical, syllogism. Unlike the istithna'i, it contains no exceptive particle. Its conclusion is not stated as a separate statement; rather, it emerges from the conjunction of the terms across the two premises. The passage gives the classic example: 'Every body is composed, and every composed is originated, therefore every body is originated.' This lesson unpacks the structure and naming of this fundamental logical form.

Guided Path
1
Confirm there is no exceptive particle
Check if the syllogism contains a particle like 'lakinna' or 'illa'. The iqtirani has none. The passage defines it by the absence of an exceptive particle.
2
Identify the three terms
Look for the minor term (asghar), major term (akbar), and middle term (awsat). In the example: 'body' is the minor, 'originated' is the major, 'composed' is the middle. The middle term connects the two premises.
The passage mentions that you will learn about these terms later, but we can identify them here.
3
Recognize that the conclusion is not explicitly stated
The conclusion 'every body is originated' is derived from conjoined premises but is not mentioned as a separate statement with its full form. The passage says it is 'scattered' in the premises.
This is the key difference from istithna'i.
4
Understand the naming rationale
The term 'iqtirani' comes from 'iqtiran' meaning conjunction. The syllogism is so named because the terms are conjoined (muqtarina) across the premises. Alternatively, some scholars say it is due to the conjunction particle 'waw' connecting the premises.
The passage attributes this to al-Isam.

Source Text

(وَإِلَّا ) ( ) أي : وإن لم يكن القولُ الآخَرُ مذكوراً فيه بمادَّتِهِ وهيئته؛ (فَاقْتِرَانِيُّ، كَقولنا : كلُّ جسم مؤلَّفٌ ، وكلُّ مؤلَّفٍ مُحدَثٌ - أي : عند أهل المنطقِ لَا عندَ النَّحويِّينَ - فكلّ جسمِ محدث. فالقولُ الآخَرُ وهو : كلُّ جسمٍ مُحدَةٌ»، ليس مذكوراً في القياس بهيئته، ويُسمى اقترانيا; لاقتران الحدودِ فيه، وستَعرِفُ الحدود بعد ذلك . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى أَدَاةِ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ) أي: عند المنطقيّينَ لا عند النَّحويين. He said: it is in the meaning of what we transmitted from some commentators, but this speaker fell into an oversight which he pointed out before where he said: 'and if he excepts from it the opposite of the antecedent, then he fell into what he objected to.' Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the two main types of syllogism: istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (categorical). The author begins by stating that a syllogism is called istithna'i when it includes an exceptive particle, specifically 'lakinna' (but). Then he explains that if the conclusion (the 'other statement') is not mentioned with its matter and form, the syllogism is iqtirani. He gives an example: 'Every body is composed, and every composed is originated, therefore every body is originated.' Here, the conclusion 'every body is originated' is not stated as a separate statement with its full form; it is distributed across the premises, hence it is iqtirani. The name iqtirani comes from the conjunction (iqtiran) of the terms within the syllogism. The commentators expand on these points: Al-Dassuqi clarifies that 'composed' means having a composition with harmony. Al-Attar notes that 'lakinna' is considered an exceptive particle by logicians because it has the meaning of 'illa' in disjunctive exception. He also points out a potential oversight in someone's earlier remark about excepting the opposite of the antecedent. The commentary further explains that 'body' is mentioned in the first premise and 'composed' in the second, so the conclusion's form is not explicitly present as a whole. The naming 'iqtirani' is due to the conjunction of the minor, major, and middle terms, or alternatively because it contains a conjunction particle (the 'waw' connecting premises). This lesson establishes the foundational classification of syllogisms in Aristotelian logic as adapted by Muslim logicians.

Distinguishing Istithna'i and Iqtirani Syllogisms
The author defines and distinguishes exceptional and conjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism is istithna'i or iqtirani based on the presence of the exception tool 'lākin'.

Core Idea
The exception tool 'lākin' determines whether a syllogism is istithna'i or iqtirani.

This passage introduces the foundational classification of syllogisms into two types: exceptional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani). The key distinguishing factor is the presence of a specific tool of exception, which in logical terminology is the word 'lākin' (but). Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines how the conclusion is presented and affects the logical analysis of arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tool of exception
Look for the word 'lākin' (but) in the syllogism. If present, the syllogism is istithna'i.
2
Check for explicit conclusion
In istithna'i, the conclusion is explicitly stated after 'lākin'. In iqtirani, the conclusion is not stated with the exception tool.
3
Apply to the example given
Examine the example: 'Every body is composed, every composed thing is originated, therefore every body is originated.' No 'lākin' appears, so it is iqtirani.
4
Understand the etymology
'Iqtirani' comes from 'iqtirān' meaning conjunction of the limits (terms) in the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ اسْتِثْنَائِيًّا لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى أَدَاةِ الاسْتِثْنَاءِ وَهِيَ لَكِنْ. وَإِلَّا فَاقْتِرَانِيٌّ، كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلُّ جِسْمٍ مُؤَلَّفٌ، وَكُلُّ مُؤَلَّفٍ مُحْدَثٌ، فَكُلُّ جِسْمٍ مُحْدَثٌ. فَالْقَوْلُ الْآخَرُ وَهُوَ كُلُّ جِسْمٍ مُحْدَثٌ لَيْسَ مَذْكُوراً فِي الْقِيَاسِ بِهَيْئَتِهِ، وَيُسَمَّى اقْتِرَانِيًّا لِاقْتِرَانِ الْحُدُودِ فِيهِ، وَسَتَعْرِفُ الْحُدُودَ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ.
Section: On Syllogism. It is called exceptional because it includes the tool of exception, which is 'but'. Otherwise, it is conjunctive, like our saying: Every body is composed, and every composed thing is originated, therefore every body is originated. The other statement, which is 'every body is originated', is not mentioned in the syllogism in its [complete] form, and it is called conjunctive because of the conjunction of the limits in it, and you will come to know the limits after that.
Source pages p. 781

Commentary
This passage defines the two main types of qiyas (syllogism): istithna'i (exceptional) and iqtirani (conjunctive). The author states that a syllogism is called istithna'i if it contains a tool of exception, which in logical terminology is the word 'lākin' (but). This is a technical usage specific to the logicians, not the grammarians. If the syllogism does not contain such a tool, it is iqtirani. An example is given: 'Every body is composed, every composed thing is originated, therefore every body is originated.' In this example, the conclusion 'every body is originated' is not stated explicitly with the tool of exception; rather, it is understood from the two premises. Therefore it is iqtirani. The term iqtirani comes from the fact that the limits (ḥudūd) of the syllogism—the terms—are conjoined (iqtirān) in the premises. The author promises to explain the limits later. A key point is that in the exceptional syllogism, the conclusion is explicitly asserted with 'but', whereas in the conjunctive it is implicit.

The Syllogism

Division of Iqtirani Syllogism and Definition of the Three Terms
Division of iqtirani and definition of the three terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conjunctive syllogism as categorical (hamli) or conditional (sharti) and identify the minor, major, and middle terms in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The three terms—minor, major, middle—are the building blocks of every categorical syllogism.

In this passage, the author subdivides the conjunctive syllogism into two types—categorical and conditional—and then introduces the three essential terms that constitute a categorical syllogism. Understanding these terms is crucial because they are the components you will manipulate in every syllogistic argument you encounter in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of iqtirani syllogism
The passage states that iqtirani is either hamli (categorical) if composed entirely of categorical propositions, or sharti (conditional) if it includes at least one conditional proposition. Notice the criterion is the composition of propositions.
Sharti itself can be further divided into muttasil (conditional) and munfasil (disjunctive), but that comes later.
2
Locate the three terms in a categorical syllogism
Any categorical syllogism must have exactly three terms: the subject of the conclusion (minor), its predicate (major), and a term repeated in both premises (middle). In the example 'body, composed, originated', identify which is which.
3
Distinguish the minor and major terms
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion and generally has fewer individuals than the predicate (major term). In the example, 'body' is the minor term because it is less general than 'originated'.
This 'fewer individuals' is a general rule, not absolute.
4
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the one repeated in both premises but absent from the conclusion. It connects the minor and major terms. In the example, 'composed' appears in both premises, so it is the middle term.
Without the middle term, the premises would not be linked, and no conclusion would follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الاقْتِرَانِيُّ: إِمَّا حَمْلِيٌّ إِنْ تَرَكَّبَ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ، أَوْ شَرْطِيٌّ إِنْ لَمْ يَتَرَكَّبْ مِنْهَا. وَلَمَّا فَرَغَ مِنْ تَعْرِيفِ الْقِيَاسِ وَتَقْسِيمِهِ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ؛ شَرَعَ فِي الْأَقْسَامِ، وَابْتَدَأَ بِالاقْتِرَانِيِّ الْمُرَكَّبِ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ، وَهُوَ يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى حُدُودٍ ثَلَاثَةٍ: مَوْضُوعِ الْمَطْلُوبِ وَمَحْمُولِهِ وَالْمُكَرَّرِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ. وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيِّ يُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَصْغَرَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي الْغَالِبِ أَقَلُّ أَفْرَاداً مِنَ الْمَحْمُولِ. وَبَيَانُهُ: أَنَّ كُلَّ قِيَاسٍ حَمْلِيٍّ لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ؛ إِحْدَاهُمَا تَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى مَوْضُوعِ الْمَطْلُوبِ كَالْجِسْمِ فِي الْمِثَالِ الْمَذْكُورِ، وَثَانِيتُهُمَا عَلَى مَحْمُولِهِ كَالْمُحْدَثِ، وَهُمَا يَشْتَرِكَانِ فِي حَدٍّ كَالْمُؤَلَّفِ، فَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ يُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَصْغَرَ، وَمَحْمُولُهُ أَكْبَرَ، وَالْمُشْتَرَكُ الْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَهُمَا يُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَوْسَطَ.
Then the conjunctive [syllogism]: either categorical if it is composed of categorical propositions, or conditional if it is not composed of them. And when he finished the definition of syllogism and its division into two parts, he proceeded to the subdivisions, and began with the conjunctive composed of categoricals, and it includes three limits: the subject of the desired conclusion, its predicate, and the repeated [term] between them in the two premises. And the subject of the desired conclusion in the categorical [syllogism] is called the minor term, because it is generally fewer in individuals than the predicate. And its explanation: every categorical syllogism necessarily has two premises; one of them contains the subject of the desired conclusion like 'body' in the mentioned example, and the second contains its predicate like 'originated', and they share a limit like 'composed'. So the subject of the desired conclusion is called the minor term, and its predicate is the major term, and the shared repeated term between them is called the middle term.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous classification by subdividing iqtirani (conjunctive) syllogisms into two types: hamli (categorical), composed entirely of categorical (subject-predicate) propositions, and sharti (conditional), which includes at least one conditional proposition. The author then focuses on categorical syllogisms and explains that they necessarily contain three terms (ḥudūd). The desired conclusion has a subject and a predicate; the subject is called the minor term (ḥadd aṣghar) because it usually has fewer individuals than the predicate. The predicate is the major term (ḥadd akbar). The term repeated in both premises—the common link—is the middle term (ḥadd awsaṭ). The commentator expands by noting that these terms are not exclusive to categorical syllogisms but apply to conditional ones as well. Using the example from earlier ('Every body is composed, every composed thing is originated, therefore every body is originated'), the minor term is 'body', the major term is 'originated', and the middle term is 'composed'. The middle term is essential because it connects the minor and major in the premises, allowing the deduction.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
beginner

The Three Terms of a Categorical Syllogism: Minor, Major, and Middle
Author defines minor, major, and middle terms with their rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism and explain why they are named as such.

Core Idea
The middle term is the logical hinge connecting the minor and major terms in a syllogism.

In any categorical syllogism, three terms work together to produce a necessary conclusion. Understanding which term is minor, major, and middle is the first step to analyzing syllogistic arguments. This lesson defines each term based on its role and extension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (natija)
Locate the conclusion of the syllogism. Its subject and predicate are the starting points for identifying the minor and major terms.
2
Locate the subject of the conclusion (minor term)
The subject of the conclusion is always the minor term. Check if it has fewer individuals compared to the predicate (usually).
The text says 'usually' because sometimes subject and predicate have equal extension.
3
Locate the predicate of the conclusion (major term)
The predicate of the conclusion is always the major term. It is usually broader in extension than the subject.
4
Find the term repeated in both premises (middle term)
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It connects the minor and major terms.
Without the middle term, the syllogism would be immediate or tautological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَابْتَدَأَ بِالاقْتِرَانِيِّ الْمُرَكَّبِ مِنَ الْحَمْلِيَّاتِ، وَهُوَ يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى حُدُودٍ ثَلاثَةٍ: مَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ وَمَحْمُولُهُ وَالْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، فَقَالَ: وَمَوْضُوعُ الْمَطْلُوبِ مِنَ الْحَمْلِي يُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَصْغَرَ؛ لأَنَّهُ فِي الْغَالِبِ أَقَلُّ أَفْرَادًا مِنَ الْمَحْمُولِ. وَمَحْمُوْلُهُ يُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَكْبَرَ؛ لأَنَّهُ فِي الْغَالِبِ أَكْثَرُ أَفْرَادًا مِنَ الْمُوْضُوْعِ. وَالْمُكَرَّرُ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي مُقَدِّمَتَيِ الْقِيَاسِ يُسَمَّى حَدًّا أَوْسَطَ.
And he began with the conjunctive syllogism composed of categoricals, and it includes three terms: the subject of the desired conclusion, its predicate, and the term repeated between them in the two premises. He said: The subject of the desired conclusion from the categorical syllogism is called the minor term, because it is usually fewer in individuals than the predicate. And its predicate is called the major term, because it is usually greater in individuals than the subject. And the term repeated between them in the two premises of the syllogism is called the middle term.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage defines the three terms (ḥudūd) of a categorical conjunctive syllogism. The author first states that the conjunctive syllogism composed of categorical propositions contains three terms: the subject of the desired conclusion (mawḍūʿ al-maṭlūb), its predicate (maḥmūluhu), and the repeated term (al-mukarrar) that occurs in both premises. Then he quotes the matn defining each: the subject of the conclusion is called the minor term (aṣghar) because it is usually fewer in individuals (i.e., has narrower extension) than the predicate. The predicate is called the major term (akbar) because it is usually broader in extension. The term that is repeated in the two premises (once in each) is called the middle term (awsaṭ). The word 'usually' warns that this extensional relationship may not always hold; sometimes subject and predicate may be equal in extension, as in 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' In such a case, the minor and major are equal, but the naming remains conventional. The middle term is essential for connecting the two premises to produce a necessary conclusion; without it, the syllogism would be merely tautological or immediate.

Key Terms
حد أصغر
حد أكبر
حد أوسط

C) Originated
D) Everything

Key Takeaway
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion, the major term is the predicate of the conclusion, and the middle term is the repeated link in the premises.

Major, Middle, and Minor Terms in a Categorical Syllogism
The author defines the three terms of a categorical syllogism and the minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major, middle, and minor terms in any categorical syllogism and distinguish the minor premise.

Core Idea
The three terms of a syllogism are defined by their roles in the conclusion and premises.

In this passage, the author defines the three essential components of a categorical syllogism: the major term, the middle term, and the minor premise. Understanding these roles is critical for analyzing and constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic. The passage also clarifies that the 'major' term is not always broader in extension, but only in most cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined in each sentence.
The passage defines three things: the predicate of the conclusion (major term), the repeated term (middle term), and the premise containing the minor term (minor premise). Notice that it does not define the minor term itself here.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the names.
The major term is called major because it usually has more individuals (broader extension) than the subject. The middle term is called middle because it is between the two premises. The minor premise is called minor because it contains the minor term.
3
Note the qualification 'fi al-ghalib' (mostly).
This shows that the definition is not absolute; sometimes the major term may not be broader. This nuance is important for precise reasoning.
4
Distinguish between terms and premises.
The passage defines two terms (major, middle) and one premise (minor premise). The minor term is implied but not defined in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ (وَمَحْمُوْلُهُ) يُسمَّى حدًّا أَكْبَرَ ) ؛ لأنَّه في الغالب أكثر أفراداً من الموضوع . (وَالْمُكَرَّرُ) بينهما في مقدّمتي القياس، يُسمَّى حدا (أَوْسَطَ)؛ (وَمَا ) ؛ أي: المقدّمة الَّتي (فِيهَا الأَصْغَرُ) تُسمَّى (الصُّغَرَى)؛ لأَنَّهَا ذات الأصغر وصاحِبَتُه
Section: On Syllogism. (And its predicate) is called the major term, because mostly it has more individuals than the subject. (And the repeated [term] between them) in the two premises of the syllogism is called the middle term; (and what) i.e., the premise which contains the minor term is called the minor premise, because it is the possessor of the minor and its companion.
Source pages p. 783

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition from a commentary on a logical matn. It begins a section on syllogism (qiyas). The author defines the three terms of a categorical syllogism. First, the predicate of the desired conclusion (which is called the major term) is defined as the term that is mostly more universal in extension than the subject (the minor term). This is why it is called 'major' (akbar). Second, the term that is repeated between the two premises is called the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). Third, the premise containing the minor term is called the minor premise (al-sughra), because it is the premise that takes the minor term as its subject. This definition sets the foundation for understanding the structure of a syllogism in Islamic logic. The commentator clarifies that the major term is not always broader; sometimes it is equal in extension, but in most cases it is broader, which justifies the name 'major'.
