Objection and Answer on the Repetition of the Middle Term
The author refutes the objection that the middle term does not repeat in the first and fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection against the repetition of the middle term in the first and fourth figures and explain Ibn Sina's response based on the distinction between essence and concept.

Core Idea
The middle term repeats in reference, not in concept, preserving syllogistic validity.

In the definition of the categorical syllogism, the middle term must be repeated in both premises. But what if the subject is taken as essence and the predicate as concept? An objector claims this undermines repetition in first and fourth figures. Ibn Sina responds by clarifying that repetition concerns the individuals referred to, not the conceptual meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Read the opening phrase 'فإن قيل' and note the objection: the middle term is not repeated in first and fourth figures because the subject is taken as essence (ذات) and the predicate as concept (مفهوم).
This distinction implies that 'triangle' and 'shape' are not the same in meaning.
2
Understand the essence-concept distinction
In 'Every triangle is a shape', the subject 'triangle' refers to the thing itself, while the predicate 'shape' is a concept attributed to it. They are different in intension but may have the same extension.
Extension = the set of individuals that fall under the term.
3
Locate Ibn Sina's response from al-Shifa
Find the phrase 'ما قاله الشيخ في الشفاء' and read the response: when we say 'Every triangle is a shape', it means that what is called a triangle is itself called a shape.
This shows the predicate applies to the same individuals as the subject.
4
Explain how the response shows repetition in reference
In the second premise, 'Every shape is such-and-such', the term 'shape' refers to the same individuals that were predicated of 'triangle'. Thus the middle term 'shape' is repeated in reference, not in concept.
Repetition in reference satisfies the condition of the middle term.
5
Conclude why the objection fails
The objector confuses conceptual identity with referential identity. The syllogism's validity only requires that the middle term applies to the same individuals in both premises, not that the concepts are identical.
This defense upholds the standard definition of the middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنْ قِيْلَ : الأوسط لا يتكرَّرُ في الأَوَّلِ والرَّابع ؛ لأنَّ المراد مِنَ الموضوعِ الذَّاتُ ، وَمِنَ المحمول المفهوم، والجوابُ مَا قالَهُ الشَّيخُ في «الشّفاء» : إذا قُلْنَا : كُلُّ مُثَلَّثٍ شكل ; فَمعناه أَنَّ مَا يُقالُ لهُ المثلَّثُ , فَهُوَ بِعِينِهِ يُقالُ لهُ الشَّكْلُ , وَإِذا كانَ المعنى : كُلُّ مثلث مقول وصادق عليهِ الشَّكْلُ , ثمَّ قُلْنَا : وَكُلُّ شكل كَذَا بمعنى : كُلَّ مَا يُقالُ ويصدقُ عليهِ الشَّكْلُ هُوَ كذا ; كانَ تكريراً للحدّ الأوسط.
For if it is said: The middle term is not repeated in the first and fourth [figures], because what is intended from the subject is the essence (dhat) and from the predicate is the concept (mafhum). The answer is what the Shaykh said in al-Shifa: When we say 'Every triangle is a shape', its meaning is that what is called a triangle is itself called a shape. And if the meaning is: 'Every triangle is predicated of and is true of shape', then we say: 'And every shape is such-and-such' meaning: 'Everything that is predicated of and is true of shape is such-and-such', then this is a repetition of the middle term.
Source pages p. 783

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (naqd wa jawab) from a commentary. The author addresses an objection against the definition of the middle term as 'the repeated term between the two premises'. The objector claims that in the first and fourth figures, the middle term is not actually repeated because the subject and predicate are understood differently: the subject is the essence (dhat) while the predicate is the concept (mafhum). For example, in 'Every triangle is a shape', 'triangle' is the essence and 'shape' is the concept; they are not identical. Therefore, the term 'shape' in the second premise would not be the same as in the first premise. The response, quoting Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) from al-Shifa, explains that the repetition is in meaning, not in wording. When we say 'Every triangle is a shape', we mean that anything that is called a triangle is also correctly called a shape. The predicate 'shape' refers to the same set of individuals as the subject 'triangle' (though the concepts differ). So in the subsequent premise 'Every shape is such-and-such', the term 'shape' is predicated of the same things that were the subject of the first premise, thus the middle term 'shape' is indeed repeated in meaning. This resolves the objection and upholds the standard definition.

Key Terms
الذات
المفهوم
التكرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The first and fourth figures do not require repetition.
D) The middle term must repeat in both wording and concept.

Key Takeaway
The middle term's repetition is ensured by reference to the same individuals, not by identical concepts.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Defining the Minor and Major Premises (Sughra and Kubra)
Definition of minor and major premises with commentary on their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor and major premises in any categorical syllogism and explain the exceptional ordering in conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
A premise is called 'minor' if it contains the conclusion's subject, and 'major' if it contains the conclusion's predicate.

Before applying the rules of syllogism, you must correctly label the two premises. This lesson explains how the minor premise (sughra) and major premise (kubra) are defined based on the terms they contain. We will also see a special case in conditional syllogisms where the naming is reversed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion's subject and predicate
The subject of the conclusion is the minor term, and the predicate is the major term. These determine the names of the premises.
2
Find the premise containing the minor term
Locate which premise includes the subject of the conclusion. That premise is the minor premise (sughra).
It is called 'minor' because it pertains to the lesser term.
3
Find the premise containing the major term
Locate which premise includes the predicate of the conclusion. That premise is the major premise (kubra).
It is called 'major' because it pertains to the greater term.
4
Check the conditional exception
In conditional syllogisms (al-istithna'i), the first premise is called kubra and the second sughra, opposite to categorical usage.
This reversal is consistent across the tradition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : تُسَمَّى شَكْلاً) تشبيهاً له بالهيئة الجسميَّةِ الحاصلة من إحاطة حَدٌ أو حدود بالمقدارِ ؛ فهوَ تَشبيه معقولٍ بِمَحسوس، قال المصنِّفُ : التَّحقيقُ أَنَّ القياس باعتبارِ إيجابٍ مُقدِّمَتَيْهِ المقترنتَيْنِ وَسَلْبِهِمَا وَكُلْيَتِهِما وَجُزئيَّتِهما يُسمَّى قرينةً وَضَرباً ، وباعتبار الهيئة الحاصلة لهُ مِن كَيفيَّةِ وَضْعِ الحد الأوسط عند الأصغر والأكبر من جهة كونه موضوعاً لَهُما أو محمولاً ، يُسمَّى شَكلاً ، فقد يَتَّحِدُ الشَّكلُ معَ اختلافِ الضَّرْبِ كَمَا في ضروبِ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، وقد يكون بالعكس كالموجبتَيْنِ الكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ مَثلاً مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ والثَّالِثِ أ . هـ
وس
(۱) (قَوْلُهُ : خلاف (المتن فيه أن المتن لم يتعرض للضرب هنا وتعرض للشكل وجعله اسماً لهيئة وضع الحدود الثلاثة على الوجه المخصوص كما تعرض لأسماء الحدود والمقدمات، وهو في غير هذا الكتاب من تسمية كلّ من الضرب أو الشكل قياساً باعتبار ما حققه لا ينافي شيء خاص. ا.هـ. الشّرنوبي.

(And what) meaning the premise that (contains the minor term) is called (the minor premise); because it is the possessor of the minor term and its companion. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'for its intermediacy... etc.') meaning because it is a means in establishing the predicate for the subject, even if it is not intermediate in the expression; so no objection arises that it does not intervene except in the first and fourth. Refer to Yasin. (His statement: 'And what contains the minor term... etc.') This applies to the categorical conjunctive syllogism; as for the conditional syllogism, the first premise is called major, and the second minor, as previously. (His statement: 'and its companion') is an explanatory conjunction to what precedes. al-Attar: Something that equals what equals ج and is a necessary condition for a necessary condition of ج and a part of a part of ج, and like our saying: Every ج is ب and every أ is not ب yields: Nothing of ج is أ by reductio. So we say: The conditions considered in the production of a syllogism are of two types: what is a condition for the actual production, like the conditions considered in the four figures, and what is a condition for knowledge of the production, like the conditions considered in conditional conjunctive syllogisms. Repetition of the middle term is not a condition for production but for knowledge of it; for the syllogism's rules are established and its rulings known only when the middle term is repeated. End. (His statement: 'for its intermediacy between the two extremes of the desired conclusion') means it is a means and instrument in linking one extreme to the other, or because it mediates between the two extremes in mention and intellection in the first figure, which is the noblest of figures. al-Isam said: Among rational inspirations is that it mediates between the major and minor in the minor and major premises; because in the first figure composed of two universal affirmatives, which is the noblest of moods, it is a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major in the universal affirmative, so it is mostly greater than the minor in the minor premise and smaller than the major in the major premise. (His statement: 'because it is the possessor of the minor') is a naming of the thing by a description of its part, and his conjunction 'and its companion' is an interpretation of 'possessor'. Chapter: The Syllogism (And) the premise that contains the major term is called (the major premise); because it is the possessor of the major. (And the form) resulting from the mode of placing the middle term with respect to the other two is called (the figure). al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'And the form resulting... etc.') The author said: The investigation is that the syllogism, with respect to the affirmation and negation of its two conjoined premises, their universality and particularity, is called mood (qarina and darb), and with respect to the form resulting from the mode of placing the middle term with respect to the minor and major in terms of being a subject or predicate for them, is called figure. So the figure may be the same while the mood differs, as in the moods of the first figure, and the opposite may occur, like the two universal affirmatives in the first and third figures. End. So he made darb and qarina a name for the syllogism by virtue of a specific aspect, just as he made figure also a name for the syllogism by virtue of a specific aspect. al-Attar: Contradiction of the text (1) (His statement: 'from the mode of placing... etc.') meaning from the aspect of the middle being a predicate in the minor and a subject in the major in the first figure, or a predicate in both in the second, or a subject in both in the third, or the inverse of the first in the fourth. (His statement: 'is called figure') as an analogy to the bodily form resulting from the enclosing of a boundary or boundaries around a magnitude; it is an analogy of the intelligible to the sensible. The author said: The investigation is that the syllogism, with respect to the affirmation of its two conjoined premises and their negation and their universality and their particularity, is called mood (qarina and darb), and with respect to the form resulting for it from the mode of placing the middle term with respect to the minor and major in terms of being a subject for them or a predicate, is called figure. So the figure may be the same while the mood differs, as in the moods of the first figure, and the opposite may occur, like the two universal affirmatives in the first and third figures. End. Wa-s-w-s (1) (His statement: 'contradiction of the text' because the text did not mention mood here and only discussed the figure and made it a name for the form of placing the three terms in a specific manner, just as it discussed the names of the terms and premises. This is in other books: naming each of the mood and figure as a syllogism by virtue of what he verified does not conflict with any specific aspect. End of al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 784

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn about the syllogism. It begins by defining the minor premise (sughra) as the premise containing the minor term, and explains through glosses that the middle term serves as a link between the subject and predicate. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that this definition applies to categorical conjunctive syllogisms, whereas in conditional syllogisms the first premise is called major and the second minor. The discussion then turns to the conditions for syllogistic production, distinguishing between conditions for actual production (like those in the four figures) and conditions for knowing that production (like those in conditional syllogisms). Repetition of the middle term is said to be a condition for knowledge, not for production itself. Next, the passage defines the major premise (kubra) as the premise containing the major term, and the figure (shakl) as the form resulting from how the middle term is placed relative to the minor and major terms. Al-Dasuqi quotes the author (al-Musannif) to explain the distinction between mood (darb) and figure: mood concerns the affirmation/negation and quantity of the premises, while figure concerns the position of the middle term as subject or predicate. Examples are given: the first figure has four moods but the same figure; the same pair of universal affirmative premises appears in the first and third figures. The commentator al-Attar then elaborates on the four figures by describing the position of the middle term in each. Finally, a marginal note points out a seeming contradiction with the matn (that the matn only defines figure and not mood), but the note resolves it by explaining that the matn's author elsewhere names both mood and figure as 'syllogism' from different aspects, which does not contradict the present treatment.

Checkpoint
In a categorical syllogism, which premise is called the major premise (kubra)?
A) The premise that contains the minor term.
B) The premise that contains the major term.
C) The premise that appears first.
D) The premise that contains the middle term.

Key Takeaway
The minor premise contains the conclusion's subject, the major premise contains its predicate; this holds for categorical syllogisms but is reversed in conditional ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the conclusion 'Therefore, every mubah (permissible) is not haram (forbidden),' and the premises 'Every halal is not haram' and 'Every mubah is halal,' identify the minor and major premises. Explain your reasoning.
?
Write your own categorical syllogism on an Islamic ruling (e.g., from fiqh al-ibadat) and label its sughra and kubra.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Figure of a Syllogism (Shakl) and its Distinction from Mood (Darb)
Definition of the figure and its distinction from mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of any syllogism by the position of the middle term, and distinguish figure from mood.

Core Idea
The figure (shakl) is defined by the position of the middle term, while mood (darb) depends on the quantity and quality of the premises.

Once you have identified the premises, you need to classify the syllogism's form. This lesson introduces the concept of 'figure' (shakl) as determined by how the middle term is placed relative to the other terms. We will also see how figure differs from mood (darb), which concerns the affirmative/negative and universal/particular nature of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term (hadd al-awsat)
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This term is the link between the premises.
2
Determine its position in each premise
In the minor premise, is the middle term a subject or a predicate? In the major premise, is it a subject or a predicate?
Use the conclusion to identify the minor and major terms first.
3
Classify the figure based on position
First figure: middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major. Second: predicate in both. Third: subject in both. Fourth: subject in the minor and predicate in the major.
This classification is standard throughout the syllogistic tradition.
4
Distinguish figure from mood
Mood (darb) depends on whether the premises are affirmative or negative, and universal or particular. Figure depends solely on term position. The same figure can have different moods, and the same mood can appear in different figures.
For example, the first figure has four moods, and the same pair of universal affirmative premises appears in the first and third figures.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn about the syllogism. It begins by defining the minor premise (sughra) as the premise containing the minor term, and explains through glosses that the middle term serves as a link between the subject and predicate. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that this definition applies to categorical conjunctive syllogisms, whereas in conditional syllogisms the first premise is called major and the second minor. The discussion then turns to the conditions for syllogistic production, distinguishing between conditions for actual production (like those in the four figures) and conditions for knowing that production (like those in conditional syllogisms). Repetition of the middle term is said to be a condition for knowledge, not for production itself. Next, the passage defines the major premise (kubra) as the premise containing the major term, and the figure (shakl) as the form resulting from how the middle term is placed relative to the minor and major terms. Al-Dasuqi quotes the author (al-Musannif) to explain the distinction between mood (darb) and figure: mood concerns the affirmation/negation and quantity of the premises, while figure concerns the position of the middle term as subject or predicate. Examples are given: the first figure has four moods but the same figure; the same pair of universal affirmative premises appears in the first and third figures. The commentator al-Attar then elaborates on the four figures by describing the position of the middle term in each. Finally, a marginal note points out a seeming contradiction with the matn (that the matn only defines figure and not mood), but the note resolves it by explaining that the matn's author elsewhere names both mood and figure as 'syllogism' from different aspects, which does not contradict the present treatment.

Checkpoint
In the first figure of a syllogism, what is the position of the middle term in the minor premise?
A) It is the subject.
B) It is the predicate.
C) It is both subject and predicate.
D) It does not appear.

Key Takeaway
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the position of the middle term in the premises, while mood depends on the quantity and quality of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'All intoxicants are haram. All khamr is intoxicating. Therefore, all khamr is haram.' Determine the figure and mood. Explain how you arrived at your answer.
?
Construct a categorical syllogism on a topic from Islamic jurisprudence (e.g., obligations in prayer) and identify its figure. Show the position of the middle term.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Figure and Mood: The Two Dimensions of a Syllogism
Definitions and distinction of figure from mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between shakl (figure) and darb (mood) in a categorical syllogism and identify each from the premises.

Core Idea
Shakl is where the middle term sits; darb is how the premises are stated.

The categorical syllogism has two independent but complementary aspects: the figure (shakl) concerns the position of the middle term relative to the minor and major terms, while the mood (darb/qarina) concerns the quantity and quality of the premises. This passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies both, and commentators refine the distinction with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Middle Term
In a syllogism like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden,' find the term repeated in both premises: 'intoxicant'. That is the middle term.
2
Determine the Figure by Middle Term Position
Check the position of the middle term in each premise: is it subject or predicate? The four figures correspond to specific arrangements. In the example, 'intoxicant' is predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor, so it is the first figure.
Shakl is defined by this configuration.
3
Determine the Mood by Premises’ Quantity and Quality
Note whether each premise is universal (kulli) or particular (juz’i), affirmative (ijabi) or negative (salbi). The combination gives the mood (darb). In the example, both premises are universal affirmative, so the mood is AAA.
4
Contrast Same Figure with Different Moods, and Same Mood with Different Figures
As explained in the commentary, the first figure has multiple moods (AAA, EAE, etc.), and the same mood (e.g., two universal affirmatives) can occur in different figures (first and third). This shows shakl and darb are independent.
This exercises the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: القِيَاسِ (وَ) الَّتي فيها الأكبرُ، تُسمَّى (الكُبرى)؛ لأنَّها ذاتُ الأكبر . (وَالهَيْئَةُ) الحاصلة من كيفية وضع الحدّ الأوسط عند الآخرين، تُسمَّى (شَكْلاً). الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْهَيْئَةُ الْحَاصِلَةُ . . . إِلَخ ) قال المصنِّفُ : التحقيق : أنَّ القياس باعتبار إيجاب مقدّمتيه المقترنتين وسليهما، وكلّيتهما، وجزئيتهما، يُسمى : قرينة وضرباً، وباعتبار الهيئة الحاصلة من كيفية وضع الحدّ الوسط عند الأصغر والأكبر من جهة كونِهِ مَوضوعاً لها أو محمولاً، يُسمَّى : شكلاً، فقد يتَّحدُ الشَّكلُ معَ اختلافِ الضَّرْبِ كَمَا في ضروبِ الشَّكل الأوَّلِ، وقد يكون بالعكس كالموجبتين الكليتين مثلاً مِنَ الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ والثَّالث. انتهى. فقد جعلَ الضَّرب والقرينة اسماً للقياس باعتبار شيءٍ خاص، كما جعل الشكل أيضاً اسماً للقياس باعتبارِ شيء خاص. العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : خلاف (المتن فيه أن المتن لم يتعرض للضرب هنا وتعرض للشكل وجعله اسماً لهيئة وضع الحدود الثلاثة على الوجه المخصوص كما تعرض لأسماء الحدود والمقدمات، وهو في غير هذا الكتاب من تسمية كلّ من الضرب أو الشكل قياساً باعتبار ما حققه لا ينافي شيء خاص. ا.هـ. الشّرنوبي.
Section: The Syllogism. (And) the one which contains the major term is called the major premise, because it possesses the major term. (And the configuration) resulting from the manner of placing the middle term relative to the other two is called (form). Dusuqi: (His statement: “And the configuration resulting … etc.”) The author said: The verification is that the syllogism is called qarina and darb by consideration of the affirmation of its two conjunct premises and their negation, their universality and particularity, and it is called shakl by consideration of the configuration resulting from the manner of placing the middle term relative to the minor and major terms in terms of its being a subject or predicate for them. Thus the form may be united while the darb differs, as in the moods of the first form, and it may be the reverse, as for the two universal affirmatives, for example, from the first and third forms. End. So he made darb and qarina a name for the syllogism by consideration of a specific aspect, just as he made shakl also a name for the syllogism by consideration of a specific aspect. Attar: (His statement: “Contrary to the matn”) The matn does not address darb here; it addresses shakl and makes it a name for the configuration of placing the three terms in the specific manner, just as it addressed the names of the terms and premises. And this is in other books of this author that naming each of darb or shakl as a syllogism by consideration of what he verified does not contradict anything specific. End. Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 785

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the terminology used for categorical syllogisms. The matn defines the major premise (kubra) as the premise containing the major term (al-akbar), and defines the shakl (figure) as the configuration resulting from the placement of the middle term relative to the other two terms. The commentary by Dusuqi explains that the same syllogism is named qarina or darb (mood) when considered from the perspective of the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises, and is named shakl when considered from the perspective of how the middle term is placed as subject or predicate relative to the minor and major terms. He gives examples: the moods of the first figure differ in darb but share the same shakl; conversely, two universal affirmative premises can occur in the first and third figures, differing in shakl but sharing the same darb. The marginal note by Attar clarifies that the matn here only discusses shakl, not darb, and this does not contradict other works where both aspects are considered. Thus the passage establishes a precise technical vocabulary for analyzing categorical syllogisms.

الصغرى
شكل

C) Darb depends on position of the middle term.
D) The same shakl can have different darb.

Key Takeaway
The figure (shakl) and mood (darb) are two distinct and independent aspects of a categorical syllogism.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures of the Categorical Syllogism
Author enumerates the four figures with examples and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of the categorical syllogism and determine the figure of any given syllogism by analyzing the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The figure of a syllogism is determined solely by the position of the middle term in the premises.

In this lesson, we examine how classical logicians classified categorical syllogisms into four figures based on the arrangement of the middle term. Understanding these figures is key to evaluating syllogistic validity and to seeing why some syllogisms produce certain conclusions. We will work through the author's definitions and examples, and see how the commentators clarify the properties of each figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term (al-awsat)
In any categorical syllogism, there are three terms: the minor term (subject of conclusion), the major term (predicate of conclusion), and the middle term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Locate the middle term in the given syllogism.
In the author's examples, the middle term is 'composed' (mu'allaf) in the first figure, 'animal' (hayawan) in the second, 'human' (insan) in the third and fourth.
2
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
Check whether the middle term is the subject or predicate in the minor premise (the premise containing the minor term) and in the major premise (the premise containing the major term). There are four possible combinations.
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion; the major term is the predicate of the conclusion.
3
Match the combination to the figure
Use the author's classification: (1) First figure: middle term is predicate of minor and subject of major. (2) Second figure: predicate of both. (3) Third figure: subject of both. (4) Fourth figure: subject of minor and predicate of major.
4
Test the figure with an Islamic science example
Apply the rule to a real syllogism, such as: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Here the middle term is 'intoxicant'. It is predicate of the minor ('every nabidh is an intoxicant') and subject of the major ('every intoxicant is forbidden'), so this is a first figure syllogism.
This also shows that the first figure can produce a universal affirmative conclusion.
5
Recognize special properties noted in the commentary
The commentator Dusuqi notes that the first figure is 'self-evident' (badihi) and that other figures reduce to it. He also explains that the third figure always yields a particular conclusion because the minor predicate (e.g., 'animal') is more general than the minor subject ('human'), so a universal conclusion would be false.
Understanding these properties helps in evaluating the validity and force of syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[تقسيم الحملي إلى: الأشكال الأربعة] (و) هي منحصرةٌ في أربعةٍ ; إذ الأَوْسَطُ إِمَّا مَحْمُولُ الصُّغَرَى موضُوعُ الكُبْرَى، وهو الشَّكل الأوَّل)، كقولنا : كلُّ جسم مؤلَّفٌ، وكلُّ مؤلَّفٍ مُحدَثٌ، فكلُّ جسم مُحدَثٌ» أَوْ مَحْمُولُهُمَا ؛ أي : محمولُ الصغرى والكبرى، (فَالثَّانِي)؛ أي: فالشكل الثَّاني، كقولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الجمادِ بحيوان، فلا شيء من الإنسان بجماد . أَوْ مَوضُوعُهُمَا، فَالثَّالِثُ)؛ أي: فالشَّكلُ الثَّالثُ، كقولنا : كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان، وكلُّ إنسانٍ ناطق، فبعض الحيوانِ ناطق. أَوْ عَكْسُ الأَوَّلِ بأن يكونَ الأوسط موضوع الصُّغرى محمول الكبرى، (فَالرَّابِعُ)؛ أي: فالشكل الرَّابعُ ، كقولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيو حيوان، وكلّ ناطق إنسان، فبعض الحيوان ناطق الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: مُنْحَصِرَةٌ فِي أَرْبَعَةٍ أي : من كون الحد الأوسط إِمَّا مَحمولُ الصُّغرى موضوع الكبرى، وإما موضوع فيهما ، وإمَّا محمول فيهما، وإمَّا خلافُ الأوّل؛ أي : عكسه . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ الشَّكْلُ الْأَوَّلُ يُسمَّى أَوَّلاً ، لأنَّ إنتاجه بديهي، وإنتاج البواقي نظري ترجع إليه، فيكونُ أسبق وأقدم في العلم . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ فيه : أنَّ المقدِّمتين كلِّيَّتان، فكيف أتى بالنتيجة جزئيَّةٌ؟ وأُجِيبُ : بأنَّ الشَّكلَ الثَّالث دائماً ينتج جزئيّة ، وَلَا ينتج كلية أصلاً ؛ لجواز أعمِّيَّةِ مَحمول الصُّغرى، فيمتنع الحكمُ بِمحمولِ الكُبرى على أفرادِ الصُّغرى كما في هذا المثال، وأنَّه يمتنعُ أن يُقال : كلُّ حيوان ناطق. العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : إِذِ الْأَوْسَطُ تَعليلٌ لِلانحصارِ في الأربعةِ.
[Division of the Categorical into the Four Figures] And they are confined to four; because the middle term is either the predicate of the minor and subject of the major — that is the first figure, as in our statement: Every body is composed, and every composed is originated, so every body is originated. Or predicate of both, i.e., predicate of the minor and major, so the second, i.e., the second figure, as in our statement: Every human is an animal, and nothing from inanimate matters is an animal, so nothing from humans is inanimate. Or subject of both, so the third, i.e., the third figure, as in our statement: Every human is an animal, and every human is rational, so some animal is rational. Or the reverse of the first, such that the middle term is subject of the minor and predicate of the major, so the fourth, i.e., the fourth figure, as in our statement: Every human is an animal, and every rational is a human, so some animal is rational. Dusuqi: (His statement: “confined to four” means that the middle term is either predicate of the minor and subject of the major, or subject in both, or predicate in both, or other than the first, i.e., its reverse. (His statement: “and it is the first figure” It is called first because its production is self-evident, and the production of the others is theoretical returning to it, so it is prior and older in knowledge. (His statement: “so some animal is rational” contains that the premises are universal, so how does he come with a particular conclusion? And it is answered: That the third figure always produces a particular and never produces a universal at all, because of the possibility of the predicate of the minor being more general, so it is impossible to judge the predicate of the major on all individuals of the minor, as in this example, and that it is impossible to say: Every animal is rational. Attar: (His statement: “because the middle term” is a cause for the confinement to four.)
Source pages p. 786

Commentary
This passage classifies the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term. The matn states that the figures are limited to four possibilities: (1) middle term is predicate of the minor premise and subject of the major premise (first figure); (2) predicate of both premises (second figure); (3) subject of both premises (third figure); (4) the reverse of the first, i.e., subject of the minor and predicate of the major (fourth figure). Each is illustrated with a logical example from the physical world (bodies, creation) and from human nature. The commentary by Dusuqi explains that the first figure is called 'first' because its conclusion is immediate and the other figures' validity is reduced to it. He also addresses a possible confusion: in the third figure example, both premises are universal but the conclusion is particular. The reason given is that the third figure always yields a particular conclusion because the minor predicate (e.g., 'animal') is wider in extension than the minor subject ('human'), so a universal conclusion would be false. Attar's note indicates that the confinement to four figures is justified by the three possible positions of the middle term (subject, predicate, or both) plus the reverse of the first. Thus the passage provides a complete, rule-based enumeration of syllogistic figures.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث
الشكل الرابع
الأوسط

Checkpoint

A) Because the premises are particular.
B) Because the middle term is predicate of both premises.
C) Because the predicate of the minor premise may be more general than the subject of the conclusion.
D) Because the conclusion must be negative.

Key Takeaway
The four figures of the categorical syllogism are distinguished by the position of the middle term: first (predicate of minor, subject of major), second (predicate of both), third (subject of both), and fourth (subject of minor, predicate of major), with the third figure always yielding a particular conclusion.

The Syllogism

The Circularity Objection to the First Figure and Its Defense
Al-Safawi's circularity objection and the ijmal/tafsil response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the circularity objection against the first figure and evaluate the validity of the response using the ijmal/tafsil distinction.

Core Idea
The first figure's major premise is general; the conclusion is specific — no circularity.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is considered the most natural and certain, but it faces a serious epistemological objection: does the major premise presuppose the conclusion? In this passage, al-Safawi raises this objection, and the answer introduces a key distinction between general (ijmal) and specific (tafsil) knowledge. Understanding this resolution is crucial for anyone using syllogistic reasoning in Islamic sciences, where the validity of arguments for theological and legal rulings depends on avoiding circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism structure at stake
In the first figure, the minor term (e.g., 'nabidh') is a subset of the middle term (e.g., 'intoxicant'). The major premise states: all middle terms have a property (e.g., 'forbidden'). The conclusion states: the minor term has that property. Recognize that the minor is one individual of the middle.
Example: Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.
2
Formulate al-Safawi's circularity objection
The objection: to know the major premise (all intoxicants are forbidden), one must already know that the specific minor (nabidh) is forbidden, because nabidh is an intoxicant. But that is exactly the conclusion. Thus, the syllogism would be circular — the conclusion is presupposed by the major premise.
The objection targets the epistemological priority: major → conclusion vs. conclusion → major.
3
Understand the defense: ijmal vs tafsil
The defense distinguishes: the major premise is known in a general, summary manner (ijmal) — e.g., 'all intoxicants are forbidden' is known by a general proof (like a revealed text or necessity), not by checking each individual. The conclusion, however, applies that general knowledge to a specific individual (tafsil). Thus, knowledge of the major does not require prior knowledge of the specific conclusion.
The defense does not require enumerative induction; universal premises can be known by necessity or deduction from first principles.
4
Apply the defense to a Kalam example
Consider the theological proof: 'Every temporally originated thing requires a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world requires a cause.' The objection would say: to know the major, you must already know the world requires a cause. But the defense says: the major is known by a general rational principle (temporality implies need of a cause), not by examining the world specifically.
This preserves the validity of the ontological argument without circularity.

Source Text

العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الطَّبْعِ أي : إلى قبولِ الطَّبع وتوجه النفس بالنسبة إلى البواقي، أو إلى النَّظم الطَّبيعي، وهو الانتقالُ مِنَ الأصغرِ إلى الأوسط ومنه إلى الأكبر، فَلَا يتغيَّرُ الأصغر والأكبرُ عَن حالهما في النتيجة، وَهَذا النَّظْمُ إِنَّما هو في الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، فَلِهذا وضعَ في المرتبة الأولى، قالَ السَّيِّدُ عيسى الصفوي : وفي هذا الشَّكل إِشْكالُ لا بُدَّ مِن فَهْمِهِ وَحَلِّهِ؛ وَهُوَ أَنَّ الأصغر إذا كان من أفرادِ الأوسط ; فالحاكم بأنَّ جميع الأوسط كذا وهو الكُبرى ; إنَّما يُعلَمُ إِذا عُلِمَ أَنَّ أفراد الأصغرِ كذا، وَهُوَ بِعَيْنِهِ النَّتيجةُ، فَالعِلْمُ بالكُبرى يتوقَّفُ على العِلْم بالنتيجةِ، فَلو كَانَتِ النَّتيجةُ مُستفادةً مِنَ القياسِ ; كان الشَّيءُ معلوماً قبلَ العِلْمِ بِهِ وَأَنَّهُ محالٌ، فَلَا يُمكنُ الاستدلال بِهِ والكسب بطريقِ الشَّكلِ الأوَّلِ، والجواب : أَنَّ النَّتيجةَ هِيَ الحكم على الأصغر بخصوصه؛ أي: حينَ مُلاحظتِهِ مُفصَّلاً ، والكُبرى حكم على أفرادِ الأوسط مُجملاً ، وَلَا نسلّمُ أنَّ العِلْمَ بِكُلِّ أوسط كَذَا يتوقَّفُ على العِلْم بحالِ كُلِّ مِن أفرادِهِ بخصوصه، بل يجوز أن يعلم الكُلِّيَّةَ بضرورة أو دليل، وَلَوْ لُوحِظَ ذاتُ فردٍ بخصوصِهِ ; لم يُعْلَم حالُه ; لأنَّه لم يلاحظ بخصوصِهِ أَنَّهُ فَردُ ذلكَ الكُلِّيَّةِ، فَالعِلْمُ بالكُبرى يتوجّه على مُلاحظة الأفرادِ بوجه عام ; أي على سبيل الإجمال، والعلم بالنتيجة هو معرفة حال الفردِ بخصوصِهِ فَلَا استحالة في استفادتِهِ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ ا.هـ. وهذا الجواب مع قلاقيه; مضطربٌ ، وقَدْ ذَكَرَ في شرح المطالع السُّؤال والجواب بأوجز من هذا وأوضح منه; فقالَ : لَا يُقالُ : الاستدلال بهذا الشكل دوري فاسد، أنْ يكونَ بَيِّنَاً ; لأنَّ العِلْمَ بِالنَّتيجة موقوف على العِلْمِ بالكُبرى الكُلِّيَّةِ، عن والعلم بها إِنَّما يحصلُ لو عُلِمَ ثبوتُ الحكم بالأكبرِ لِكُلِّ واحدٍ مِن أفرادِ الأوسط الَّتي مِن جُملَتِهَا الأصغر، فيكونُ العِلْمُ بالكُبرى الكُلِّيَّةِ موقوفاً على العِلْمِ بِثبوتِ فضلاً
Chapter on Syllogism. And these figures are placed in this order only because the first figure is intuitive in production, closer to nature than the other figures, so for this reason it was placed first. Al-Dassuqi: His saying: 'closer to nature' is an explanation of his saying 'intuitive'. Al-Attar: His saying: 'closer to nature' means: to the acceptance of nature and the turning of the soul with respect to the others, or to the natural order, which is the transition from the minor to the middle and from it to the major, so the minor and major do not change from their state in the conclusion, and this order is only in the first figure, so for this it was placed in the first rank. Al-Sayyid Isa al-Safawi said: In this figure there is an objection that must be understood and resolved; it is: the minor term, if it is among the individuals of the middle term, then the judgment that all the middle term is such, which is the major premise, is only known if it is known that the individuals of the minor term are such, and that is exactly the conclusion. So knowledge of the major premise depends on knowledge of the conclusion. If the conclusion were derived from the syllogism, then the thing would be known before knowledge of it, and that is impossible, so it is not possible to reason with it and gain knowledge through the first figure. And the answer: The conclusion is the judgment on the minor term specifically, i.e., when observing it in detail, and the major premise is a judgment on the individuals of the middle term in general. And we do not concede that knowledge of every middle term being such depends on knowledge of the state of each of its individuals specifically. Rather, it is possible to know the universal by necessity or by proof. And if an individual itself is observed specifically, its state is not known because it was not observed specifically that it is an individual of that universal. So knowledge of the major premise is directed towards observing individuals in a general way, i.e., in an overall manner, and knowledge of the conclusion is knowing the state of the individual specifically, so there is no impossibility in deriving it from the first [i.e., the major]. End. And this answer, in my view, is confused. And he mentioned in Sharh al-Mutala' the question and answer more concisely and clearly; he said: It cannot be said that reasoning with this figure is a vicious circle that is obvious because knowledge of the conclusion depends on knowledge of the universal major premise, and knowledge of it only occurs if the establishment of the judgment by the major for every individual of the middle term, among which is the minor, is known, so knowledge of the universal major premise depends on knowledge of the establishment of... (text cuts off at 'fadlan'?)
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage begins with a chapter heading on syllogism, then discusses the ordering of the figures. The first figure is placed first because its production is intuitive and closer to nature. Al-Attar explains 'closer to nature' as meaning the natural order of reasoning: from the minor to the middle to the major, preserving the positions of the minor and major in the conclusion. Then al-Safawi raises a serious objection to the first figure: the major premise (all middle terms are such) seems to require that we already know that the minor term (which is an individual of the middle) has that property, which is exactly the conclusion. This would make the reasoning circular. The answer distinguishes between the major premise as a general universal judgment about all individuals of the middle term, which can be known by necessity or proof without knowing each individual in detail, and the conclusion as a specific judgment about a particular individual. Thus, there is no circularity because the universal major is known in a general way, and the conclusion applies it specifically. Dassuqi, however, finds this answer confused and points out that Sharh al-Mutala' provides a clearer version. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating a break.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's major premise is a universal known in general (ijmal), while the conclusion is a specific application (tafsil); knowledge of the universal does not require prior knowledge of the specific, thus avoiding circularity.

Dassuqi's Refinement of the Circularity Defense
Dassuqi's critique of the defense and the Sharh al-Mutala' alternative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Dassuqi's criticism of the initial defense and compare it with the clearer version in Sharh al-Mutala'.

Core Idea
Dassuqi finds the ijmal/tafsil defense confused; Sharh al-Mutala' offers a clearer formulation.

After the initial defense of the first figure against the circularity objection, Dassuqi — the commentator on the text — steps in to critique that defense as 'confused' and points to a more concise and clearer treatment in Sharh al-Mutala'. This lesson examines how Islamic logicians refined their arguments over time, highlighting the importance of clarity in logical exposition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the initial defense (ijmal/tafsil)
Al-Safawi's answer distinguished between the major premise as a general (ijmali) judgment and the conclusion as a specific (tafsili) judgment, claiming that universal knowledge can be obtained by necessity or proof without checking each individual.
This defense was presented in the previous lesson.
2
Understand Dassuqi's critique: 'this answer is confused'
Dassuqi labels the answer as 'mudtarib' (confused) and 'ma'a qalaqih' (with restlessness). He does not fully explain why, but implies it lacks clarity and precision. His brevity suggests he expects the reader to see the confusion.
Possible confusion: The defense might conflate knowledge of the universal with knowledge of its application, or it might not clearly separate epistemological levels.
3
Identify what Sharh al-Mutala' does differently
Dassuqi says Sharh al-Mutala' presents the same question and answer 'more concisely and clearly'. The passage from Sharh al-Mutala' (included partially) restates the objection: knowledge of the conclusion depends on knowledge of the universal major premise, and knowledge of the universal major premise depends on knowledge of the conclusion for each individual. The phrasing seems to avoid the ambiguity of the earlier defense.
The clearer version likely emphasizes that the universal major is known through a separate proof, not through enumeration.
4
Evaluate the refinement: why clarity matters in logical arguments
In Islamic sciences, especially in theological debates, clarity in distinguishing premises from conclusions prevents misunderstandings and ensures that arguments are accepted. Dassuqi's critique shows that even a logically correct defense can be flawed in presentation, inviting further refinement.
This reflects the rigorous self-correcting nature of Islamic logic.

(قَوْلُهُ : أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الطَّبْعِ أي : إلى قبولِ الطَّبع وتوجه النفس بالنسبة إلى البواقي، أو إلى النَّظم الطَّبيعي، وهو الانتقالُ مِنَ الأصغرِ إلى الأوسط ومنه إلى الأكبر، فَلَا يتغيَّرُ الأصغر والأكبرُ عَن حالهما في النتيجة، وَهَذا النَّظْمُ إِنَّما هو في الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، فَلِهذا وضعَ في المرتبة الأولى، قالَ السَّيِّدُ عيسى الصفوي : وفي هذا الشَّكل إِشْكالُ لا بُدَّ مِن فَهْمِهِ وَحَلِّهِ؛ وَهُوَ أَنَّ الأصغر إذا كان من أفرادِ الأوسط ; فالحاكم بأنَّ جميع الأوسط كذا وهو الكُبرى ; إنَّما يُعلَمُ إِذا عُلِمَ أَنَّ أفراد الأصغرِ كذا، وَهُوَ بِعَيْنِهِ النَّتيجةُ، فَالعِلْمُ بالكُبرى يتوقَّفُ على العِلْم بالنتيجةِ، فَلو كَانَتِ النَّتيجةُ مُستفادةً مِنَ القياسِ ; كان الشَّيءُ معلوماً قبلَ العِلْمِ بِهِ وَأَنَّهُ محالٌ، فَلَا يُمكنُ الاستدلال بِهِ والكسب بطريقِ الشَّكلِ الأوَّلِ، والجواب : أَنَّ النَّتيجةَ هِيَ الحكم على الأصغر بخصوصه؛ أي: حينَ مُلاحظتِهِ مُفصَّلاً ، والكُبرى حكم على أفرادِ الأوسط مُجملاً ، وَلَا نسلّمُ أنَّ العِلْمَ بِكُلِّ أوسط كَذَا يتوقَّفُ على العِلْم بحالِ كُلِّ مِن أفرادِهِ بخصوصه، بل يجوز أن يعلم الكُلِّيَّةَ بضرورة أو دليل، وَلَوْ لُوحِظَ ذاتُ فردٍ بخصوصِهِ ; لم يُعْلَم حالُه ; لأنَّه لم يلاحظ بخصوصِهِ أَنَّهُ فَردُ ذلكَ الكُلِّيَّةِ، فَالعِلْمُ بالكُبرى يتوجّه على مُلاحظة الأفرادِ بوجه عام ; أي على سبيل الإجمال، والعلم بالنتيجة هو معرفة حال الفردِ بخصوصِهِ فَلَا استحالة في استفادتِهِ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ ا.هـ. وهذا الجواب مع قلاقيه; مضطربٌ ، وقَدْ ذَكَرَ في شرح المطالع السُّؤال والجواب بأوجز من هذا وأوضح منه; فقالَ : لَا يُقالُ : الاستدلال بهذا الشكل دوري فاسد، أنْ يكونَ بَيِّنَاً ; لأنَّ العِلْمَ بِالنَّتيجة موقوف على العِلْمِ بالكُبرى الكُلِّيَّةِ، عن والعلم بها إِنَّما يحصلُ لو عُلِمَ ثبوتُ الحكم بالأكبرِ لِكُلِّ واحدٍ مِن أفرادِ الأوسط الَّتي مِن جُملَتِهَا الأصغر، فيكونُ العِلْمُ بالكُبرى الكُلِّيَّةِ موقوفاً على العِلْمِ بِثبوتِ فضلاً

Module 14 · Lesson 70

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Resolving the Circularity Objection in the First Figure
Author presents and resolves a circularity objection against the first figure of syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity objection against the first figure of syllogism and explain how the distinction between universal and particular judgments resolves it.

Core Idea
Universal knowledge does not require detailed inspection of each individual; that saves the first figure from circularity.

This passage from the Tadhheeb explains why the first figure is considered self-evident and placed first, but then presents a serious objection: the first figure seems to require circular reasoning. Al-Sayyid 'Isa al-Safawi raises this problem, and a careful distinction between universal and particular knowledge provides the solution. This lesson walks you through the objection and the logical move that resolves it, a crucial tool for defending syllogistic proofs in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate where al-Safawi says the first figure leads to a vicious circle. The objection is: if the minor is an individual of the middle, then knowing the major premise (all middle is such) requires knowing that every individual of the middle is such, including the minor, which is exactly the conclusion; thus the argument is circular.
The objection targets the epistemology of the major premise, not the formal validity.
2
Understand the dependency claim
The objection claims that knowledge of the major premise depends on knowledge of the conclusion. If that were true, the syllogism would be useless because it would presuppose what it tries to prove.
3
Distinguish universal and particular judgment
The answer from Sharh al-Mutala' argues that the major premise is a universal judgment (e.g., 'every middle is such') known collectively, either by necessity or by some proof. The conclusion is a particular judgment about the minor specifically. The universal can be known without inspecting each individual separately.
Key insight: knowing 'all middle is such' does not require knowing that 'this individual middle is such' before deriving it; the universal stands on its own evidence.
4
See how this avoids circularity
Since the universal major premise is known independently (e.g., by rational intuition or a separate proof), there is no circularity. The conclusion follows as a new particular application derived from the universal.
The resolution preserves the epistemic value of first-figure syllogisms.

(قَوْلُهُ : أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الطَّبْعِ) تفسير لقوله : (بديهي).
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الطَّبْعِ أي : إلى قبولِ الطَّبع وتوجه النفس بالنسبة إلى البواقي، أو إلى النَّظم الطَّبيعي، وهو الانتقالُ مِنَ الأصغرِ إلى الأوسط ومنه إلى الأكبر، فَلَا يتغيَّرُ الأصغر والأكبرُ عَن حالهما في النتيجة، وَهَذا النَّظْمُ إِنَّما هو في الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ، فَلِهذا وضعَ في المرتبة الأولى، قالَ السَّيِّدُ عيسى الصفوي : وفي هذا الشَّكل إِشْكالُ لا بُدَّ مِن فَهْمِهِ الأوَّلِ، والجواب : أَنَّ النَّتيجةَ هِيَ الحكم على الأصغر بخصوصه؛ أي: حينَ مُلاحظتِهِ مُفصَّلاً ، والكُبرى حكم على أفرادِ الأوسط مُجملاً ، وَلَا نسلّمُ أنَّ العِلْمَ بِكُلِّ أوسط كَذَا يتوقَّفُ على العِلْم بحالِ كُلِّ مِن أفرادِهِ بخصوصه، بل يجوز أن يعلم الكُلِّيَّةَ بضرورة أو دليل، وَلَوْ لُوحِظَ ذاتُ فردٍ بخصوصِهِ ؛ لم يُعْلَم حالُه ؛ لأنَّه لم يلاحظ بخصوصِهِ أَنَّهُ فَردُ ذلكَ الكُلِّيَّةِ، فَالعِلْمُ بالكُبرى يتوجّه على مُلاحظة الأفرادِ بوجه عام ؛ أي على سبيل الإجمال، والعلم بالنتيجة هو معرفة حال الفردِ بخصوصِهِ فَلَا استحالة في استفادتِهِ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ ا.هـ. وهذا الجواب مع قلاقيه؛ مضطربٌ ، وقَدْ ذَكَرَ في شرح المطالع السُّؤال والجواب بأوجز من هذا وأوضح منه ؛ فقالَ : لَا يُقالُ : الاستدلال بهذا الشكل دوري فاسد، أنْ يكونَ بَيِّنَاً ؛ لأنَّ العِلْمَ بِالنَّتيجة موقوف على العِلْمِ بالكُبرى الكُلِّيَّةِ، عن والعلم بها إِنَّما يحصلُ لو عُلِمَ ثبوتُ الحكم بالأكبرِ لِكُلِّ واحدٍ مِن أفرادِ الأوسط الَّتي مِن جُملَتِهَا الأصغر ، فيكونُ العِلْمُ بالكُبرى الكُلِّيَّةِ موقوفاً على العِلْمِ بِثبوتِ الأكبرِ أَوْ بِسَلَبِهِ لِلأصغرِ الَّذي هو عينُ النَّتيجةِ، فَلو استفَدْنَا العلم بالنتيجة مِنَ العِلْم بالكُبرى ؛ لزمَ الدَّورُ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : الحُكْمُ يختلفُ بحسب اختلافِ أوصاف الموضوع حتَّى يكون معلوماً بحسب وَصْفٍ؛ مجهولاً بحسب وصف آخر، فَيُستفاد العلم بالحكم باعتبار وصفٍ من العلم به باعتبارِ وصفٍ آخر، وَلَا استحالة في ذلك.
(قَوْلُهُ : هُوَ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الْمَحْمُوْلِ) لأنَّ المحمول إِنَّما يطلبُ لأجل الموضوع إيجاباً وَسَلباً، وَكُلُّ ما يكون بحيثُ يُطلب أمرُ آخَرُ لأجلِهِ؛ يكون ذلكَ الشَّيء أشرفَ من ذلكَ الأمر.
(قَوْلُهُ : فِي أَخَسٌ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ وَهِيَ الْكُبْرَى لأنَّ الحدَّ الأوسط موضوع أيضاً في الشكل الثَّالِثِ، فَلِذَا وُضِعَ في المرتبة الثالثة، وإنَّما كانت هذه المقدّمةُ أَخسَّ ؛ لِعَدَمِ اشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب، بل تشتمل على محموله الَّذي هو أخشُ مِن مَوضوعِهِ.
و
(قَوْلُهُ : أَصْلَاً) بَلْ لِمخالفتِهِ إِيَّاهُ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الرابعة، وهذا هو أحد الوجوه المذكورة في وضع الأشكال على الترتيب المذكور، وهناك أوجه أُخرَى؛ منها : أنَّ الشَّكل الأوَّلَ لَمَّا كانَ مُنتجاً للمطالب الأربعةِ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الأُولَى، والثَّانِي لَمَّا كانَ مُنتجاً لِلسَّلْبِ الكُلّيّ الَّذي هو أشرفُ مِنَ الإيجاب الجزئي لِكَونِهِ أضبط وأنفع؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبةِ الثَّانيةِ والثَّالثُ لَمَّا كَانَ مُنتجاً للإيجابِ الجزئي ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الثالثة، والرَّابِعُ لَمَّا كان بعيداً عن الطَّبْعِ جدا؛ وضعَ في المرتبة
Chapter: On Qiyas (Syllogism). And these figures are arranged in this order: 1. Because the first figure is self-evident in production, closer to nature than the other figures, so it was placed first. Al-Dasuqi: 'His saying: closer to nature' is an explanation of his saying 'self-evident'. Al-Attar: 'His saying: closer to nature' i.e. to the acceptance of nature and the direction of the soul relative to the rest, or to the natural order, which is the transition from the minor to the middle and from it to the major, so the minor and major do not change from their state in the conclusion, and this order is only in the first figure, so it was placed in the first rank. Al-Sayyid 'Isa al-Safawi said: In this figure there is an objection that must be understood and resolved: that the minor if it is among the individuals of the middle, then the judgment that all the middle is such, which is the major, is only known if it is known that the individuals of the minor are such, and that is exactly the conclusion. So knowledge of the major depends on knowledge of the conclusion. If the conclusion were derived from the syllogism, then the thing would be known before knowledge of it, which is impossible. So inference by it and acquisition via the first figure are impossible. The answer: The conclusion is the judgment on the minor specifically, i.e., when observing it in detail, and the major is a judgment on the individuals of the middle in summary. We do not concede that knowledge of every middle such depends on knowledge of the state of each of its individuals specifically, rather it is permissible to know the universal by necessity or proof. Even if the essence of an individual is observed specifically, its state is not known because it is not observed specifically that it is an individual of that universal. So knowledge of the major is directed to the observation of individuals in a general way, i.e., in summary, and knowledge of the conclusion is knowing the state of the individual specifically. So there is no impossibility in deriving it from the first. End. This answer, despite its [؟], is confused. And he mentioned in Sharh al-Mutala' the question and answer more concisely and clearly than this; he said: It should not be said that inference by this figure is a vicious circle, that it is evident, because knowledge of the conclusion depends on knowledge of the universal major, and knowledge of it is only obtained if it is known that the judgment of the major is established for every one of the individuals of the middle which include the minor, so knowledge of the universal major depends on knowledge of the establishment of the major or its negation for the minor which is exactly the conclusion. So if we derive knowledge of the conclusion from knowledge of the major, circularity would follow. But we say: The judgment varies according to the variation of the descriptions of the subject, so that it is known with respect to one description and unknown with respect to another. So knowledge of the judgment is derived with respect to one description from knowledge of it with respect to another description, and there is no impossibility in that. (His saying: It is more noble than the predicate) because the predicate is only sought for the subject, positively or negatively, and everything for which another thing is sought is more noble than that thing. (His saying: In the meanest of its two premises, which is the major) because the middle term is also a subject in the third figure, so it was placed in the third rank. And this premise was meanest because it does not include the subject of the desired conclusion, but rather includes its predicate, which is meaner than its subject. (His saying: at all) but rather for its opposition to it, it was placed in the fourth rank. And this is one of the aspects mentioned for arranging the figures in the mentioned order. And there are other aspects: among them: that the first figure, since it produces the four desired conclusions, was placed in the first rank; the second, since it produces the universal negative which is more noble than the particular affirmative because it is more precise and beneficial, was placed in the second rank; the third, since it produces the particular affirmative, was placed in the third rank; and the fourth, since it is very far from nature, was placed in the fourth rank.
Source pages p. 787

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the arrangement of the four figures of the categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). It begins with the chapter heading 'On Qiyas' and then states that the figures are arranged in a specific order. The first figure is placed first because its production of a conclusion is self-evident (badihi) and closer to natural human reasoning. Al-Dasuqi simply notes that 'closer to nature' explains 'self-evident'. Al-Attar expands by saying 'closer to nature' means either that the mind naturally accepts it more readily, or that it follows the natural order of moving from minor term to middle term to major term without repositioning. He then quotes al-Sayyid 'Isa al-Safawi raising an objection: if the minor is an individual of the middle, then knowing the major premise (all middle is such) requires knowing that every individual of the middle (including the minor) is such, which is precisely the conclusion. This would make the argument circular. The initial answer distinguishes between knowledge of the conclusion (detailed judgment on the minor) and the major (general judgment on the middle collectively), arguing that the universal major can be known through necessity or proof without individual inspection. However, al-Attar finds this answer confused and presents a clearer one from Sharh al-Mutala': the judgment varies with the description of the subject, so we can know a judgment with one description (e.g., the minor as part of the universal) and use that to derive knowledge with another description (the minor specifically), avoiding circularity. The passage then continues with the reasons for the order of the remaining figures. The second figure shares with the first the more noble premise, the minor (which contains the subject of the conclusion). The third figure shares the less noble premise, the major. The fourth figure shares nothing with the first, so it is placed last. Additional reasons are given: the first produces all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), the second produces the universal negative (more noble), the third produces the particular affirmative, and the fourth is far from nature.

بديهي
صغرى
كبرى
دور

A) The minor premise is always true.
B) The major premise is a universal judgment known without detailed inspection of each individual.
C) The conclusion is exactly the same as the minor premise.
D) The syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's apparent circularity is resolved by recognizing that universal knowledge (the major premise) can be known independently of the particular conclusion, preserving the epistemic value of syllogistic inference.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ranking the Four Figures of the Syllogism
Text and commentary explain the order of the second, third, and fourth figures relative to the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ranking of the four syllogistic figures according to classical logic and identify the criteria of nobility and naturalness.

Core Idea
The ranking reflects logical perfection and natural fit, not mere convention.

The syllogism has four figures, but why are they ordered as they are? This lesson explores the criteria used by classical logicians to rank the second, third, and fourth figures relative to the first. The passage explains that the ranking is based on shared premises, nobility of terms, and naturalness to the human mind.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four figures
Recall the four figures of the categorical syllogism: first (middle term subject in major, predicate in minor), second (middle term predicate in both), third (middle term subject in both), fourth (middle term predicate in major, subject in minor).
2
Understand the nobility of premises
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion, which is considered nobler than the predicate because the subject is sought for itself, while the predicate is sought to qualify the subject.
This is key to understanding why the second figure (sharing the minor) ranks above the third (sharing the major).
3
Analyze the order given in the text
The text ranks: first, second (shares noblest premise), third (shares basest premise), fourth (shares none). Follow the reasoning step by step.
4
Consider alternative criteria
The passage mentions other reasons: first figure produces all four proposition types, second produces universal negatives (noblest), third produces particular affirmatives, fourth is unnatural. Also, the first figure keeps the subject-predicate order unchanged, while others alter it.
These are considered matters of choice, not logical necessity.
5
Recognize the status of the fourth figure
The fourth figure is so unnatural that many scholars (al-Ghazali, al-Farabi, Ibn Sina) omitted it. Only three figures are commonly considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢.  ُثمَّ الشَّكلُ الثَّاني ؛ لمشاركَتِه الأوَّلَ في أشرف مقدِّمَتَيه، وهي الصغرى المشتملة على موضوع المطلوب، الذي هو أشرفُ من المحمول . ٣.  ثُمَّ الثَّالث؛ لمشاركته الأوَّلَ في أخس مقدِّمتيه، وهي الكبرى. ٤ .  ثمَّ الرَّابِعُ ؛ لعدم اشتراكه مع الأول أصلاً. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : مَوْضُوْعِ الْمَطْلُوبِ أي : النَّتيجة، واعلَمْ أَنَّ موضوع المطلوبِ أشرفُ من محمولِهِ؛ لأنَّ الموضوع مقصود لذاته، والمحمول مقصود لغيره، وهو الموضوع؛ لأنَّه أتى به ليحمل على الموضوع . العطار الأكبرِ أَوْ بِسَلَبِهِ لِلأصغرِ الَّذي هو عينُ النَّتيجةِ، فَلو استفَدْنَا العلم بالنتيجة مِنَ العِلْم بالكُبرى ؛ لزمَ الدَّورُ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : الحُكْمُ يختلفُ بحسب اختلافِ أوصاف الموضوع حتَّى يكون معلوماً بحسب وَصْفٍ؛ مجهولاً بحسب وصف آخر، فَيُستفاد العلم بالحكم باعتبار وصفٍ من العلم به باعتبارِ وصفٍ آخر، وَلَا استحالة في ذلك. (قَوْلُهُ : هُوَ أَشْرَفُ مِنَ الْمَحْمُوْلِ) لأنَّ المحمول إِنَّما يطلبُ لأجل الموضوع إيجاباً وَسَلباً، وَكُلُّ ما يكون بحيثُ يُطلب أمرُ آخَرُ لأجلِهِ؛ يكون ذلكَ الشَّيء أشرفَ من ذلكَ الأمر. (قَوْلُهُ : فِي أَخَسٌ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ وَهِيَ الْكُبْرَى لأنَّ الحدَّ الأوسط موضوع أيضاً في الشكل الثَّالِثِ، فَلِذَا وُضِعَ في المرتبة الثالثة، وإنَّما كانت هذه المقدّمةُ أَخسَّ؛ لِعَدَمِ اشتمالها على موضوع المطلوب، بل تشتمل على محموله الَّذي هو أخشُ مِن مَوضوعِهِ.  (قَوْلُهُ : أَصْلَاً) بَلْ لِمخالفتِهِ إِيَّاهُ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الرابعة، وهذا هو أحد الوجوه المذكورة في وضع الأشكال على الترتيب المذكور، وهناك أوجه أُخرَى؛ منها : أنَّ الشَّكل الأوَّلَ لَمَّا كانَ مُنتجاً للمطالب الأربعةِ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الأُولَى، والثَّانِي لَمَّا كانَ مُنتجاً لِلسَّلْبِ الكُلّيّ الَّذي هو أشرفُ مِنَ الإيجاب الجزئي لِكَونِهِ أضبط وأنفع؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبةِ الثَّانيةِ والثَّالثُ لَمَّا كَانَ مُنتجاً للإيجابِ الجزئي؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الثالثة، والرَّابِعُ لَمَّا كان بعيداً عن الطَّبْعِ جدا؛ وضعَ في المرتبة الرابعة، ومنها أنَّ الأنسب أن لا يتغيَّر الأصغر والأكبر في القياس عن حالهما في المطلوبِ مِنْ كَوْنِ الأصغرِ موضوعاً والأكبر محمولاً، فَلَمَّا كانَ الشَّكلُ الأَوَّلُ لم يتغير فيه الأصغر والأكبرُ عَن حالهما؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الأُولى، وَلَمَّا كانَ الثاني تغير فيه الأكبرُ عن حالِهِ دونَ الأصغرِ الَّذي هو أشرفُ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الثانية، وَلَمَّا كانَ الثَّالثُ تغيَّرَ فيهِ الأصغرُ عَن حالِهِ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبةِ الثَّالثةِ، وَلَمَّا كَانَ الرَّابِعُ تغيَّرَ فِيهِ كِلَاهُمَا عَنْ حالِهِ؛ وُضِعَ في المرتبة الرابعة، وهناك وجوهٌ أُخَر، وَلَا كبير جدوى في ذلكَ، وَلِذلِكَ قال شارح المطالع : هذه أمورٌ وَضعيَّةٌ اختياريَّةٌ لَا وُجوبَ فيها، وإِنَّما دعا إليها استحسان، والأخذُ بِالأَليقِ والأَوْلَى، وَلِكَوْنِ الرَّابِعِ بَعيداً عَنِ الطَّبْع جِدًّا؛ أسقط المصنِّفُ الكلام عليه بحسب الجهةِ، قيلَ: وَلِذلكَ وقعت الأشكال في القرآنِ ما عداهُ كَمَا بَيَّنَ ذلكَ الشَّيخُ السَّنوسي في مُختصره، وقد أسقطَهُ الغزالي والفارابي وابن سينا حتَّى قال في الإشاراتِ: كَمَا أَنَّ الشَّكل الأوّلَ وُجِدَ كاملاً فاضلاً جدًّا بحيث تكونُ قياسيَّتُهُ ضروريَّةَ النَّتيجة بَيْنَةٌ بِنَفْسِهَا لا تحتاج إلى حجّةٍ؛ كَذَلِكَ وُجِدَ الَّذي هو عكسهُ بَعِيداً عَنِ الطَّبْعِ يحتاج في إبانةِ قياسيته إلى كلفة شاقَّةٍ مُتضاعفةٍ، وَلَا يكاد يسبق إلى الذهن، والطَّبَعُ قياسيتُهُ، وَوُجِدَ الشَّكلانِ الآخرَانِ وإِنْ لم يكونَا بَيْنَي القياسيَّةِ قريبَيْن مِنَ الطَّبْع؛ يكادُ الطَّبْعُ الصَّحيحُ يفطنُ لِقِيَاسِيَّتِهِمَا قبل أن يتبيَّنَ ذلك، أو يكادُ بيان ذلك يسبق إلى الذَّهَنِ مِن نفْسِهِ؛ فَيَلْحظُ لميَّةَ قياسيَّتِهِ عَن قريب؛ فَلِهذا : صارَ لهما قبول، وَلِعكس الأَوَّلِ اطَّراح، وصارَتِ الأشكالُ الاقترانيَّةُ الحمليّة الملتفتُ إليها ثلاثة.
2. Then the second figure, because it shares with the first the noblest of its two premises, which is the minor premise containing the subject of the sought judgement, which is nobler than the predicate. 3. Then the third, because it shares with the first the basest of its two premises, which is the major. 4. Then the fourth, because it shares nothing with the first at all. Al-Dasuqi: His saying 'subject of the sought judgement' means the conclusion. Know that the subject of the sought judgement is nobler than its predicate, because the subject is sought for itself, while the predicate is sought for the sake of something else, which is the subject, because it is brought to be predicated on the subject. Al-Attar: ... (omitted for brevity) ... Thus the categorical syllogistic figures that are considered are three.
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ranking of the four figures of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating the second figure is placed second because it shares the noblest premise (the minor) with the first figure. The third figure shares the basest premise (the major) with the first. The fourth figure shares no premise with the first. Then al-Dasuqi explains why the subject is nobler than the predicate: the subject is sought for its own sake, while the predicate is sought to be attributed to the subject. Al-Attar's commentary continues with a discussion on circularity and the nobility of the subject. The text then mentions alternative reasons for the ranking, including that the first figure produces all four types of logical propositions, while others produce specific ones. It also notes that some scholars, like the author of Sharh al-Matali', consider the ranking matters of choice based on suitability. The passage concludes by citing that the fourth figure is so unnatural that even al-Ghazali, al-Farabi, and Ibn Sina omitted it, and that only three figures are commonly considered.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Three Conditions for a Productive First-Figure Syllogism
Commentary introduces the necessity of three conditions for the first figure to be productive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proof for the necessity of the three conditions in the first figure of the syllogism and identify when a first-figure syllogism is sterile.

Core Idea
The first figure's conditions are proven by sterility: no condition, no conclusion.

In this commentary passage, the author moves from describing the essence of the three syllogistic figures to focusing on the conditions specific to the first figure. The matn states that there are three conditions, and the commentator provides the crucial proof: if any condition is violated, the syllogism becomes sterile ('uqm) – it fails to produce a conclusion. This lesson unpacks that proof and its significance for evaluating syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the shift from essence to condition
The commentator indicates that after discussing the differences between figures in terms of essence (ma'hiya), the matn now discusses differences in terms of condition (ishtira). This sets the stage for introducing the three conditions.
2
Identify the three conditions in the matn
The matn states 'And it is conditioned in the first...' but the full list is not reproduced here. However, the commentator refers to 'the three conditions' as necessary for the first figure.
For a concrete example, consider the valid first-figure syllogism: Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden. This syllogism satisfies the conditions.
3
Understand the proof: sterility
The commentator explicitly states that the proof of the conditions is that sterility ('uqm) appears when any of the three conditions is absent. Sterility means the syllogism does not yield a necessary conclusion.
This is a strong argument: the conditions are not arbitrary; they are empirically necessary for validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ شروط الشكل الأول وضروبه المنتجة (قَوْلُهُ: وَيُشْتَرَطُ ... إِلَخ لَمَّا فرغَ مِن بيانِ الفرق بين الأشكال بحسب الماهية؛ شرع في بيان الفرق بينها بحسب الاشتراط فقال: (وَيُشترط في الأوَّلِ... إلخ)، ثمَّ الدَّليلُ على هذا الاشتراط ؛ ظهور العقم بانتفاء أحدِ الشُّروط الثَّلاثةِ.
Chapter on syllogism. Conditions of the first figure and its productive moods. His saying: 'And it is conditioned in the first... etc.' Since he finished explaining the difference between the figures in terms of essence, he began explaining the difference between them in terms of condition, saying: 'And it is conditioned in the first... etc.' Then the proof of this condition is the appearance of sterility when one of the three conditions is absent.
Source pages p. 789

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the transition in the matn. It states that after discussing the differences between the figures in terms of their essences (ma'hiya), the author now turns to the differences in terms of conditions (ishtira). The matn condition statement for the first figure is cited inductively. The commentator then notes that the proof for the necessity of these conditions is that if any of them is missing, the syllogism fails to produce a conclusion ('uqm, sterility). In the first figure, the conditions ensure that the middle term is properly placed to necessitate the conclusion. For example, in a fiqh context, if we say 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden,' the conditions (such as the minor being affirmative and the major being universal) are satisfied. If one condition fails, like if the minor is negative, the syllogism yields no conclusion.

Key Terms
الشرط
العقم

Checkpoint

B) The number of premises increases.
C) The conclusion becomes more certain.
D) The middle term disappears.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the three conditions for the first figure is proven by the fact that violating any one renders the syllogism sterile.
