The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Necessity of an Affirmative Minor Premise in the First Figure
Author states the condition of affirmative minor for first figure and defends it against counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a negative minor premise is invalid in the first figure of a categorical syllogism and rebut a counterexample using the concept of an extraneous premise.

Core Idea
A negative minor breaks the inclusion of the subject under the middle term.

In the first figure of categorical syllogism, the minor premise must be affirmative. Without this, the subject (minor term) is not included under the middle term, so the judgment in the major premise cannot transfer. Some object that if the middle and major are equal, a negative minor still yields a valid conclusion. This lesson unpacks the condition and the rebuttal to this objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its rationale
Find the statement: 'A condition is required in the first figure: affirmation of the minor premise.' The reason given: if the minor is negative, the subject is not under the middle, so the major premise's judgment cannot apply.
The condition is about the structure, not the content.
2
Analyze the counterexample
The objector gives an example: 'Nothing of man is a horse; every horse is a neighing thing; therefore nothing of man is a neighing thing.' The middle ('horse') and major ('neighing thing') are equal in extension. It seems the conclusion follows even though the minor is negative.
The conclusion appears valid because of the equality between middle and major.
3
Evaluate the rebuttal
The author replies that the inference works only because of an implicit extra premise: 'Whatever one of two equals is negated from, the other is also negated.' This premise is not part of the original two; it is extraneous. Without it, the conclusion would not follow.
The rebuttal shows that the syllogism is not self-contained; the condition is about the basic form.
4
Recognize the role of extraneous premises
An extraneous premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya) has terms different from both given premises. Its addition is what makes the inference valid in the counterexample, but it is not allowed in a pure syllogistic form.
This clarifies why the condition stands: the first figure alone cannot produce a conclusion from a negative minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ. وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِي الشَّكل الأَوَّلِ: إِيْجَابُ الصُّغْرَى. ثُمَّ الدَّليلُ على هذا الاشتراط؛ ظهور العقم بانتفاء أحدِ الشُّروط الثَّلاثةِ. (قَوْلُهُ: إِيْجَابُ الصُّغْرَى ... إلخ) لأنَّكَ إذا نفيت شيئاً عن شيء؛ لم يكن الحكم على المنفي حكماً على المنفي عنه. قِيلَ: لو كانَ إيجابُ الصُّغرى شرطاً؛ لَما تحقَّقَ الإنتاج بدونِهِ؛ لانتفاء المشروط عند انتفاء الشَّرطِ، لكنَّ الثَّالي باطل، فإنَّ الأوسط إذا كانَ مُساوياً للأكبر؛ فَكُلُّ شيءٍ سُلِبَ عنهُ أوسط؛ سُلِبَ عنهُ الأكبر؛ لأنَّ سَلْبَ أحد المتساويَيْنِ عَن شيءٍ؛ يستلزمُ سَلْبَ الآخَرِ عنه ضرورةً، كَقولنا: لا شيء مِنَ الإنسانِ بِفرسٍ، وَكُلُّ فرسٍ صَهَّالٌ، فَإِنَّهُ ينتج: لا شيء مِنَ الإنسانِ بِصَهَّال. وَأُجِيبُ عنه: بأنَّ لزومَ سَلْبِ الأكبرِ عَنِ الأصغرِ في تلك المادة بواسطةِ العِلْمِ؛ بأنَّ كُلَّ مَا سُلِبَ عنهُ أحد المتساويَيْنِ سُلِبَ عنهُ الآخَرُ بالضَّرورةِ، وَإِلَّا، لم يكونا متساويين، وهذه المقدّمة أجنبيَّةٌ تُغايرُ حدودُهَا حدودَ المقدمتين، فليس استلزام القياس النتيجةَ لِذاتِهِ، بل بواسطة هذه المقدّمة.
Chapter on Syllogism. A condition is required in the first figure: affirmation of the minor premise. Then the proof of this condition: the appearance of barrenness when any of the three conditions is missing. (His saying: 'affirmation of the minor premise...') because when you negate something of something, the judgment on the negated thing is not a judgment on the thing from which it is negated. It was said: If affirmation of the minor were a condition, then production would not be realized without it, due to the disappearance of the conditioned when the condition is absent, but the consequent is false, because when the middle is equal to the major, then everything from which the middle is negated, the major is also negated, because negating one of two equals from something necessitates negating the other from it necessarily, as in our saying: 'Nothing of man is a horse, and every horse is a neighing thing,' then it produces: 'Nothing of man is a neighing thing.' And it was answered: That the necessity of negating the major from the minor in that material is through the knowledge that everything from which one of two equals is negated, the other is necessarily negated, otherwise they would not be equals, and this premise is extraneous, its terms different from the terms of the two premises, so the syllogism does not entail the result by itself, but through this extraneous premise.
Source pages p. 789

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessary condition for the first figure of categorical syllogism: the minor premise must be affirmative. The author states the condition and provides the rationale: if the minor premise were negative, the subject (minor term) would not be included under the middle term, so the judgment from the major premise (which applies to the middle) cannot transfer to the minor. A potential counterexample is raised: if the middle term happens to be equal in extension to the major term (i.e., they are co-extensive), then a negative minor still seems to produce a valid conclusion. For instance, 'Nothing of man is a horse; every horse is a neighing thing; therefore nothing of man is a neighing thing.' The author replies that this inference only works because of an implicit extra premise: 'Whatever one of two equals is negated from, the other is also negated.' This premise is not part of the original two premises; it is extraneous. Hence, the syllogism does not produce the conclusion by itself but only with the addition of this extra premise. Therefore, the condition of an affirmative minor premise remains necessary for a purely self-contained first-figure syllogism.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
إيجاب الصغرى
متساويان
مقدمة أجنبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the minor premise be affirmative in the first figure of a categorical syllogism?
A) Because a negative minor would make the middle term equal to the major term.
B) Because a negative minor would prevent the subject from falling under the middle term, so the major premise's judgment cannot transfer.
C) Because a negative minor is only valid when the middle is narrower than the major.
D) Because the first figure only produces universal conclusions.

Key Takeaway
A first-figure syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise; apparent counterexamples with equal terms rely on an extraneous premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure syllogism on the topic of khamr (wine): 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this beverage is an intoxicant; therefore this beverage is forbidden.' Rewrite the minor premise as a negative proposition and explain why the conclusion does not follow without an extra premise.
?
Consider this syllogism: 'No fish is a mammal; every whale is a mammal; therefore no fish is a whale.' Is it in the first figure? If not, what figure is it? Does it use an affirmative minor? Discuss its validity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Affirmative Minor Premise in First Figure: Condition and Refutation of Counterexample
Defense of affirmative minor condition in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the minor premise must be affirmative in the first figure and identify when a negative minor apparently produces a conclusion due to an extraneous premise.

Core Idea
A negative minor in the first figure never yields a conclusion by itself; any production requires an additional premise.

In this passage, the commentator defends the rule that the minor premise in the first figure must be affirmative. A critic offers a counterexample using equal terms, but the response shows that the apparent production relies on an implicit premise about equality. A further objection about compound negatives is also dismantled. Understanding this refines your ability to evaluate syllogistic validity in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being defended
The commentator states that the affirmative minor premise is required. Find the phrase 'إيجاب الصغرى' and note that this is the condition under discussion.
2
Understand why the condition exists
If the minor premise were negative, the minor term would not be subsumed under the middle term. Consequently, the judgment of the major on the middle would not extend to the minor. This is the logical rationale.
3
Examine the counterexample
The critic offers: 'Nothing of man is a horse; every horse is a neigher.' This has a negative minor and appears to produce a valid conclusion: 'Nothing of man is a neigher.' Identify the terms: minor term 'man', middle 'horse', major 'neigher'.
The conclusion seems to follow, but the commentator will show it depends on an additional assumption.
4
Follow the response to the counterexample
The response says the production is not by the syllogism itself but by means of an extraneous premise: the knowledge that the middle (horse) and major (neigher) are equal. Without that premise, the inference fails. Recognize that the equality premise is not stated in the original two premises.
5
Analyze the compound negative objection
A further objection claims a compound negative proposition (e.g., 'Nothing of man is a horse and everything non-horse is non-neigher') entails an affirmative and thus produces. The response clarifies that a compound proposition is actually two propositions; the negative part alone does not entail the affirmative. The real producer is the affirmative component.
This reinforces that the minor must be straightforwardly affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : إِيْجَابُ الصُّغْرَى إِنَّما اشترط إيجابها ; لأنَّها لو كانَتْ سالبة; لم يندرج الأصغر تحتَ الأوسط ، فَلَا يتعدى الحكم بالأكبر على الأوسط إلى الأصغرِ، قِيلَ: لو كانَ إيجابُ الصُّغرى شرطاً ; لَما تحقَّقَ الإنتاج بدونِهِ; لانتفاء المشروط عند انتفاء الشَّرطِ ، لكنَّ الثَّالي باطل، فإنَّ الأوسط إذا كانَ مُساوياً للأكبر ; فَكُلُّ شيءٍ سُلِبَ عنهُ أوسط ; سُلِبَ عنهُ الأكبر; لأنَّ سَلْبَ أحد المتساويَيْنِ عَن شيءٍ; يستلزمُ سَلْبَ الآخَرِ عنه ضرورةً، كَقولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسانِ بِفرسٍ، وَكُلُّ فرسٍ صَهَّالٌ، فَإِنَّهُ ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسانِ بِصَهَّال ، وَأُجِيبُ عنه : بأنَّ لزومَ سَلْبِ الأكبرِ عَنِ الأصغرِ في تلك المادة بواسطةِ العِلْمِ ; بأنَّ كُلَّ مَا سُلِبَ عنهُ أحد المتساويَيْنِ سُلِبَ عنهُ الآخَرُ بالضَّرورةِ، وَإِلَّا ، لم يكونا متساويين، وهذه المقدّمة أجنبيَّةٌ تُغايرُ حدودُهَا حدودَ المقدمتين، فليس استلزام القياس النتيجةَ لِذاتِهِ ، بل بواسطة هذه المقدّمة، قال في شرح المطالع : لا يُقالُ : السَّالبة إذا كانَت مُركَّبَةً تنتج في الصُّغرى; لأنَّها تستلزم الموجبةَ ، وَهِيَ تَستلزمُ النَّتيجةَ ، وتَوَسيط الموجبة لا يُخرجُهَا عَنِ الاستلزام; لأنَّها ليسَتْ مُقدِّمةً غريبةً ; لأنَّا نقولُ : القضيَّةُ المركَّبةُ لَمَّا اشتملَتْ على حُكْمَيْنِ; فَهِيَ فِي التحقيق قضيَّتان، فإن أردتُمْ بِقَولِكُم : السَّالبة المركَّبةُ مُستلزمةٌ لِلموجبة ; أَنَّ مَجموع الحُكْمَيْنِ مُستلزم لِلإيجاب; فهو ممنوع ، وإن أردتُمْ أنَّ السَّلْبَ مُستلزم; فهو بين البطلانِ وإِنْ أردتُمْ أنَّ الإيجاب مُستلزم للإيجاب; فَهو هذيان، فالمنتج هناك بالتحقيقِ ليس إلا الإيجاب .ا.هـ.
His statement: 'Affirmative of the minor' — indeed he stipulated its affirmation because if it were negative, the minor would not fall under the middle, so the judgment of the major on the middle would not extend to the minor. It was objected: If affirmation of the minor were a condition, then production would not be realized without it, because the conditioned is absent when the condition is absent, but the consequent is false, for if the middle is equal to the major, then everything from which the middle is negated, the major is negated from it, because negation of one of the two equals from something necessarily entails negation of the other from it, as in our saying: 'Nothing of man is a horse, and every horse is a neigher,' for it produces: 'Nothing of man is a neigher.' And it was answered: That the entailment of the negation of the major from the minor in that material is by means of the knowledge that everything from which one of the two equals is negated, the other is necessarily negated from it, otherwise they would not be equal, and this premise is extraneous, its terms differing from the terms of the two premises, so the entailment of the conclusion by the syllogism is not by itself but by means of this premise. He said in Sharh al-Mutali': It should not be said: The negative, if it is compound, produces in the minor because it entails the affirmative, and it entails the conclusion, and the mediation of the affirmative does not remove it from entailment because it is not an extraneous premise; for we say: The compound proposition, since it contains two judgments, is in reality two propositions. If you mean by your statement 'The compound negative entails the affirmative' that the total of the two judgments entails the affirmation, it is denied; and if you mean that the negation entails it, it is clearly false; and if you mean that the affirmation entails affirmation, it is nonsense. So what produces there is in reality only the affirmative. End.
Source pages p. 790

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the condition that the minor premise in the first figure must be affirmative. The commentator begins by stating why the affirmative is necessary: if the minor were negative, the minor term (الأصغر) would not be subsumed under the middle term (الأوسط), so the judgment of the major term (الأكبر) on the middle would not transfer to the minor. The author then presents a counterargument: if the affirmative condition were strict, then production would be impossible when the condition is absent, but the consequent is false because if the middle equals the major, then negating the middle from something necessarily negates the major, as in the example 'Nothing of man is a horse; every horse is a neigher' producing 'Nothing of man is a neigher.' The response clarifies that this production depends on an extraneous premise: the knowledge that the middle and major are equal. Without this added premise, the syllogism would not produce by itself. Then a further objection is considered: a compound negative proposition (e.g., 'Nothing of man is a horse and everything non-horse is...') might entail the affirmative, and via that produce the conclusion. The answer is that a compound proposition is actually two propositions; the negative part does not entail the affirmative in a way that preserves the syllogism's self-sufficiency. The real producer is the affirmative component. Thus, the condition stands: the minor must be straightforwardly affirmative.

Key Terms
الصغرى
الأوسط
الأكبر
المتساويان
قضية مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the counterexample 'Nothing of man is a horse; every horse is a neigher' not invalidate the condition that the minor must be affirmative?
A) Because the conclusion 'Nothing of man is a neigher' is false.
B) Because the syllogism's validity depends on an extraneous premise about the equality of 'horse' and 'neigher'.
C) Because the minor premise is actually affirmative when properly analyzed.
D) Because the first figure does not require an affirmative minor.

Key Takeaway
A negative minor in the first figure never yields a conclusion by the syllogism's own terms; any production requires an additional premise, so the condition of affirmative minor is necessary for self-sufficient reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid first-figure categorical syllogism on a fiqh issue (e.g., concerning the prohibition of riba) that has an affirmative minor premise.
?
In the counterexample 'Nothing of man is a horse; every horse is a neigher', identify the extraneous premise that would be needed to make the conclusion follow without it. Explain why that premise is not part of the original syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
When Syllogistic Conditions Fail – Material vs. Form
Commentary on exceptions to syllogistic production conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between syllogistic failures due to false premises/material and failures due to form, using the examples from al-Salkuti.

Core Idea
Form ensures validity; material determines truth and production.

The first figure has strict conditions (affirmative minor, universal major). But the great commentator al-Salkuti notes two peculiar cases: conditions met yet no product, and conditions absent yet product occurs. Understanding these helps us see that syllogistic production depends not only on form but also on the truth and nature of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Recall first-figure conditions
List the standard conditions: minor must be affirmative, major universal, subject of minor is subject of conclusion, etc.
2
Analyze the first counterexample (knowledge divided)
Examine the syllogism: 'Mawrid al-qisma is knowledge; every knowledge is either theoretical or necessary.' The minor is false because 'mawrid al-qisma' is the concept of knowledge, not knowledge itself. Thus no product due to false premise, not invalid form.
The product fails because of material falsity.
3
Analyze the second counterexample (species-man)
Consider: 'Some of the species is man; nothing of man is a species.' The minor is not a common proposition (qadiyya muta'arifa); it asserts identity, not predication. Hence the form does not apply, and no product follows.
The minor is not a standard categorical predication.
4
Understand the material confinement anomaly
Examine: 'Nothing of stone is an animal; some animal is a neigher' produces 'Nothing of stone is a neigher' despite violating conditions. The production is due to the major term 'neigher' being confined to 'animal'. Replacing with 'body' changes the conclusion, proving the effect is material, not formal.
The conclusion follows because of the specific material, not the figure's form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحواشي السَّلْكُوتِيَّةِ قيل : قد تتحقَّقُ الشرائط ولا ينتج، وقد لا تتحقَّقُ وينتج ، أمَّا الأَوَّلُ ; فنحو قولنا : موردُ القسمةِ عِلْمٌ، وكلُّ عِلْمٍ إِمَّا نظري أو ضروري ، وقولنا : بعضُ النَّوع إنسانٌ، وَلَا شيء من الإنسانِ بِنوعٍ مع كذب نتيجتهما ، والجواب عَن الأَوَّلِ : أنَّ الصُغرى كاذبةٌ ; لأنَّ مورد القسمة مفهوم العلم وهو مَعلومٌ لا عِلْمٌ، وإنَّ أُريد من حيثُ حصوله في الذهنِ فَلَا نسلّم كذبَ النتيجة، وعن الثَّاني : بأنَّ الصُّغرى لَيسَتْ مِنَ القضايا المتعارفة بأن يكون المحمول فيها صادقاً على أفراد الموضوع صِدْقَ الكُلِّيّ على جزئيّاتِهِ، إِذِ الحكمُ هَهُنا باتِّحادِ المحمول بالموضوع ذهناً وخارجاً، وأما الثاني ; فنحو قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بحيوان وبعضُ الحيوانِ صهَّالٌ، فَإِنَّهُ ينتج : لا شيء من الحجر بصهَّال، مع انتفاء الأمرَيْنِ ; لأنَّ سلب شيءٍ عن أفرادِ شيءٍ وحصر شيءٍ آخَرَ في المسلوبِ; يفيدُ سَلْبَ المحصور في ذلك الشَّيء، والجواب : أنَّ الإنتاج المذكور بواسطة خصوصيَّةِ المادة وكونَ المحمول محصوراً لا باعتبار هيئةِ الشَّكلِ ; فإنَّهُ لو بدَّلَ الكُبرى بقولنا : بعضُ الحيوانِ جسم ; كانَ الحقُّ الإيجاب .. هـ . قال بعضُهم : وَلِاشْتِراطِ إيجابِ الصُّغرى في هذا الشَّكلِ، لم يقع لفظ : وحدَهُ في صُغراهُ لِمَا فِيهَا مِن معنى النَّفي، فإذا قُلْتَ الإنسان وحده ضاحك ، كان في قوَّةِ قضيَّتَين نفي وإثبات; أي: الإنسان ضاحكٌ وليس غيرُ الإنسان ضاحكاً ، وبهذا : ظهر أنَّ من المغالطةِ مَا لو قِيلَ : الإِنسانُ وحدَهُ ضاحكٌ، وكلُّ ضاحك حيوان ينتج : الإنسان وحده حيوان، وهو باطل; لأنَّ هذا القياس لم يستوفِ شرائط الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ ; لعدم إيجابِ صُغراهُ، وَلَا حاجةً لِقَولِ بعضهم : إنَّ : وحده، حالٌ مِنَ الضَّاحِكِ تقدَّمَتْ على عاملها، وحينئذٍ فليست قيداً في موضوع الصغرى، فالنتيجةُ إنَّما هِيَ : الإنسانُ ضاحك مع عدم استقامته في نفسه; لأنَّ الحَالَ تفيدُ التَّقييد مُطلقاً ; تقدَّمَتْ أو تأخَرَتْ ، على أنَّ دعوى تقدُّمِهَا مَمنوعةٌ .
And in the al-Salkuti glosses it was said: It may happen that the conditions are met and no product, and it may happen that they are not met and product occurs. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'The thing divided (mawrid al-qisma) is knowledge, and every knowledge is either theoretical or necessary,' and our saying: 'Some of the species is man, and nothing of man is a species' with the falsity of their conclusions. The answer to the first: that the minor is false because the thing divided is the concept of knowledge, and it is known, not knowledge; and if intended from the aspect of its occurrence in the mind, we do not concede the falsity of the conclusion. And regarding the second: that the minor is not among common propositions where the predicate is true of the individuals of the subject as the universal is true of its particulars, for the judgment here is of the identity of predicate and subject in mind and externally. As for the second [type, where conditions are not met but product occurs], it is like our saying: 'Nothing of stone is an animal, and some animal is a neigher,' for it produces: 'Nothing of stone is a neigher,' despite the absence of two things [affirmative minor and universal major], because negation of something from individuals of something and confining another thing to the negated thing entails negation of the confined from that thing. The answer: That the mentioned production is by virtue of the particularity of the material and the confinement of the predicate, not by virtue of the figure's form; for if he replaced the major with our saying: 'Some animal is a body,' the correct conclusion would be affirmative. End. Some said: Because of the requirement of affirmative minor in this figure, the word 'alone' (wahdahu) does not occur in its minor because it contains a meaning of negation. So if you say 'Man alone is laughing,' it is in the strength of two propositions, negation and affirmation: man is laughing, and no one other than man is laughing. By this it is apparent that it is a fallacy if it is said: 'Man alone is laughing, and every laughing is an animal' produces 'Man alone is an animal,' and this is false, because this syllogism does not fulfill the conditions of the first figure due to lack of affirmation of its minor. And there is no need for the statement of some that 'alone' is a circumstance (ḥāl) from 'laughing' that preceded its operator, and therefore it is not a restriction in the subject of the minor, so the conclusion is only 'Man is laughing' while it is not sound in itself; because the circumstance indicates restriction absolutely, whether it precedes or follows. Moreover, the claim of its precedence is untenable.
Source pages p. 791

Commentary
This passage addresses two anomalies in syllogistic theory: cases where the formal conditions are met but no valid conclusion follows, and cases where conditions are absent yet a conclusion appears. The first type includes two examples. In the first example, 'The thing divided is knowledge; every knowledge is either theoretical or necessary' — the minor is false because the thing divided is the concept of knowledge, not knowledge itself. So the failure is due to a false premise, not the form. In the second example, 'Some of the species is man; nothing of man is a species' — the minor is not a standard universal proposition; it asserts identity (اتحاد) of predicate and subject, not predication in the usual sense. Thus the form does not apply. The second anomaly is where conditions (affirmative minor and universal major) are missing yet a conclusion seems to follow, as in 'Nothing of stone is an animal; some animal is a neigher' producing 'Nothing of stone is a neigher.' The answer clarifies that this depends on the material: the major term 'neigher' is confined to 'animal', and the negation of 'animal' from stone necessarily negates 'neigher'. This is not due to the figure's form, because if we replace the major with a non-confined predicate like 'body', the conclusion becomes affirmative, proving the form was not responsible. Finally, the passage discusses the logical force of the word 'وحده' (alone). In the phrase 'الإنسان وحده ضاحك', it implicitly contains a negation: man laughs and no one else laughs. Thus if used as the minor premise in the first figure, it is not truly affirmative; it has a hidden negative component. Consequently, a syllogism like 'Man alone is laughing; every laughing is an animal' would produce 'Man alone is an animal,' which is false. The fallacy arises because the minor does not meet the affirmative condition. Some try to argue that 'وحده' is a circumstantial expression (حال) that does not modify the subject, so the minor is still affirmative. The author rejects this, insisting that any حَال indicates restriction, regardless of position, so the minor remains non-affirmative. Thus the condition stands.

Key Terms
الشرائط
القضية المتعارفة
المادة

Checkpoint

A) Because the major premise is false.
B) Because the minor premise is false.
C) Because the figure is wrong.
D) Because the middle term is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's form may be valid, but its product depends on the truth and nature of its premises (material).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Logical Force of 'Wahdahu' in the First Figure
Analysis of 'wahdahu' as a logical operator violating first-figure conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hidden negation in premises containing 'wahdahu' (alone) and explain why such premises violate the affirmative minor condition in the first figure.

Core Idea
‘Alone’ smuggles a negation into an affirmative sentence.

One strict condition of the first figure is that the minor premise must be affirmative. But what if the minor contains the word 'wahdahu' (alone)? The commentator shows that 'man alone laughs' actually implies two propositions: one affirmative and one negative. This hidden negation breaks the condition, leading to a fallacy. Some scholars try to rescue it by grammatical analysis, but the author rejects those attempts.

Guided Path
1
Decompose the phrase with 'wahdahu'
Translate 'al-insān waḥdahu ḍāḥik' as 'Man alone is laughing'. Recognize that it implies: 'Man is laughing' and 'No one other than man is laughing.'
2
Identify the hidden negative component
The second part contains a negation, so the overall meaning of the phrase is not purely affirmative. Hence it cannot serve as an affirmative minor in the first figure.
3
Test the fallacious syllogism
Consider: 'Man alone is laughing; every laughing is an animal; therefore man alone is an animal.' The conclusion is false because other animals also laugh (e.g., hyenas). The failure is due to the minor not being truly affirmative.
The syllogism appears valid but is a fallacy (mughalat).
4
Engage with the grammatical objection
Some scholars argue that 'wahdahu' is a circumstantial expression (ḥāl) that does not modify the subject, so the minor remains affirmative. The author rebuts that a ḥāl always restricts, regardless of position, so the minor is still non-affirmative.
This shows the depth of logical analysis in the tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحواشي السَّلْكُوتِيَّةِ قيل : قد تتحقَّقُ الشرائط ولا ينتج، وقد لا تتحقَّقُ وينتج ، أمَّا الأَوَّلُ ; فنحو قولنا : موردُ القسمةِ عِلْمٌ، وكلُّ عِلْمٍ إِمَّا نظري أو ضروري ، وقولنا : بعضُ النَّوع إنسانٌ، وَلَا شيء من الإنسانِ بِنوعٍ مع كذب نتيجتهما ، والجواب عَن الأَوَّلِ : أنَّ الصُغرى كاذبةٌ ; لأنَّ مورد القسمة مفهوم العلم وهو مَعلومٌ لا عِلْمٌ، وإنَّ أُريد من حيثُ حصوله في الذهنِ فَلَا نسلّم كذبَ النتيجة، وعن الثَّاني : بأنَّ الصُّغرى لَيسَتْ مِنَ القضايا المتعارفة بأن يكون المحمول فيها صادقاً على أفراد الموضوع صِدْقَ الكُلِّيّ على جزئيّاتِهِ، إِذِ الحكمُ هَهُنا باتِّحادِ المحمول بالموضوع ذهناً وخارجاً، وأما الثاني ; فنحو قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بحيوان وبعضُ الحيوانِ صهَّالٌ، فَإِنَّهُ ينتج : لا شيء من الحجر بصهَّال، مع انتفاء الأمرَيْنِ ; لأنَّ سلب شيءٍ عن أفرادِ شيءٍ وحصر شيءٍ آخَرَ في المسلوبِ; يفيدُ سَلْبَ المحصور في ذلك الشَّيء، والجواب : أنَّ الإنتاج المذكور بواسطة خصوصيَّةِ المادة وكونَ المحمول محصوراً لا باعتبار هيئةِ الشَّكلِ ; فإنَّهُ لو بدَّلَ الكُبرى بقولنا : بعضُ الحيوانِ جسم ; كانَ الحقُّ الإيجاب .. هـ . قال بعضُهم : وَلِاشْتِراطِ إيجابِ الصُّغرى في هذا الشَّكلِ، لم يقع لفظ : وحدَهُ في صُغراهُ لِمَا فِيهَا مِن معنى النَّفي، فإذا قُلْتَ الإنسان وحده ضاحك ، كان في قوَّةِ قضيَّتَين نفي وإثبات; أي: الإنسان ضاحكٌ وليس غيرُ الإنسان ضاحكاً ، وبهذا : ظهر أنَّ من المغالطةِ مَا لو قِيلَ : الإِنسانُ وحدَهُ ضاحكٌ، وكلُّ ضاحك حيوان ينتج : الإنسان وحده حيوان، وهو باطل; لأنَّ هذا القياس لم يستوفِ شرائط الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ ; لعدم إيجابِ صُغراهُ، وَلَا حاجةً لِقَولِ بعضهم : إنَّ : وحده، حالٌ مِنَ الضَّاحِكِ تقدَّمَتْ على عاملها، وحينئذٍ فليست قيداً في موضوع الصغرى، فالنتيجةُ إنَّما هِيَ : الإنسانُ ضاحك مع عدم استقامته في نفسه; لأنَّ الحَالَ تفيدُ التَّقييد مُطلقاً ; تقدَّمَتْ أو تأخَرَتْ ، على أنَّ دعوى تقدُّمِهَا مَمنوعةٌ .
And in the al-Salkuti glosses it was said: It may happen that the conditions are met and no product, and it may happen that they are not met and product occurs. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'The thing divided (mawrid al-qisma) is knowledge, and every knowledge is either theoretical or necessary,' and our saying: 'Some of the species is man, and nothing of man is a species' with the falsity of their conclusions. The answer to the first: that the minor is false because the thing divided is the concept of knowledge, and it is known, not knowledge; and if intended from the aspect of its occurrence in the mind, we do not concede the falsity of the conclusion. And regarding the second: that the minor is not among common propositions where the predicate is true of the individuals of the subject as the universal is true of its particulars, for the judgment here is of the identity of predicate and subject in mind and externally. As for the second [type, where conditions are not met but product occurs], it is like our saying: 'Nothing of stone is an animal, and some animal is a neigher,' for it produces: 'Nothing of stone is a neigher,' despite the absence of two things [affirmative minor and universal major], because negation of something from individuals of something and confining another thing to the negated thing entails negation of the confined from that thing. The answer: That the mentioned production is by virtue of the particularity of the material and the confinement of the predicate, not by virtue of the figure's form; for if he replaced the major with our saying: 'Some animal is a body,' the correct conclusion would be affirmative. End. Some said: Because of the requirement of affirmative minor in this figure, the word 'alone' (wahdahu) does not occur in its minor because it contains a meaning of negation. So if you say 'Man alone is laughing,' it is in the strength of two propositions, negation and affirmation: man is laughing, and no one other than man is laughing. By this it is apparent that it is a fallacy if it is said: 'Man alone is laughing, and every laughing is an animal' produces 'Man alone is an animal,' and this is false, because this syllogism does not fulfill the conditions of the first figure due to lack of affirmation of its minor. And there is no need for the statement of some that 'alone' is a circumstance (ḥāl) from 'laughing' that preceded its operator, and therefore it is not a restriction in the subject of the minor, so the conclusion is only 'Man is laughing' while it is not sound in itself; because the circumstance indicates restriction absolutely, whether it precedes or follows. Moreover, the claim of its precedence is untenable.

The Condition of an Affirmative Minor and the Fallacy of 'Alone'
Refutation of fallacy involving 'alone' in first figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect hidden negations in a minor premise that violate the first figure's affirmative condition.

Core Idea
The first figure demands an affirmative minor; even the word 'alone' smuggles in a negation.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism requires the minor premise to be affirmative. But sometimes a seemingly affirmative minor conceals a negation, as in the phrase 'man alone is laughing'. The commentator refutes attempts to treat 'alone' as a mere circumstantial accusative and reaffirms the strict condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Write down the minor premise of the syllogism: 'Man alone is laughing.' Notice the word 'alone'.
2
Check for hidden negation
Analyze the meaning of 'alone'. It implies 'man is laughing and no one else is laughing', adding a negative component. The minor is not purely affirmative.
The word 'alone' (وحده) gives the force of two propositions: one affirmative, one negative.
3
Apply the condition of the first figure
The first figure requires an affirmative minor. Since the minor is effectively negative, the syllogism is invalid.
4
Refute the alternative interpretation
Some claim that 'alone' is a circumstantial accusative preceding its operator, so it does not restrict the subject. But the commentator argues that a circumstantial accusative always restricts, regardless of position, and the claim of precedence is disputed.
Thus, the condition stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: القِيَاسِ البطلانِ وإِنْ أردتُمْ أنَّ الإيجاب مُستلزم للإيجاب؛ فَهو هذيان، فالمنتج هناك بالتحقيقِ ليس إلا الإيجاب .ا.هـ. وفي الحواشي السَّلكوتية قيل : قد تتحقَّقُ الشرائط ولا ينتج، وقد لا تتحقَّقُ وينتج ، أمَّا الأَوَّلُ ؛ فنحو قولنا : موردُ القسمةِ عِلْمٌ، وكلُّ عِلْمٍ إِمَّا نظري أو ضروري ، وقولنا : بعضُ النَّوع إنسانٌ، وَلَا شيء من الإنسانِ بِنوعِ مع كذب نتيجتهما ، والجواب عَن الأَوَّلِ : أنَّ الصُغرى كاذبةٌ ؛ لأنَّ مورد القسمة مفهوم العلم وهو مَعلومٌ لا عِلْمٌ، وإنَّ أُريد من حيثُ حصوله في الذهنِ فَلَا نسلّم كذبَ النتيجة، وعن الثَّاني : بأنَّ الصُّغرى لَيسَتْ مِنَ القضايا المتعارفة بأن يكون المحمول فيها صادقاً على أفراد الموضوع صِدْقَ الكُلّيّ على جزئيّاتِهِ، إِذِ الحكمُ هَهُنا باتِّحادِ المحمول بالموضوع ذهناً وخارجاً، وأما الثاني ؛ فنحو قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بحيوان وبعضُ الحيوانِ صهَّالٌ، فَإِنَّهُ ينتج : لا شيء من الحجر بصهال، مع انتفاء الأمرَيْنِ ؛ لأنَّ سلب شيءٍ عن أفرادِ شيءٍ وحصر شيءٍ آخَرَ في المسلوبِ؛ يفيدُ سَلْبَ المحصور في ذلك الشَّيء، والجواب : أنَّ الإنتاج المذكور بواسطة خصوصيَّةِ المادة وكونَ المحمول محصوراً لا باعتبار هيئةِ الشَّكل ؛ فإنَّهُ لو بدَّلَ الكُبرى بقولنا : بعضُ الحيوانِ جسم ؛ كانَ الحقُّ الإيجاب .. هـ . قال بعضُهم : وَلَا شترط إيجابُ الصُّغرى في هذا الشَّكلِ، لم يقع لفظ : وحدَهُ في صُغراهُ لِمَا فِيهَا مِن معنى النَّفي، فإذا قُلْتَ الإنسان وحده ضاحك ، كان في قوَّةِ قضيَّتَين نفي وإثبات؛ أي: الإنسان ضاحكٌ وليس غيرُ الإنسان ضاحكاً ، وبهذا : ظهر أنَّ من المغالطةِ مَا لو قِيلَ : الإِنسانُ وحدَهُ ضاحكٌ، وكلُّ ضاحك حيوان ينتج : الإنسان وحده حيوان، وهو باطل؛ لأنَّ هذا القياس لم يستوفِ شرائط الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ ؛ لعدم إيجابِ صُغراهُ، وَلَا حاجةً لِقَولِ بعضهم : إنَّ : وحده، حالٌ مِنَ الصَّاحِكِ تقدَّمَتْ على عاملها، وحينئذٍ فليست قيداً في موضوع الصغرى، فالنتيجةُ إنَّما هِيَ : الإنسانُ ضاحك مع عدم استقامته في نفسه؛ لأنَّ الحَالَ تفيدُ التَّقييد مُطلقاً ؛ تقدَّمَتْ أو تأخَرَتْ ، على أنَّ دعوى تقدُّمِهَا مَمنوعةٌ .
Chapter: Syllogism. [As for] the two invalidities: if you mean that affirmation entails affirmation, that is nonsense; what is produced there, in reality, is only affirmation. End quote. In the al-Salkuti glosses it is said: Sometimes the conditions are realized but nothing is produced, and sometimes they are not realized but something is produced. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'The subject of division is knowledge, and every knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive' – and our saying: 'Some of the species is man, and no man is a species' – with the falsity of their conclusions. The answer to the first: the minor is false because the subject of division is the concept of knowledge, which is known, not knowledge; if it is intended in terms of its occurrence in the mind, we do not concede the falsity of the conclusion. As for the second: the minor is not among the conventional propositions such that the predicate is true of the individuals of the subject in the manner of a universal to its particulars, for the judgment here is about the identity of the predicate with the subject mentally and externally. As for the second (type of exception): it is like our saying: 'No stone is an animal, and some animals are horses' – for it produces: 'No stone is a horse', despite the absence of both conditions [i.e., affirmative minor and universal major]. Because the negation of something from individuals of a thing and the confinement of another thing in the negated thing entails the negation of the confined thing from that thing. The answer: the aforementioned production is due to the specificity of the matter and the fact that the predicate is confined, not due to the form of the figure; for if he changed the major to our saying: 'Some animals are bodies', the correct [conclusion] would be affirmative. ... End quote. Some said: The condition that the minor is affirmative in this figure is [required]. The word 'alone' does not occur in its minor because it contains a negative meaning. So if you say 'Man alone is laughing', it has the force of two propositions: negation and affirmation; i.e., man is laughing and no one other than man is laughing. By this it becomes clear that it is among fallacies if it is said: 'Man alone is laughing, and every laughing thing is an animal', producing 'Man alone is an animal', which is false. Because this syllogism does not fulfill the conditions of the first figure due to the lack of an affirmative minor. And there is no need for the statement of some that 'alone' is a circumstantial accusative (hal) from 'laughing' that has preceded its operator, and then it is not a restriction in the subject of the minor, so the conclusion is only 'Man is laughing' – but this is not valid in itself, because the circumstantial accusative indicates restriction absolutely, whether it precedes or follows. Moreover, the claim that it precedes is disputed.
Source pages p. 791

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the conditions of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by quoting a statement about invalid syllogisms (البطلان), then cites a view from the al-Salkuti glosses that conditions may be met without production, and vice versa. Two examples of the first type are given. First example: 'The subject of division (mawrid al-qisma) is knowledge, and every knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive' – the minor is false because 'mawrid al-qisma' means the concept of knowledge, which is 'known' (ma'lum), not 'knowledge' (ilm). However, if by 'knowledge' is meant its mental occurrence, the conclusion is not conceded false. Second example: 'Some of the species is man, and no man is a species' – the minor is not an ordinary universal predication; it asserts identity, not subsumption. Then the passage gives an example of production despite conditions lacking: 'No stone is an animal, some animals are horses' yields 'No stone is a horse' even though the minor is negative and major is particular. The answer is that this production relies on the specific matter (the predicate 'horse' is confined to animals), not on the pure form. If the major were replaced with 'some animals are bodies', the conclusion would be affirmative. The passage then turns to the condition that the minor must be affirmative in the first figure. It explains that the word 'alone' (wahdahu) contains implicit negation, so 'Man alone is laughing' means 'Man is laughing and no one else is laughing'. This leads to a fallacious syllogism: 'Man alone is laughing, every laughing thing is an animal, therefore man alone is an animal' – which is false because the minor is not truly affirmative. Some tried to treat 'alone' as a circumstantial accusative preceding its operator, removing the restriction from the subject. But the commentator refutes this: a circumstantial accusative always imposes restriction, regardless of position. Furthermore, the claim that 'alone' precedes its operator is contested. Thus, the original condition stands.

الصغرى
المغالطة

C) Because the middle term is not distributed.
D) Because the conclusion is false.

Key Takeaway
The word 'alone' introduces a hidden negation, so the minor is not truly affirmative; the first figure's condition is violated.

When Conditions Are Met but No Product: The Necessity of True Premises
Example of conditions met but no product due to false minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a first-figure syllogism fails despite meeting formal conditions due to a false minor premise.

Core Idea
Formal conditions are necessary but not sufficient; the premises must also be materially true.

Sometimes a syllogism satisfies all the conditions of the first figure but still fails to produce a valid conclusion because the premises are false. The commentator gives two examples where the minor premise is problematic, showing that formal validity is not enough – truth matters.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first syllogism
Write the syllogism: 'Mawrid al-qisma is knowledge; every knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive; therefore mawrid al-qisma is either theoretical or intuitive.'
This is a first-figure syllogism (Barbara mood).
2
Verify the formal conditions
Check that the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor, the minor is affirmative, and the major is universal. All conditions are met.
3
Test the truth of the minor premise
Is 'Mawrid al-qisma is knowledge' true? The commentator says no: mawrid al-qisma is the concept of knowledge, which is 'known' (ma'lum), not 'knowledge' (ilm) itself. So the minor is false.
A false premise means the conclusion is not guaranteed, even if the form is valid.
4
Conclude: formal validity alone is insufficient
The syllogism is formally valid but materially unsound. A valid syllogism requires both correct form and true premises. The first figure conditions ensure formal validity, but truth is separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: القِيَاسِ البطلانِ وإِنْ أردتُمْ أنَّ الإيجاب مُستلزم للإيجاب؛ فَهو هذيان، فالمنتج هناك بالتحقيقِ ليس إلا الإيجاب .ا.هـ. وفي الحواشي السَّلكوتية قيل : قد تتحقَّقُ الشرائط ولا ينتج، وقد لا تتحقَّقُ وينتج ، أمَّا الأَوَّلُ ؛ فنحو قولنا : موردُ القسمةِ عِلْمٌ، وكلُّ عِلْمٍ إِمَّا نظري أو ضروري ، وقولنا : بعضُ النَّوع إنسانٌ، وَلَا شيء من الإنسانِ بِنوعِ مع كذب نتيجتهما ، والجواب عَن الأَوَّلِ : أنَّ الصُغرى كاذبةٌ ؛ لأنَّ مورد القسمة مفهوم العلم وهو مَعلومٌ لا عِلْمٌ، وإنَّ أُريد من حيثُ حصوله في الذهنِ فَلَا نسلّم كذبَ النتيجة، وعن الثَّاني : بأنَّ الصُّغرى لَيسَتْ مِنَ القضايا المتعارفة بأن يكون المحمول فيها صادقاً على أفراد الموضوع صِدْقَ الكُلّيّ على جزئيّاتِهِ، إِذِ الحكمُ هَهُنا باتِّحادِ المحمول بالموضوع ذهناً وخارجاً، وأما الثاني ؛ فنحو قولنا : لا شيء مِنَ الحجرِ بحيوان وبعضُ الحيوانِ صهَّالٌ، فَإِنَّهُ ينتج : لا شيء من الحجر بصهال، مع انتفاء الأمرَيْنِ ؛ لأنَّ سلب شيءٍ عن أفرادِ شيءٍ وحصر شيءٍ آخَرَ في المسلوبِ؛ يفيدُ سَلْبَ المحصور في ذلك الشَّيء، والجواب : أنَّ الإنتاج المذكور بواسطة خصوصيَّةِ المادة وكونَ المحمول محصوراً لا باعتبار هيئةِ الشَّكل ؛ فإنَّهُ لو بدَّلَ الكُبرى بقولنا : بعضُ الحيوانِ جسم ؛ كانَ الحقُّ الإيجاب .. هـ . قال بعضُهم : وَلَا شترط إيجابُ الصُّغرى في هذا الشَّكلِ، لم يقع لفظ : وحدَهُ في صُغراهُ لِمَا فِيهَا مِن معنى النَّفي، فإذا قُلْتَ الإنسان وحده ضاحك ، كان في قوَّةِ قضيَّتَين نفي وإثبات؛ أي: الإنسان ضاحكٌ وليس غيرُ الإنسان ضاحكاً ، وبهذا : ظهر أنَّ من المغالطةِ مَا لو قِيلَ : الإِنسانُ وحدَهُ ضاحكٌ، وكلُّ ضاحك حيوان ينتج : الإنسان وحده حيوان، وهو باطل؛ لأنَّ هذا القياس لم يستوفِ شرائط الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ ؛ لعدم إيجابِ صُغراهُ، وَلَا حاجةً لِقَولِ بعضهم : إنَّ : وحده، حالٌ مِنَ الصَّاحِكِ تقدَّمَتْ على عاملها، وحينئذٍ فليست قيداً في موضوع الصغرى، فالنتيجةُ إنَّما هِيَ : الإنسانُ ضاحك مع عدم استقامته في نفسه؛ لأنَّ الحَالَ تفيدُ التَّقييد مُطلقاً ؛ تقدَّمَتْ أو تأخَرَتْ ، على أنَّ دعوى تقدُّمِهَا مَمنوعةٌ .
Chapter: Syllogism. [As for] the two invalidities: if you mean that affirmation entails affirmation, that is nonsense; what is produced there, in reality, is only affirmation. End quote. In the al-Salkuti glosses it is said: Sometimes the conditions are realized but nothing is produced, and sometimes they are not realized but something is produced. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'The subject of division is knowledge, and every knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive' – and our saying: 'Some of the species is man, and no man is a species' – with the falsity of their conclusions. The answer to the first: the minor is false because the subject of division is the concept of knowledge, which is known, not knowledge; if it is intended in terms of its occurrence in the mind, we do not concede the falsity of the conclusion. As for the second: the minor is not among the conventional propositions such that the predicate is true of the individuals of the subject in the manner of a universal to its particulars, for the judgment here is about the identity of the predicate with the subject mentally and externally. As for the second (type of exception): it is like our saying: 'No stone is an animal, and some animals are horses' – for it produces: 'No stone is a horse', despite the absence of both conditions [i.e., affirmative minor and universal major]. Because the negation of something from individuals of a thing and the confinement of another thing in the negated thing entails the negation of the confined thing from that thing. The answer: the aforementioned production is due to the specificity of the matter and the fact that the predicate is confined, not due to the form of the figure; for if he changed the major to our saying: 'Some animals are bodies', the correct [conclusion] would be affirmative. ... End quote. Some said: The condition that the minor is affirmative in this figure is [required]. The word 'alone' does not occur in its minor because it contains a negative meaning. So if you say 'Man alone is laughing', it has the force of two propositions: negation and affirmation; i.e., man is laughing and no one other than man is laughing. By this it becomes clear that it is among fallacies if it is said: 'Man alone is laughing, and every laughing thing is an animal', producing 'Man alone is an animal', which is false. Because this syllogism does not fulfill the conditions of the first figure due to the lack of an affirmative minor. And there is no need for the statement of some that 'alone' is a circumstantial accusative (hal) from 'laughing' that has preceded its operator, and then it is not a restriction in the subject of the minor, so the conclusion is only 'Man is laughing' – but this is not valid in itself, because the circumstantial accusative indicates restriction absolutely, whether it precedes or follows. Moreover, the claim that it precedes is disputed.
Source pages p. 791

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the conditions of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by quoting a statement about invalid syllogisms (البطلان), then cites a view from the al-Salkuti glosses that conditions may be met without production, and vice versa. Two examples of the first type are given. First example: 'The subject of division (mawrid al-qisma) is knowledge, and every knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive' – the minor is false because 'mawrid al-qisma' means the concept of knowledge, which is 'known' (ma'lum), not 'knowledge' (ilm). However, if by 'knowledge' is meant its mental occurrence, the conclusion is not conceded false. Second example: 'Some of the species is man, and no man is a species' – the minor is not an ordinary universal predication; it asserts identity, not subsumption. Then the passage gives an example of production despite conditions lacking: 'No stone is an animal, some animals are horses' yields 'No stone is a horse' even though the minor is negative and major is particular. The answer is that this production relies on the specific matter (the predicate 'horse' is confined to animals), not on the pure form. If the major were replaced with 'some animals are bodies', the conclusion would be affirmative. The passage then turns to the condition that the minor must be affirmative in the first figure. It explains that the word 'alone' (wahdahu) contains implicit negation, so 'Man alone is laughing' means 'Man is laughing and no one else is laughing'. This leads to a fallacious syllogism: 'Man alone is laughing, every laughing thing is an animal, therefore man alone is an animal' – which is false because the minor is not truly affirmative. Some tried to treat 'alone' as a circumstantial accusative preceding its operator, removing the restriction from the subject. But the commentator refutes this: a circumstantial accusative always imposes restriction, regardless of position. Furthermore, the claim that 'alone' precedes its operator is contested. Thus, the original condition stands.

Key Terms
مورد القسمة
الصغرى
القضايا المتعارفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the syllogism 'Mawrid al-qisma is knowledge; every knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive; therefore mawrid al-qisma is either theoretical or intuitive' fail to produce a valid conclusion?
A) Because the major is false.
B) Because the minor is false – mawrid al-qisma is not knowledge but 'known'.
C) Because the first figure condition of affirmative minor is violated.
D) Because the middle term is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
Meeting the first figure conditions ensures formal validity, but the conclusion is only guaranteed if the premises are true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the syllogism 'Every obligatory act is rewarded by God; fasting during Ramadan is obligatory; therefore fasting during Ramadan is rewarded by God.' Check whether both premises are true according to Islamic sources. Is the minor false? Defend your answer.
?
Find a first-figure syllogism in a kalam text (e.g., al-Sanusiyya) and test both its formal validity and the truth of its premises.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Apparent Production Despite Missing Conditions: Matter vs. Form
Example of production despite missing conditions due to matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid logical inference and a conclusion that follows only because of the specific content of the premises.

Core Idea
A conclusion may be true due to the subject matter even if the syllogistic form is defective.

Sometimes a syllogism that violates the conditions of the first figure still yields a true conclusion. The commentator gives an example and explains that this is due to the specificity of the matter, not the logical form. Changing the major premise reveals that the original inference was not formally valid.

Guided Path
1
Examine the syllogism
Write the syllogism: 'No stone is an animal; some animals are horses; therefore no stone is a horse.' Check the form: minor is negative, major is particular – both violate first figure conditions.
2
Observe that the conclusion is true
Despite the invalid form, the conclusion 'no stone is a horse' is actually true. Why does this happen?
3
Identify the role of matter
The conclusion holds not because of the syllogistic form but because the predicate 'horse' is confined within 'animal'. Since 'stone' is negated from 'animal', it is automatically negated from 'horse'. This is a material inference, not formal.
The commentator says: 'الانتاج المذكور بواسطة خصوصيَّة المادة' – i.e., due to the specificity of the matter.
4
Test by changing the major
Replace the major with 'some animals are bodies'. Now the syllogism would attempt to conclude something affirmative, breaking the inference. This shows the original conclusion relied on the specific content, not the logical form.
If the form were valid, changing matter would preserve the inference pattern.

Source Text

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Actuality of the Minor Premise in the First Figure
Commentator explains requirement for actuality of minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a minor premise satisfies the actuality condition for a valid first-figure categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A possible minor premise breaks the transfer of the middle term to actuality, invalidating the conclusion.

The first figure requires that the minor premise be actual, not possible. This condition ensures that the subject of the minor actually possesses the middle term, allowing the major's judgment to apply. The commentator explains this with reference to disagreements between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi, and clarifies that actuality is necessary for production.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition on the minor premise
The minor must be actual (fi‘liyya), meaning it asserts an actual state, not a possible one. The text states: 'its actuality, i.e., that the minor is not one of the two possibles (particular possible and general possible).'
2
Understand why possible minors fail
The major premise indicates that everything to which the middle is actually affirmed is judged by the major. If the minor is possible, it only indicates that the subject possibly has the middle; it may not be actual, so the major's judgment does not extend to it. The commentator says: 'the possible minor only indicates that the minor term is among those to which the middle is possibly affirmed; it may not come into actuality, so the judgment does not extend to it.'
3
Compare the views of Ibn Sina and al-Farabi
Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) held that actuality is required for the minor, so no production if the minor is possible. Al-Farabi held that truth by possibility suffices. The commentator sides with Ibn Sina, noting that al-Farabi's view is problematic.
4
Apply to an example
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Some beverage can be intoxicating.' The minor is possible, so we cannot conclude that the beverage is haram, because it may not actually be intoxicating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) بحسب الجهة (فِعْلِيَّتُهَا) بأن تكونَ الصُّغرى غير الممكنتَين . (وَ) بحسَبِ الكمّ كُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى)، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فِعْلِيَّتُهَا) أي : وقعَتْ بالفعل. (قَوْلُهُ : غَيْرُ الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ) أي: الممكنة الخاصة، والممكنة العامة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَبِحَسَبِ الْكَمّ كُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى) أي : حتَّى يدخل المحكوم عليه فإِنَّكَ إِذا قُلْتَ : كلُّ سفرجل مطعوم، وبعضُ المطعوم ربوي، لم يلزم منه كونُ السفرجل ربويا ؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة الحكم على بعض المطعوم أن يتناول الربوي. (قَوْلُهُ: كُلَّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى) فإنْ قُلْتَ : شرط فى الكُبرى أن تكونَ كلّيَّة، وقد صرَّحَ في الشَّمسيَّةِ بأنَّ المخصوصة في حكم الكلّيَّةِ نحو: هذا زيد، وزيد إنسان، ينتج : هذا إنسان . (قَوْلُهُ : وَفِعْلِيَّتُهَا) بأنْ تكونَ غير الممكنتَيْنِ ؛ لأنَّ الكُبرى تدلُّ على أنَّ كُلَّ مَا يثبت له الأوسط بالفعل فهوَ مَحكوم عليه بالأكبر، والصُّغرى الممكنة إنَّما تدلُّ على أنَّ الأصغر ممَّا ثبت لَهُ الأوسط بالإمكان، فيجوز أن لا يخرج إلى الفعل، فَلَا يتعدى الحكم إليه، وهذا ظاهرُ إن اعتبر في صدقِ الموضوع على أفراده بالفعل هو رأي الشَّيخِ، فَلا إنتاج عنده، أمَّا على قول الفارابي : إِنَّ صدقَهُ بِالإمكانِ؛ فالقياس منتج، وقد عَلِمْتَ مَا في ذلك. (قَوْلُهُ: وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى لأنَّهُ لو لم يكن كذلكَ؛ فَلَا إنتاج؛ لأنَّه يمكن أن يكونَ لَوْ البعضُ المحكوم عليه بالأكبر غير الأصغرِ كَمَا يُقالُ : كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان وبعضُ الحيوان فرس، قال الهروي : لا يُقالُ : يجوز تعيين ذلك البعض بأنْ تُجعَلَ الإضافةُ للعهد الخارجيّ، فَلَا يتحقَّقُ حينئذٍ إلا الاندراج، فيصحُ الإنتائج ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : تصيرُ (قَوْلُهُ : المحكوم عليه وهو الحد الأصغر ، وقوله فيها ؛ أي الكبرى، أي: في موضوعها وهو الحد الأوسط، فإذا كانت الكبرى جزئية لم يتحقق الاندراج كمثاله ، إذ المحكوم عليه بأنه ربوي المقتات المدخر من المطعوم والسفرجل ليس منه فلم يشمله حكم الكبرى فلا إنتاج . ا . هـ .
(And) according to modality (jiha): its actuality (fi‘liyyatuha), i.e., that the minor is not one of the two possibles. (And) according to quantity: the universality of the major. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'its actuality' means it occurs in actuality. His saying: 'not one of the two possibles' means the particular possible and the general possible. His saying: 'and according to quantity: the universality of the major' means so that the subject of judgment is included; for if you say: 'Every quince is edible, and some edible things are subject to usury (ribawi)', it does not follow that the quince is subject to usury; because it is not necessary that the judgment on some edible things includes the ribawi. His saying: 'universality of the major': if you say: the condition for the major is that it be universal, and it was explicitly stated in al-Shamsiyya that the particular [proposition] is in the ruling of universal, such as 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is a man' – it produces 'This is a man'. His saying: 'and its actuality' means that it is not one of the two possibles, because the major indicates that everything to which the middle is actually affirmed is judged by the major, while the possible minor only indicates that the minor term is among those to which the middle is possibly affirmed; it may not come into actuality, so the judgment does not extend to it. This is apparent if actuality is considered in the truth of the subject to its individuals – this is the view of al-Shaykh [Ibn Sina], so there is no production according to him. As for the view of al-Farabi that its truth is by possibility, the syllogism produces – and you have known what is in that. His saying: 'and universality of the major' because if it were not so, there would be no production, because it is possible that the part judged by the major is not the minor, as it is said: 'Every man is an animal, and some animals are horses'. Al-Harawi said: It cannot be said that it is permissible to specify that part by making the addition for external reference, so then only subsumption is realized, and production is valid; because we say: it becomes... His saying: 'the subject of judgment is the minor term, and his saying: 'in it' means in the major, i.e., in its subject which is the middle term; so if the major is particular, subsumption is not realized, as in his example, for the subject judged as ribawi is the stored food from the edible, and the quince is not from it, so the judgment of the major does not include it, hence no production. End quote.
Source pages p. 792

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on two conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism: the actuality (fi‘liyya) of the minor premise and the universality (kulliyya) of the major premise. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains each. First, 'actuality of the minor' means the minor premise must assert an actual rather than possible state—it cannot be a possible proposition (mumkina), whether particular possible (mumkina khassa) or general possible (mumkina ‘amma). The reason given is that the major premise states that everything to which the middle term is actually affirmed is ruled by the major term. If the minor is possible, it only asserts that the subject possibly has the middle term; it may not actually occur, so the major's judgment does not transfer. This is the view of Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh). Al-Farabi, however, held that truth by possibility suffices for production, but the commentator notes that this is problematic. Second, 'universality of the major' means the major premise must be universal (not particular). If it were particular, the middle term might not include the minor term. For example, 'Every man is an animal, some animals are horses' does not entail that man is a horse. Al-Harawi attempted to argue that if the particular major is interpreted as referring to a specific external set, subsumption might occur, but the commentator rejects that. The passage also clarifies that the condition of universality for the major is strict; a particular proposition like 'some animals are horses' cannot serve as a major in the first figure. The commentary ends by emphasizing that without these conditions, the middle term does not properly subsume the minor, and production fails.

Key Terms
الجهة
فعلية
الممكنة

A) Because the major premise requires actual possession of the middle term.
B) Because the conclusion must be actual.
C) Because the minor must be universal.
D) Because of al-Farabi's view.

Key Takeaway
The minor premise in the first figure must assert actual possession of the middle term, not mere possibility, otherwise the conclusion does not follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure syllogism on an Islamic topic where the minor premise is actual, and then rewrite it with a possible minor. Explain why the second syllogism fails to produce a certain conclusion.
?
Given the following argument, determine whether the minor satisfies the actuality condition: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh can be intoxicating. Therefore nabidh is haram.' What is the problem?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Universality of the Major Premise in the First Figure
Commentator explains requirement for universality of major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a major premise satisfies the universality condition required for a valid first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
A particular major premise breaks the subsumption of the minor term under the middle term, invalidating the syllogism.

The first figure requires that the major premise be universal. If it is particular, the middle term may not include all individuals of the minor term, so the conclusion does not follow. The commentator explains this with examples and addresses an objection by al-Harawi, reinforcing the necessity of universality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition on the major premise
The major premise must be universal (kulliyya). The text says: 'according to quantity: the universality of the major' and explains that if it is not universal, there is no production.
2
Understand why a particular major fails
If the major is particular, the part of the middle term that is judged by the major may not include the minor term. Example: 'Every man is an animal; some animals are horses' – it does not follow that a man is a horse, because the 'some animals' may exclude humans.
The commentator says: 'it is possible that the part judged by the major is not the minor.'
3
Examine al-Harawi's attempted exception
Al-Harawi argued that if the particular major refers to a specific external set (e.g., 'those animals'), then subsumption might occur. But the commentator rejects this, because the 'some' in the major does not guarantee that the minor term is included in that specific set.
The commentator responds: 'we say: it becomes...' and explains that subsumption is not realized.
4
Apply to an example
Consider: 'Every quince is edible; some edible things are ribawi.' Even if the major is particular, we cannot conclude that quince is ribawi, because the 'some edible things' may not include quince. This is the example given in the text.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on two conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism: the actuality (fi‘liyya) of the minor premise and the universality (kulliyya) of the major premise. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains each. The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Conditions of the First Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Author explains the two conditions for a valid first figure syllogism and its affirmative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions (actuality of the minor and universality of the major) required for a valid first figure categorical syllogism, and explain why each condition is necessary using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A valid first figure syllogism requires an actual minor and a universal major to guarantee the conclusion.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the most powerful form because it yields certain conclusions directly. However, not every syllogism in this figure is valid. Classical logicians identified two essential conditions: the minor premise must be actual (not merely possible), and the major premise must be universal. In this lesson, we explore why these conditions are necessary and how they ensure sound reasoning, drawing on the explanations of al-Dasuqi and al-Attar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Locate in the Arabic passage the two conditions mentioned: 'actuality of the minor' (فعليتها) and 'universality of the major' (كلية الكبرى). Note that the first condition concerns modality (جهة) and the second concerns quantity (كم).
2
Understand the modality condition
Al-Dasuqi explains that if the minor is possible (ممكنة), it only indicates that the middle term possibly applies to the minor, but it may never become actual. Since the major judgment attaches only to what actually has the middle, the conclusion cannot be drawn. Internalize this: the minor must be an actual proposition.
The two possible propositions excluded are al-mumkina al-khassa and al-mumkina al-'amma.
3
Understand the quantity condition
Al-Attar explains that without a universal major, the middle term might not be distributed, so the minor may not fall under the major. He gives the example: 'Every human is an animal; some animal is a horse' does not prove 'Some human is a horse.' Recognize that the major must be universal to ensure subsumption.
4
Consider the objection about personal propositions
The text raises an objection: al-Shamsiyya treated personal propositions (e.g., 'This is Zayd') as universal. Al-Dasuqi responds that such propositions, though functionally universal, are not used in scientific syllogisms because they are not general. Understand why personal propositions are excluded from formal logic.
Personal propositions are considered universal in ruling but not in scientific practice.
5
Grasp the purpose of the first figure
The passage concludes that the first figure, with these conditions, is designed to produce affirmative conclusions (الموجبتان). It yields both universal and particular affirmative conclusions from affirmative premises. Note that the first figure does not produce negative conclusions without additional conditions.
This is a final cause: the purpose of the first figure is to generate affirmative results.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) بحسب الجهة (فِعْلِيَّتُهَا) بأن تكونَ الصُّغرى غير الممكنتَين . (وَ) بحسَبِ الكمّ كُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى). قال الدسوقي: قَوْلُهُ: وَفِعْلِيَّتُهَا) بأنْ تكونَ غير الممكنتَيْنِ ؛ لأنَّ الكُبرى تدلُّ على أنَّ كُلَّ مَا يثبت له الأوسط بالفعل فهوَ مَحكوم عليه بالأكبر، والصُّغرى الممكنة إنَّما تدلُّ على أنَّ الأصغر ممَّا ثبت لَهُ الأوسط بالإمكان، فيجوز أن لا يخرج إلى الفعل، فَلَا يتعدى الحكم إليه. وقال العطار: قَوْلُهُ: وَكُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى لأنَّهُ لو لم يكن كذلكَ؛ فَلَا إنتاج؛ لأنَّه يمكن أن يكونَ لَوْ البعضُ المحكوم عليه بالأكبر غير الأصغرِ كَمَا يُقالُ : كلُّ إنسانٍ حيوان وبعضُ الحيوان فرس. فإن قيل: شرط فى الكُبرى أن تكونَ كلّيَّة، وقد صرَّحَ في الشَّمسيَّةِ بأنَّ المخصوصة في حكم الكلّيَّةِ نحو: هذا زيد، وزيد إنسان، ينتج : هذا إنسان. أجاب الدسوقي: قُلْنَا : الشَّخصيَّة، وإن كانت في حكم الكلِّيَّة؛ لكنها غير معتد بها؛ لعدم استعمالها في العلوم. فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ بأن يكون موضوعها كليًّا؛ (لِيُنْتِجَ) هذه علة غائية؛ أي: الغرض من وضع الشكل الأوَّل، والاشتراط في صغراه وكبراه. أن يُنتج الصُّغريان (الموجِبَتَانِ) الكلِّيَّة والجزئية مع الكبرى (الموجِبَةِ) الكُلِّيَّةِ النَّتيجتين (الموْجِبَتَيْن) كلَّيَّةً وجزئيَّةٌ.
And) by modality, its actuality: that the minor be not of the two possible kinds. And) by quantity, the universality of the major. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement: 'its actuality': that it be not of the two possible kinds because the major indicates that every [thing] to which the middle is actually attributed is judged by the major, while the possible minor only indicates that the minor is among things to which the middle is possibly attributed, so it is possible that it does not become actual, so the judgment does not extend to it. Al-Attar said: His statement: 'and universality of the major' because if it were not so, there would be no production; for it is possible that the part judged by the major is other than the minor, as is said: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse.' If it is said: The condition for the major is that it be universal, and it was stated in al-Shamsiyya that the particular is in the ruling of the universal, like 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is a human' produces 'This is a human.' Al-Dasuqi answered: We say: The personal [proposition], although it is in the ruling of the universal, is not relied upon because it is not used in the sciences. Section: On the syllogism: that its subject be universal; to produce this is a final cause; i.e., the purpose of positing the first figure and the condition in its minor and major. That the two minors (the two affirmative) universal and particular with the major (affirmative) universal produce the two affirmative conclusions, universal and particular.
Source pages p. 792

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for a valid first figure syllogism. It begins by stating two conditions: (1) by modality, the minor premise must be actual, meaning it cannot be a possible proposition (neither al-mumkina al-khassa nor al-mumkina al-'amma). (2) By quantity, the major premise must be universal. Al-Dasuqi explains the first condition: the major premise indicates that everything to which the middle term is actually attributed is subject to the major term's judgment. If the minor premise is possible, it only indicates that the minor term possibly has the middle, but it might never become actual, so the judgment does not transfer. Al-Attar explains the second condition: if the major is not universal, we cannot guarantee that the middle term is predicated of the minor; for example, 'Every human is an animal' (true) and 'Some animal is a horse' (true) does not produce 'Some human is a horse' because the horse might be a different animal. The text then addresses an objection: particular propositions (like 'This is Zayd') are treated as universal in some contexts (as per al-Shamsiyya), so why require universality? Al-Dasuqi responds that such personal propositions, though functionally universal, are not relied upon in scientific discourse because they are not used in the sciences. Finally, the passage states the purpose of the first figure: to produce two affirmative conclusions (universal and particular) from two affirmative premises (one universal and one particular minor with a universal major). The entire discussion highlights the precision required in modal and quantitative conditions for valid syllogistic inference in the first figure.

Key Terms
فِعْلِيَّة
الممكنتان
كُلِّيَّة الْكُبْرَى
الشَّخْصِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the major premise be universal in the first figure syllogism?
A) Because the minor premise is always particular.
B) Without it, the middle term may not be distributed, and we cannot ensure that the minor falls under the major.
C) Because the first figure only produces universal conclusions.
D) Because the major premise must be actual.

Key Takeaway
A valid first figure categorical syllogism requires an actual minor premise and a universal major premise to ensure the conclusion follows necessarily.

The Syllogism

5 min
Two Valid Moods of the First Figure
Author states the two valid moods of the first figure with examples and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid affirmative moods of the first figure of categorical syllogism and verify whether a given syllogism meets the conditions for these moods.

Core Idea
The first figure's two affirmative moods yield certain knowledge when the major is universal and the minor is affirmative.

This passage introduces the two valid moods of the first figure of categorical syllogism. These moods are the bedrock of demonstrative reasoning in the Islamic sciences, as they produce certain conclusions. By mastering these moods, you will be able to evaluate arguments in kalam, fiqh, and other disciplines, distinguishing sound proofs from fallacious ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. Label the terms accordingly.
The major premise is the one containing the predicate of the conclusion; the minor contains the subject of the conclusion.
2
Check the major premise's quantity
The major premise must be universal (كلي). If it is not universal, the syllogism is invalid in the first figure.
Universality means the predicate applies to all members of the subject class.
3
Check the minor premise's quality
The minor premise must be affirmative (موجبة). It can be universal or particular, but not negative.
A negative minor would violate the first figure conditions.
4
Determine the mood
If both premises are universal affirmative, it is the first mood (Barbara). If the minor is particular affirmative and the major universal affirmative, it is the second mood (Darii).
These two moods are the only valid ones with affirmative conclusions in the first figure.
5
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion is affirmative and matches the quantity of the minor: universal if the minor is universal, particular if the minor is particular.
For example: 'Every J is B, every B is A' yields 'Every J is A'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: On Syllogism. (The condition is) that its (the major premise's) subject is universal. (This is) so that it produces. The two minor premises, the universal affirmative and the particular affirmative, with the universal affirmative major, produce two affirmative conclusions, universal and particular. So the universal affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major produces the universal affirmative, as in our saying: Every J is B, and every B is A, so every J is A. And the particular affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major produces the particular, as in our saying: Some J is B, and every B is A, so some J is A.
Source pages p. 793

Commentary
This passage defines the two valid moods of the first figure of categorical syllogism, with the condition that the major premise must be universal and the minor premise must be affirmative. The author states that the two affirmative moods—when the minor is universal and the major universal affirmative—yield a universal affirmative conclusion (e.g., 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore every human is a body'). When the minor is particular affirmative and the major universal affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore some human is a body'). The key condition is that the subject of the major premise must be universal, ensuring the middle term is distributed appropriately. These moods are foundational because they produce certain knowledge in the sciences.

Key Terms
موضوعها (mawḍū'uhā): The subject of the major premise in the first figure; it must be universal.
صغرى (ṣughrā): The minor premise of the syllogism; it must be affirmative in the first figure. [Note: In other figures, the minor may be negative; here it is restricted to affirmative.]
كبرى (kubrā): The major premise of the syllogism; it must be universal in the first figure.
موجبة (mūjiba): An affirmative proposition; the minor must be affirmative in the moods discussed.
كلية (kulliyya): A universal proposition; the major must be universal, and the minor can be universal or particular.
جزئية (juz'iyya): A particular proposition; the minor can be particular affirmative in the second mood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The first figure has exactly two valid affirmative moods: Barbara (both premises universal affirmative) and Darii (minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative).

Two Affirmative Moods of the First Figure
Affirmative moods of the first figure explained with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the two valid affirmative moods of the first figure and verify their conclusions.

Core Idea
The first figure yields affirmative conclusions: universal when both premises are universal, particular when the minor is particular.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the most powerful because its conclusions are immediate and certain. This passage from the commentary explains the two affirmative moods: one producing a universal affirmative conclusion, another producing a particular affirmative conclusion. You will learn to identify the premises and apply the rule that the conclusion follows the weaker premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise (sughrā) contains the subject of the conclusion; the major premise (kubrā) contains the predicate. In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
Example: 'Every J is B' (minor), 'Every B is A' (major).
2
Check the quality and quantity of each premise
Both premises are affirmative. For the first mood, both are universal. For the second mood, the minor is particular and the major is universal.
Affirmation is quality; universality/particularity is quantity.
3
Apply the rule: conclusion follows the weaker
The conclusion takes the quantity of the weaker premise: if one premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if both are universal, the conclusion is universal. In both moods, the quality is affirmative because all premises are affirmative.
Weaker means particular over universal, and negative over affirmative, but here only quantity matters.
4
Construct the conclusion
The conclusion has the subject of the minor and the predicate of the major. Substitute concrete Islamic examples to verify: e.g., 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore every human is a body.'
Use the substitution given by al-Dasuqi.
