Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the valid moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. It begins by stating the two affirmative moods: (1) universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major yields universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore every human is a body'), and (2) particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major yields particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some human is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore some human is a body'). The comment then notes that the term 'with the affirmative' in the matn includes both affirmative and negative majors, anticipating the negative moods. Al-Dasuqi explains the examples by substituting concrete terms: J=human, B=animal, A=body. He also explains that the conclusion follows the weaker premise (the one with lower quantity or quality). Then he provides a detailed comparison of the four categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) in terms of nobility (sharaf). He states that affirmation is nobler than negation because existence is nobler than non-existence, and universality is nobler than particularity because it is more precise and beneficial. Thus universal affirmative is the noblest, and particular negative is the lowest. Universal negative is nobler than particular affirmative because universality contributes nobility from multiple aspects whereas affirmation contributes only one aspect. Al-'Attar then clarifies the examples for the particular affirmative mood. Throughout, the examples are from the Islamic intellectual tradition: human, animal, body—terms used in kalām and falsafah.

Nobility of Categorical Propositions
Ranking of four categorical propositions by nobility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank the four categorical propositions by nobility and justify the ranking using the criteria of existence (affirmation) and precision (universality).

Core Idea
Universal affirmative is the noblest proposition; particular negative is the lowest; universal negative is nobler than particular affirmative.

Not all propositions carry equal weight in Islamic scholarship. The commentary classifies categorical propositions by nobility (sharaf) based on quality and quantity. Affirmation is considered nobler than negation because it indicates existence; universality is nobler than particularity because it is more precise and useful in sciences. This ranking helps scholars choose stronger premises for syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two dimensions: quality and quantity
Quality: affirmative (positive) vs. negative (absence). Quantity: universal (all) vs. particular (some). The commentary treats these as distinct aspects that confer nobility.
Affirmation is existence; negation is non-existence.
2
Rank the four combinations
Start with universal affirmative (A) which has both noble aspects: existence and universality. Then compare universal negative (E) and particular affirmative (I): E has universality but negation; I has affirmation but particularity. The commentary says E is nobler because universality contributes nobility from multiple aspects, while affirmation contributes only one aspect. Finally, particular negative (O) is lowest with both low aspects.
Order: A > E > I > O.
3
Justify the ranking using the commentator's reasoning
The commentator states: 'The universal is more precise (adbat) and more useful in the sciences, and more specific than the particular, and the more specific is nobler.' Affirmation is one aspect; universality is multiple aspects. Therefore, a universal negative outweighs a particular affirmative.
This applies to choosing premises in a syllogism.
4
Apply the ranking to an Islamic science context
In Kalam, a universal affirmative proposition like 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause' is preferred over a negative or particular one because it provides certainty and comprehensiveness.
Use the example from the lesson's application.

Then he provides a detailed comparison of the four categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) in terms of nobility (sharaf). He states that affirmation is nobler than negation because existence is nobler than non-existence, and universality is nobler than particularity because it is more precise and beneficial. Thus universal affirmative is the noblest, and particular negative is the lowest. Universal negative is nobler than particular affirmative because universality contributes nobility from multiple aspects whereas affirmation contributes only one aspect. Al-'Attar then clarifies the examples for the particular affirmative mood. Throughout, the examples are from the Islamic intellectual tradition: human, animal, body—terms used in kalām and falsafah.

Key Takeaway
The nobility of a proposition is determined by its quality (affirmation > negation) and quantity (universal > particular), with universality having greater weight than affirmation.

Negative Moods of the First Figure and Its Necessary Validity
Commentary on the negative moods and necessary validity of the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate the two negative moods of the first figure (Celarent and Ferio) and explain why its validity is considered self-evident (ḍarūrī).

Core Idea
The first figure's validity is self-evident; other figures need proof.

You already know the first figure produces affirmative conclusions. Now we explore its negative moods. The commentator emphasizes that this figure's productivity is necessary (ḍarūrī) – it needs no external proof, unlike other figures which require reductio or conversion. We'll walk through the two negative moods with concrete examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two negative moods
The passage says the first figure produces the two negatives, universal and particular. Note the conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative (universal or particular) and the major premise must be a universal negative.
Universal negative mood: minor A, major E → conclusion E. Particular negative mood: minor I, major E → conclusion O.
2
Extract the abstract forms
Write the schemas exactly as the text does: Every J is B, No B is A → No J is A. Some J is B, No B is A → Some J is not A.
These are Celarent and Ferio in traditional nomenclature.
3
Test with Islamic examples
The commentary uses human/animal/stone. Replace with a fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is haram, No haram is permissible → No intoxicant is permissible.' This fits the universal negative mood.
Ensure the middle term (intoxicant?) Actually, here intoxicant is the minor term? Example: Every nabidh is an intoxicant (minor), No intoxicant is permissible (major) → No nabidh is permissible. Middle term: intoxicant.
4
Understand why first figure is self-evident
The commentator says 'ḍarūrī' – the conclusion follows immediately because the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor, creating a direct connection. Other figures lack this immediacy.
This contrasts with second figure where the middle term is predicate in both premises, requiring conversion or reductio.
5
Compare with other figures
The text mentions that other figures require reductio (khulf) or conversion (ʿaks al-kubrā, ʿaks al-tartīb). In the first figure, no such devices are needed; the validity is intrinsic.
This highlights the primacy of the first figure in the logical system.

Section on the syllogism. The first produces the two affirmatives, universal and particular, and the second produces the two negatives, universal and particular. (By necessity) is attached to his saying 'to produce'; i.e., production in this figure is necessary, needing no proof, unlike other figures, for production in them is either by way of reductio or other, as will come. And the detail of his saying 'with the negative' is: that the universal affirmative minor with the universal negative major produces a universal negative, as in our saying: Every J is B, and no B is A, so no J is A. And the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major produces a particular negative, as in our saying: Some J is B, and no B is A, so some J is not A. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: other) i.e., remaining. (His saying: And the detail of his saying) i.e., the author, it is a subject; its predicate is his saying: (that the minor... etc.), and his saying: (produces universal negative) is the predicate of (that). (His saying: Every J is B) i.e., every human is an animal, and his saying: (and no B is A) i.e., no animal is a stone, and his saying: (so no J is A) i.e., no human is a stone. (His saying: and the particular affirmative minor) is a conjunction to his saying: (that the minor... etc.): produces a particular negative; i.e., because you know that the conclusion follows the weaker. (His saying: Some J is B) i.e., some human is an animal, and his saying: (and no B is A) i.e., no animal is a stone, and his saying: (so some J is not A) i.e., some human is not a stone. Al-'Attar. (His saying: necessary) i.e., self-evident, needing no proof. (His saying: or other) such as conversion of the major or reversal of order.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the negative moods of the first figure and the claim that the first figure's productivity is necessary (darurī) and self-evident, unlike other figures which require proof such as reductio (khulf) or conversion. The text begins with a section heading 'On the Syllogism'. It states that the first figure produces the two affirmatives (universal and particular) and also the two negatives (universal and particular) when combined with a negative major. The commentary clarifies that the production (intāj) in the first figure is necessary, needing no external proof, contrasting with other figures which require devices like reductio ad absurdum or conversion. Then it details the negative moods: (1) universal affirmative minor with universal negative major yields universal negative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone; therefore no human is a stone'), and (2) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major yields particular negative (e.g., 'Some human is an animal; no animal is a stone; therefore some human is not a stone'). Al-Dasuqi explains the examples using human, animal, and stone—terms from the Islamic sciences. He also notes that the word 'other' (sā'ir) means 'remaining', and provides grammatical analysis. Al-'Attar clarifies that 'necessary' means self-evident (badīhī) and that 'or other' refers to methods like conversion of the major or reversing the order of premises. The passage reinforces that the first figure is the most perfect because its validity is immediately obvious.

The First Figure's Productive Moods and Their Necessity
Author enumerates the four productive moods of the first figure and illustrates the two negative moods with variable examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and validate their conclusions using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
The first figure yields conclusions by necessity without conversion or reductio.

In this passage, the author explains that the first figure of the categorical syllogism produces four types of conclusions: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. The key property is that this production is necessary (bi al-darura) and does not require additional proof, unlike other figures. The passage then illustrates the two negative moods with variable examples. Understanding these moods is foundational for assessing arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main modes
The passage states: 'the first produces the two affirmatives, universal and particular, and the second produces the two negatives, universal and particular.' Here 'first' and 'second' refer to the two modes of the first figure: the first mode has both premises affirmative, yielding affirmative conclusions; the second mode has a negative premise (the major) yielding negative conclusions.
The first mode corresponds to Barbara (AAA) and Darii (AII); the second to Celarent (EAE) and Ferio (EIO).
2
Understand the necessity claim
The author says production in the first figure is 'by necessity' (bi al-darura). This means the conclusion follows directly from the premises without needing conversion, reductio, or any external proof. This self-evident validity is unique to the first figure.
Other figures may require conversion of premises or reductio ad absurdum to prove validity.
3
Analyze the universal negative example
The author gives: 'Every J is B, and no B is A, so no J is A.' Here the minor premise is universal affirmative, the major is universal negative, and the conclusion is universal negative. This is the Celarent mood (EAE).
The variables J, B, A are placeholders for terms. In the sharh, they are replaced with concrete terms: J = human, B = animal, A = stone.
4
Analyze the particular negative example
The author gives: 'Some J is B, and no B is A, so some J is not A.' Here the minor is particular affirmative, the major universal negative, and the conclusion particular negative. This is the Ferio mood (EIO).
Both examples show that the quality and quantity of the conclusion follow the major and minor respectively.
5
Substitute concrete Islamic terms
Replace the variables with terms from Islamic sciences: J = 'human', B = 'animal', A = 'stone'. Check that the syllogism remains valid: 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone; therefore no human is a stone.' This demonstrates the practical use of the first figure.
These terms are standard in Islamic logic textbooks. You can later apply the same structure to fiqh or kalam arguments.

(بالضَّرُورَةِ) متعلّق بقولِهِ : «لينتج» ؛ أي: الإنتاج في هذا الشكل ضروري لا يحتاج إلى دليل، بخلافِ سائر الأشكال، فإنَّ الإنتاج فيها إما بواسطةِ الخُلْفِ أو غيره، كما سيجيء. وتفصيلُ قَوْلِهِ : مع السَّالبةِ السَّالبتين: أنَّ الصُّغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية تنتج سالبة كلية، كقولنا : كل ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، فلا شيء من ج أ . والصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية، تنتج سالبة جزئية، كقولنا : بعض ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، فبعض ج ليس أ .

'By necessity' is attached to his statement 'to produce': i.e., production in this figure is necessary, not requiring a proof, unlike the other figures, for production in them is either by means of reductio or other, as will come. Commentary
This passage is a definitional and illustrative explanation of the productive moods of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating that the first figure produces two affirmative conclusions (universal and particular) and two negative conclusions (universal and particular). The word 'first' refers to the first figure, and 'second' to the second mood within that figure? Actually, 'al-awwal' and 'al-thani' here likely refer to the two modes of the first figure: the first mode with both premises affirmative, and the second mode with one negative. Conditions and Methods for Productive Moods in the First Figure
Conditions for first figure's moods and two methods for determining productive combinations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the first figure's moods and apply both the method of elimination and the method of acquisition to determine the four productive moods.

Core Idea
Only four out of sixteen premise combinations yield conclusions in the first figure, determined by two conditions.

The first figure is the most intuitive syllogistic form, but not every combination of premises produces a valid conclusion. The commentators explain two key conditions—the minor must be affirmative and the major universal—that reduce possible productive moods to exactly four. They also present two complementary methods to identify these moods: elimination of invalid combinations and direct multiplication of permissible types. Along the way, a linguistic debate arises over the proper Arabic participle for 'productive', which the author resolves using classical lexicography.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for minor and major premises
From the text, extract the two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular), and the major premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative). These conditions are essential for the first figure to produce a necessary conclusion.
Note that the minor cannot be negative, and the major cannot be particular.
2
Apply the method of elimination (tariq al-hadhf)
Start with all 16 possible premise combinations (2 types of minors × 2 types of majors × 4 quantifiers: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). Eliminate combinations that violate the two conditions. The text says requiring the minor to be affirmative eliminates 8 (all negatives), and requiring the major to be universal eliminates 4 more (particular majors), leaving 4 valid moods.
The 16 combinations include both affirmative and negative for each premise type.
3
Apply the method of acquisition (tariq al-tahsil)
Directly multiply the permissible minor types (universal affirmative and particular affirmative) by the permissible major types (universal affirmative and universal negative). 2 × 2 = 4, yielding the four productive moods: (1) universal affirmative + universal affirmative, (2) universal affirmative + universal negative, (3) particular affirmative + universal affirmative, (4) particular affirmative + universal negative.
This method confirms the result of elimination more efficiently.
4
Evaluate the linguistic critique of 'al-muntija'
The passage includes a debate: al-‘Isam objects that the active participle 'muntija' is incorrect because the verb 'nataja' is used only passively. However, ‘Abd al-Hakim refutes this by citing the classical dictionary 'Shams al-‘Ulum' to show transitive usages. Understand that the author ultimately accepts 'muntija' as valid, which is how later texts use it.
This shows that logical terminology can be refined through linguistic analysis, but the technical meaning remains stable.

Al-‘Attar: His statement 'by necessity' means self-evident, needing no proof. His statement 'or other' like conversion of the major or reversal of order. The summary: That the minor in this figure is only affirmative, whether universal or particular, and the major is only universal, whether affirmative or negative. So the productive moods become four, resulting from multiplying the two affirmative minors by the two universal majors, but the logical division requires [more]. Dusuqi: His statement 'in this figure' means the first. His statement 'the two affirmative minors' means the universal affirmative and the particular affirmative. His statement 'the two universal majors' means the universal negative and the universal affirmative. His statement 'but the logical division requires' means the mental division. Al-‘Attar: His statement 'but the logical division' is a correction of his statement 'so the productive moods become four'. Al-‘Isam said: The verb 'nataja' is only used in the passive voice, so the linguistically correct term is 'al-mantuj' (produced), not 'al-natija' or 'al-muntija' as active participle; because the transmitted usage is 'antaj al-naqata ahluha' (the owners made the she-camel give birth), so the moods are things that thought produces, not that they themselves produce something, unless we say that the moods and figures make the premises have results. And ‘Abd al-Hakim refuted him using Shams al-‘Ulum: 'Natajat al-naqatu natajan wa natajan, wa natajaha ahluha' if they attend to her to give birth, it is used transitively and intransitively, and 'antajat al-farasu' if her time of birth has come, and it is said 'antajat' meaning 'natajat'. So what was said that linguists do not accept the use of 'al-natija' is refuted. Then for them in distinguishing the sterile from the productive there are two ways: one is the method of elimination, for requiring the minor to be affirmative eliminates eight combinations resulting from multiplying the two negatives by the four quantified majors, and requiring the major to be universal eliminates four others, namely the particular affirmative major and the particular negative major with the two affirmatives. The second is the method of acquisition: the affirmative minor is either universal or particular, and the universal major is either affirmative or negative, and multiplying two by two gives four. The commentator indicated the method of acquisition by his statement 'so its productive moods become four' and the method of elimination by his statement 'except'.
Source pages p. 795

Commentary
This passage is a layered commentary combining expansion and critical refinement. It begins with al-‘Attar explaining the meaning of 'by necessity' as self-evident and 'other' as reference to conversion or reversal of order. Then the main text (al-Hasil) summarizes the condition for the first figure: the minor premise must be affirmative (either universal or particular), and the major premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative). Multiplying the two types of minors by the two types of majors yields exactly four productive moods—this is the method of acquisition (tariq al-tahsil). The commentator Dusuqi clarifies that 'the two affirmative minors' are the universal and particular affirmatives, and 'the two universal majors' are the universal negative and universal affirmative. He also explains that 'but the logical division requires' refers to a mental division that might suggest more moods, but the actual productive moods are only four. Then al-‘Attar enters into a linguistic critique: he quotes al-‘Isam objecting to the active participle 'al-muntija' (productive) for the moods, arguing that the verb 'nataja' is only used in the passive form, so the correct term is 'al-mantuj' (produced). He mentions that some use 'antaj' transitively, but al-‘Attar refutes this with a rebuttal from 'Abd al-Hakim citing the classical dictionary Shams al-‘Ulum to show that both usages are attested. Finally, al-‘Attar explains the two methods for identifying the productive moods: the method of elimination (tariq al-hadhf) which starts with all possible combinations (16 by some count) and eliminates those that violate the conditions (all minors must be affirmative, all majors universal), leaving four; and the method of acquisition (tariq al-tahsil) which directly multiplies the two types of minors by the two types of majors. The commentator (al-Sharh) is said to have indicated the method of acquisition by his statement 'so the productive moods become four' and the method of elimination by his statement 'except' (alluding to the continuation not included here).

طَرِيق الحَذْف
طَرِيق التَّحْصِيل

Productive Moods of the First Figure
Author states conditions and enumerates four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure and verify them using the conditions of affirmative minor and universal major.

Core Idea
Only four moods are valid in the first figure: those with an affirmative minor and a universal major.

The first figure of categorical syllogism is the most direct and powerful form. But not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. In this passage, the author lays down the two conditions that limit the possibilities to just four productive moods. Mastering these conditions will allow you to construct and evaluate syllogisms with confidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the passage, note that the minor premise must be affirmative (whether universal or particular) and the major premise must be universal (whether affirmative or negative). These are the only constraints.
2
List the possible minors and majors
There are two types of minors that satisfy the condition: universal affirmative (A) and particular affirmative (I). There are two types of majors that satisfy the condition: universal affirmative (A) and universal negative (E).
3
Generate the four combinations
Multiply the two minors by the two majors: minor A with major A, minor A with major E, minor I with major A, minor I with major E. These are the only productive moods.
4
Match to classical mood names
The four combinations correspond to Barbara (AAA), Celarent (EAE), Darii (AII), and Ferio (EIO). Learn these names as they are standard.
5
Understand why other combinations fail
The author mentions that without conditions there would be 16 combinations. The condition of affirmative minor eliminates 8 (where minor is negative). The condition of universal major eliminates 4 (where major is particular). Thus only 4 remain.

والحاصلُ : أنَّ الصُّغرى في هذا الشكل لا تكونُ إِلَّا موجبةٌ أعم من أن تكونَ كلّيَّةً أو جزئيَّةٌ والكبرى لا تكونُ إلَّا كلِّيَّةٌ أعم من أن تكونَ موجبةً أو سالبةٌ . فتكونُ الضُّروبُ المنتجة أربعة حاصلة من ضرب الصُّغريين الموجبتَينِ في الكُبريين الكليتين، لكن القياس يقتضي فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ ستَّةَ عشرَ ضرباً؛ حاصلةٌ من ضرب الصُّغريات المحصوراتِ الأربع في الكبريات المحصوراتِ الأربع. إلَّا أنَّ اشتراط إيجاب الصُّغرى أسقط ثمانية؛ حاصلةٌ من ضرب الصغريين السالبتين
The conclusion: the minor premise in this figure can only be affirmative, whether universal or particular, and the major premise can only be universal, whether affirmative or negative. So the productive moods are four, resulting from multiplying the two affirmative minors by the two universal majors, but the syllogism requires... Chapter on syllogism: Sixteen types result from multiplying the four enumerated minors by the four enumerated majors. Except that the condition of affirmative minor removes eight, resulting from multiplying the two negative minors.
Source pages p. 796

Commentary
This passage classifies the valid moods of the first figure of categorical syllogism. The author explains that in the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative (whether universal or particular), and the major premise must be universal (whether affirmative or negative). Given these conditions, there are exactly four productive moods: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major (AAA), (2) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major (EAE), (3) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major (AII), (4) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major (EIO). The text then mentions that without these conditions, there would be 16 possible combinations of the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) for both premises, but the condition of affirmative minor eliminates the eight combinations where the minor is negative, and the condition of universal major eliminates the four where the major is particular (both affirmative and negative), leaving four. This is standard Aristotelian logic as taught in Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
الصغرى (al-ṣughrā): The minor premise in a syllogism, which contains the subject of the conclusion.
الكبرى (al-kubrā): The major premise in a syllogism, which contains the predicate of the conclusion.
شكل (shakl): Figure; the arrangement of the middle term in the premises. Here, the first figure where the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
ضرب (ḍarb): Mood; a specific combination of quantity and quality of the two premises.
موجبة (mūjiba): Affirmative proposition; one that affirms the predicate of the subject.
سالبة (sālba): Negative proposition; one that denies the predicate of the subject.
كلية (kulliyya): Universal proposition; one that applies to all individuals of the subject.
جزئية (juz'iyya): Particular proposition; one that applies to some individuals of the subject.
منتجة (muntija): Productive; a mood that yields a valid conclusion. [Note: Some authors argue that the active participle is incorrect and should be passive (muntaj), but this text uses the active form.]

C) It must be negative.
D) It must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The first figure has exactly four productive moods because the minor must be affirmative and the major universal; any other combination is invalid.

Reducing the Sixteen Moods to Four in the First Figure
Author enumerates the possible moods of the first figure and explains the restrictions that yield only four productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the number of possible moods in the first figure and identify which four moods are productive given the conditions of affirmative minor and universal major.

Core Idea
Only four moods in the first figure produce certainty because the minor must be affirmative and the major universal.

This passage from Tadhheeb al-Mantiq introduces the foundational reduction of moods in the first figure. Mastering this reduction is your key to validating countless arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. We will walk through how the initial 16 combinations are narrowed down to the four that actually work.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the four types of quantified premises
Each premise (minor and major) can be universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Write them out: A, E, I, O (standard notation).
Indefinite and individual premises are treated as particular and universal respectively, as per the commentary.
2
Calculate the total combinations before restrictions
Multiply the 4 types of minor by the 4 types of major: 4 × 4 = 16 possible moods. Confirm this matches the matn's starting point.
3
Apply the condition: minor must be affirmative
Eliminate all moods where the minor is negative (E or O). That removes 2 minor types × 4 majors = 8 moods. Now 8 remain.
Affirmative minors are A and I.
4
Apply the condition: major must be universal
From the remaining 8, eliminate moods where the major is particular (I or O). That removes 2 major types × the 2 affirmative minors = 4 moods. Now 4 remain.
Universal majors are A and E.
5
List the four productive moods
The remaining moods are: AAA, AII, EAE, EIO. Write them out and verify with examples from the tradition (e.g., Barbara, Celarent, Darii, Ferio).
These are the only valid first-figure moods in classical logic.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ. ستَّةَ عشرَ ضرباً؛ حاصلةٌ من ضرب الصُّغريات المحصوراتِ الأربع في الكبريات المحصوراتِ الأربع. إلَّا أنَّ اشتراط إيجاب الصُّغرى أسقط ثمانية؛ حاصلةٌ من ضرب الصغريين السالبتين في الكبريات الأربع، واشتراط كلّية الكبرى، أسقط أربعة حاصلة، ضرب الكبريين الجزئيتين في الصغريين الموجبتين . فبقيتِ الضُّروبُ المنتجة أربعة والأمثلة مذكورة. قال الدسوقي: (قوله: ستة عشر ضرباً) أي نوعاً ففيه: أن القياس يقتضي أربعة وستين، وذلك لأن الصغرى إما كلية، أو جزئية، أو مهملة، أو شخصية، وعلى كل إما موجبة أو سالبة. والحاصل من ضرب أربعة في اثنين ثمانية، وكذا يقال في الكبرى، والحاصل من ضرب ثمانية الصغرى في ثمانية الكبرى أربعة وستون. وأجيب: بأن المهملة ترجع إلى الجزئية لأنها في قوتها، والشخصية ترجع إلى الكلية بدليل إنتاجها في الشكل الأول إذا كانت كبرى، كما إذا قيل: هذا زيد، وزيد إنسان، ينتج: هذا إنسان، كما ذكره شيخ الإسلام على إيساغوجي، فعلم أن القياس يقتضي (قوله: الصغريات المحصورات) أي المسورات، وذلك لأن الصغرى إما موجبة أو سالبة، وعلى كل إما أن تكون كلية أو جزئية، فهذه أربعة، والكبرى كذلك. (قوله: الصالبتين) أي: السالبة الكلية، والسالبة الجزئية.
Chapter: On Syllogism. Sixteen moods, resulting from multiplying the four quantified minors by the four quantified majors. However, the condition of the minor being affirmative eliminates eight, resulting from multiplying the two negative minors by the four majors, and the condition of the major being universal eliminates four, resulting from multiplying the two particular majors by the two affirmative minors. So the productive moods remain four, and examples are mentioned. Al-Dasuqi said: (His saying: sixteen moods) i.e., types. In it: that the syllogism would require sixty-four, because the minor is either universal, particular, indefinite, or individual, and in each case either affirmative or negative. The product of multiplying four by two is eight, and likewise for the major, and the product of multiplying eight minors by eight majors is sixty-four. The answer: that the indefinite reverts to the particular because it is in its power, and the individual reverts to the universal as evidenced by its productivity in the first figure when it is the major, as when it is said: This is Zayd, and Zayd is a man, so it produces: This is a man, as mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam on Isaghuji. So one knows that the syllogism requires (his saying: the quantified minors) i.e., the quantified, because the minor is either affirmative or negative, and in each case either universal or particular, so these are four, and likewise the major. (His saying: the two negatives) i.e., the universal negative and the particular negative.
Source pages p. 797

Commentary
This passage enumerates the moods of the first figure. The matn states that there are sixteen moods from combining the four types of minor premises (affirmative universal, affirmative particular, negative universal, negative particular) with the four types of major premises. However, two restrictions reduce them: the minor must be affirmative, eliminating the two negative minors (eight moods gone), and the major must be universal, eliminating the two particular majors (four more gone). Thus only four moods remain: AAA, AII, EAE, EIO. The commentary by Dasuqi explains why we start with sixteen and not sixty-four: indefinite and individual propositions are reduced to particular and universal respectively. For example, 'Zayd is a man' is an individual premise that behaves like a universal; 'Some man is white' covers indefinite. The example 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is a man, therefore this is a man' shows an individual premise in the major producing a conclusion. Thus the only quantified types are universal and particular, each affirmative and negative, giving four types per premise.

Conditions for Productive Second Figure Syllogisms
Author states the two conditions for validity in the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical syllogism in the second figure is valid by checking the two conditions: difference in quality and universality of the major premise.

Core Idea
The second figure requires a difference in quality and a universal major to produce a necessary conclusion.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms has specific constraints that distinguish it from the first. The author presents two conditions for a syllogism to be productive (منتج) in this figure: premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. The Attar commentary explains why violating these conditions leads to sterility, using examples that illustrate the logical necessity behind these rules. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions from the matn
Read the Arabic text and extract the two conditions: (1) the premises must differ in quality (ikhtilāfuhumā), and (2) the major premise must be universal (kulliyyat al-kubrā).
2
Understand why difference in quality is necessary
Read the commentary: if both premises are affirmative, the middle term is not distributed, so no necessary conclusion follows. Example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every speaker is an animal' – the conclusion could be either affirmative or negative. Similarly, if both premises are negative, no conclusion follows.
The commentator uses the term 'uqm' (sterility) to describe the failure to produce a necessary conclusion.
3
Apply the universality condition
The major premise must be universal to ensure the major term is properly distributed. If it were particular, the middle term might not connect the extremes, causing sterility.
For example, a major like 'Some intoxicant is forbidden' would not allow a valid inference.
4
Test a valid second figure syllogism
Construct a valid example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major, universal affirmative) and Some drink is not an intoxicant (minor, particular negative).' This satisfies both conditions (difference in quality and universal major) and is valid (Baroco mood).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ شروط الشكل الثاني وضروبه المنتجة]: (وَ) يُشترَطُ (فِي) الشَّكل (الثَّاني) : (في من ١ . بحسَبِ الكيفيَّة ( اخْتِلَافُهُما)؛ أي: اختلافُ الصُّغرى والكبرى الكَيْفِ بأن تكونَ إحداهما موجبةً، والأخرى سالبةٌ . ۲. (و) بحسب الكمّيَّةِ (كُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى) بأن يكون موضوعُها كُلِّيا. قال العطار: (قوله: أي اختلاف الصغرى والكبرى) يعني: أن اختلافهما في الكيف شرط إنتاج الشكل الثاني، إذ لو اتفقنا فيه؛ لزم الاختلاف الموجب للعقم؛ لأن الاختلاف يدل على أن النتيجة ليست لازمة لذات القياس؛ لاستحالة اختلاف مقتضى الذات، أما عند إيجاب المقدمتين; فكقولنا: كل إنسان حيوان وكل ناطق أو فرس حيوان، والحق في الأولى: الإيجاب، وفي الثانية: السلب، وأما عند سلبهم، فكقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولا شيء من الفرس أو من الناطق بحجر، والحق في الأولى: السلب، وفي الثانية: الإيجاب، وهذا موجب لعقم القياس وعدم اطراده. (قوله: وبحسب الكمية كلية الكبرى إذ لو كانت جزئية; فهي إما أن تكون...).
[Conditions of the Second Figure and Its Productive Moods]: (And) it is required (in) the second figure: (In terms of quality) their difference; i.e., the difference of the minor and major in quality, by one being affirmative and the other negative. (And) in terms of quantity, the universality of the major, i.e., its subject being universal. Al-Attar said: (His saying: i.e., the difference of the minor and major) means that their difference in quality is a condition for the productivity of the second figure, because if they agreed in it, there would necessarily be a difference causing sterility; because the difference indicates that the conclusion is not necessary from the essence of the syllogism, due to the impossibility of the difference of the requirement of the essence. As for when both premises are affirmative, as if we say: Every human is an animal and every speaker or horse is an animal – the truth in the first is affirmative, and in the second is negative. And as for when both are negative, as if we say: Nothing of humanity is a stone, and nothing of the horse or the speaker is a stone – the truth in the first is negative, and in the second is affirmative. And this causes sterility of the syllogism and lack of consistency. (His saying: And in terms of quantity, the universality of the major, because if it were particular, then it would either be...)
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for a valid syllogism in the second figure. The matn states two conditions: (1) the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal. The Attar commentary explains the first condition: if both premises are affirmative, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every speaker is an animal', the conclusion could be either affirmative or negative, so no necessary conclusion follows. Similarly, if both are negative, no conclusion follows. The difference in quality forces the middle term to be distributed in one premise, allowing a valid inference. The second condition ensures that the major term covers all individuals; if the major is particular, the middle term might not be distributed correctly. For example, in fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (universal major) and 'Some nabidh is not intoxicating' (negative minor) yields a valid conclusion; but if the major were 'Some intoxicant is forbidden', no valid conclusion would follow.

Key Takeaway
The second figure yields a necessary conclusion only if the premises differ in quality and the major premise is universal.

Conditions of the Second Figure: Quality and Quantity
Author states and explains the two conditions for productive moods in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two conditions for productive moods in the second figure: difference in quality and universality of the major premise.

Core Idea
The second figure requires one affirmative, one negative premise, and a universal major.

In categorical syllogisms, the second figure (where the middle term is predicate in both premises) has specific conditions for productivity. This passage from the logical tradition clarifies that the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. Understanding these conditions ensures you can determine valid moods in the second figure and avoid barren syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find the two conditions explicitly stated in the text: اختلافهما (difference in quality) and كلية الكبرى (universality of the major).
2
Understand the quality condition
The condition requires one premise affirmative and the other negative. If both were affirmative or both negative, the syllogism would be barren, producing no necessary conclusion.
Al-Attar explains that agreement in quality leads to a difference in truth values that prevents a necessary relation.
3
Analyze the examples of barren syllogisms
Look at the examples: both affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational being is an animal') and both negative (e.g., 'No human is a stone' and 'No horse is a stone'). Note that no valid conclusion follows.
The middle term is predicate in both premises, and the extremes are not linked.
4
Understand the quantity condition
The major premise must be universal, meaning its subject is taken universally and quantified with a universal quantifier. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that even if the subject term is universal in itself, the premise must be explicitly universal.
For example, 'Some humans are animals' is not allowed even though 'human' is a universal term.
5
Apply the conditions
Given a second-figure syllogism, check: (1) do the premises differ in quality? (2) is the major premise universal? If not, the syllogism is invalid.

[ شروط الشكل الثاني وضروبه المنتجة]: (وَ) يُشترَطُ (فِي) الشَّكل (الثَّانِي) : بحسَبِ الكيفيَّة ( اخْتِلَافُهُما)؛ أي: اختلافُ الصُّغرى والكبرى في الكَيْفِ بأن تكونَ إحداهما موجبةً، والأخرى سالبةٌ . (و) بحسب الكمّيَّةِ (كُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى) بأن يكون موضوعُها كُلِّيا. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فِي الْكُبْرَيَاتِ الْأَرْبَعِ) أي: الموجبة والسالبة؛ كلية كانت أو جزئية. (قَوْلُهُ : الْكُبْرَيَيْن الْجُزْئِيَّتَين) أي : الموجبة الجزئية، والسالبة الجزئية. (قَوْلُهُ : الْمُوْجِبَتَيْن) أي : الموجبة الكلية، والموجبة الجزئية. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْأَمْيِلَةُ) أي : أمثلةُ النَّتج . (قَوْلُهُ : مَذْكُورَةٌ) أي : فيما سبق . (قَوْلُهُ : بِأَنْ يَكُوْنَ مَوْضُوْعُهَا كُلِّيَّا) ومُسوَّراً بالسُّورِ الكلّي أيضاً؛ لأنَّنا لو قلنا الكُبرى : وبعضُ الإنسان حيوان؛ لا يصح، مع أنَّ موضوعها وهو الإنسانُ؛ كُلِّيّ؛ العطار (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ اخْتِلَافُ الصُّغْرَى وَالْكُبْرَى) يعني : أنَّ اختلافهما في الكيف شرط إنتاج الشَّكلِ الثَّاني، إِذْ لَو اتَّفَقْنَا فِيهِ؛ لَزِمَ الاختلاف الموجب للعقم؛ لأنَّ الاختلاف يدلُّ على أنَّ النَّتيجةَ ليست لازمةٌ لِذاتِ القياس ؛ لاستحالةِ اختلافِ مُقتضَى الذَّاتِ، أمَّا عند إيجاب المقدِّمتَينِ ؛ فَكَقولنا : كُلُّ إنسان حيوان وكُلُّ ناطق أو فرس حيوان، والحقُّ في الأولى : الإيجاب، وفي الثَّانيةِ : السَّلْبُ، وأمَّا عندَ سَلْبِهمَا ، فَكَقَولِنَا : لَا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بحجرٍ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الفرس أَوْ مِنَ النَّاطِقِ بِحَجَرٍ، والحقُّ في الأَوْلَى : السَّلْبُ، وَفي الثَّانية : الإيجاب، وهذا موجب لعقمِ القياس وعدمِ اطَرادِهِ . (قَوْلُهُ : وَبِحَسَبِ الْكَمِّيَّةِ كُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى إذ لو كانَتْ جزئيَّةٌ ; فَهِيَ إِمَّا أَنْ تَكونَ
[Conditions of the second figure and its productive moods]: (And) it is required (in) the (second) figure: (1) with respect to quality (their difference); i.e., the difference of the minor and major in quality, such that one of them is affirmative and the other negative. (2) And with respect to quantity, (universality of the major); i.e., that its subject be universal. al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'in the four major premises') i.e., affirmative and negative, whether universal or particular. (His saying: 'the two particular major premises') i.e., the affirmative particular and the negative particular. (His saying: 'the two affirmative') i.e., the universal affirmative and the particular affirmative. (His saying: 'and the examples') i.e., examples of the conclusion. (His saying: 'mentioned') i.e., in what preceded. (His saying: 'that its subject be universal') and also quantified with a universal quantifier; because if we said the major: 'Some humans are animals', it is not valid, even though its subject, i.e., human, is universal. al-Attar: (His saying: 'i.e., the difference of the minor and major') means that their difference in quality is a condition for the productivity of the second figure, for if they agreed in it, the necessary difference would cause barrenness; because the difference indicates that the conclusion is not necessary by the essence of the syllogism, due to the impossibility of difference in what the essence requires. As for when both premises are affirmative: as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational or horse is an animal', and the truth in the first is affirmation, and in the second is negation. And as for when both are negative: as in our saying: 'No human is a stone' and 'No horse or rational is a stone', and the truth in the first is negation, and in the second is affirmation. This causes barrenness of the syllogism and lack of regularity. (His saying: 'And with respect to quantity, universality of the major') because if it were particular, then it is either
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage defines the two conditions for productive moods in the second figure of categorical syllogism. The first condition is that the two premises differ in quality: one must be affirmative and the other negative. The commentator al-Attar explains why: if both were affirmative or both negative, the syllogism would be barren, producing no necessary conclusion, as shown with examples. For instance, if both premises are affirmative ('Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational/horse is an animal'), there is no valid conclusion because the middle term fails to relate the extremes properly. The second condition is that the major premise must be universal; its subject must be taken universally and quantified with a universal quantifier. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that even if the subject term is universal in itself, the premise must be explicitly universal (e.g., 'All humans are animals', not 'Some humans are animals'). The passage uses standard examples from logic, such as 'human', 'animal', 'stone', and 'horse'.

Key Terms
اختلاف الكيف
كلية الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second figure, if both premises are affirmative, what happens?
A) The syllogism produces a valid conclusion.
B) The syllogism is barren (no necessary conclusion).
C) The conclusion is always negative.
D) The conclusion is always particular.

Key Takeaway
The second figure requires the premises to differ in quality and the major premise to be universal; otherwise the syllogism yields no necessary conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check whether the following second-figure syllogism satisfies both conditions: 'Some humans are animals' (minor) and 'Every rational being is an animal' (major). Identify any violation and explain why the syllogism would be barren.

Understanding the Modality Conditions for the Second Figure
Author states the two modality conditions and derives the number of productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two disjunctive modality conditions required for a valid second-figure syllogism and explain how each condition combines premise types.

Core Idea
Modal validity in the second figure pivots on two disjunctive conditions, not fixed rules.

In this passage, the author presents two conditions that govern which modal premises can produce a valid conclusion in the second figure. These conditions are disjunctive, meaning each condition offers two or three alternative ways to meet it. Understanding these conditions is crucial for determining the number of productive moods in the second figure and for ensuring that modal syllogisms are valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The author states that two conditions are required with respect to modality. Each condition is a disjunctive concept (one of two or three things). Locate the Arabic phrasing for the first condition and the second condition.
The first condition is about the minor being permanent or the major being a convertible universal negative. The second condition involves possible propositions and necessary/conditional propositions.
2
Analyze the first condition's alternatives
The first condition offers two alternatives: either the minor is one of the two 'permanent' propositions (necessary or perpetual), or the major is among the six propositions whose universal negatives convert by simple conversion. Note that 'convertible universal negatives' are propositions like 'No A is B' that can be converted to 'No B is A'.
The text explicitly states 'either with permanence of the minor' or 'the major be among the universal negatives that convert'.
3
Analyze the second condition's alternatives
The second condition offers three alternatives: (1) neither premise is a possible proposition (general or specific), (2) the minor is one of the two possible propositions and the major is absolute necessary or conditional (general or specific), or (3) the major is one of the two possible propositions and the minor is absolute necessary.
This condition restricts the use of possible premises to specific combinations with necessary/conditional premises.
4
Understand the disjunctive nature
Each condition is a logical 'or' – any one of the alternatives suffices. The combination of both conditions further restricts which premise pairs are allowed. Recognize that the conditions do not mandate a single pattern but allow flexibility.
This flexibility is important for counting the productive moods, as different combinations can satisfy the conditions.
5
Connect conditions to counting
The author then derives the number of productive moods (84) by considering all possible premise types that satisfy these conditions. The first condition determines which minors and majors can be paired, and the second condition eliminates some of those pairs.
This step bridges to the next lesson, which focuses on the arithmetic.

وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ، فَيُشْتَرَطُ فِيْهِ شَرْطَانٍ؛ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْن : الشَّرط الأوَّل : أن يكونَ (إِمَّا مَعَ دَوَام الصُّغْرَى) بأن تكونَ الصُّغرى إِمَّا مَعَ دَوَامِ الصُّغْرَى، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ الْكُبْرَى مِنَ السَّوَالبِ الْمُنْعَكِسَةِ. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مُسَوَّرَةً بِالسُّورِ الْكُلِّيِّ) فَالْأَوْلَى أَنْ يَقُولَ الشَّارِحُ : بِأَنْ تَكُونَ مُسَوَّرَةً بِالسُّورِ الْكُلِّيِّ. العطار (قَوْلُهُ: وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ ; فَيُشْتَرَطُ فِيْهِ شَرْطَانٍ ; كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْن) يَعْنِي : أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ أَمْرَانِ ; أَحَدُهُمَا : مَفْهُومٌ مُرَدَّدٌ بَيْنَ كَوْنِ الصُّغْرَى إِحْدَى الدَّائِمَتَيْنِ، وَكَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى مِنَ الْقَضَايَا السِّتِّ الَّتِي تَنْعَكِسُ سَوَالِبُهَا الْكُلِّيَّةُ بِالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي، وَثَانِيهِمَا : مَفْهُومٌ مُرَدَّدٌ بَيْنَ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مُمْكِنَةً عَامَّةً وَلَا خَاصَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ الصُّغْرَى إِحْدَى الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ، وَالْكُبْرَى ضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ أَوْ مَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ أَوْ خَاصَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ الْكُبْرَى إِحْدَى الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ، وَالصُّغْرَى ضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، فَضُرُوبُهُ الْمُنْتِجَةُ بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ : أَرْبَعَةٌ وَثَمَانُونَ؛ حَاصِلَةٌ مِنْ ضَرْبِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ فِي ثَلَاثَةَ عَشَرَ كُبْرَى تَارَةً، وَضَرْبِ سِتِّ كُبْرَيَاتٍ فِي إِحْدَى عَشْرَةَ صُغْرَى تَارَةً أُخْرَى بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَإِسْقَاطِ ثَمَانِيَةٍ مِنْهَا بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطِ الثَّانِي عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ فِي عَدَدِ الْبَسَائِطِ الْمُعْتَبَرَةِ.
And as for modality, two conditions are required in it, each of which is one of two things: The first condition: that it be (either with permanence of the minor) i.e., the minor be one of the two permanent [propositions], or the major be among the universal negatives that convert. al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'because it is not quantified with a universal quantifier') so it is better for the commentator to say: 'that it be quantified with a universal quantifier'. al-Attar: (His saying: 'And as for modality, two conditions are required in it, each of which is one of two things') means: two things are required with respect to modality; one of them is a concept that is disjunctive between the minor being one of the two permanent propositions, and the major being among the six propositions whose universal negatives convert by simple conversion; and the second is a concept that is disjunctive between: nothing from the two premises being general or specific possible, and the minor being one of the two possible propositions and the major being absolute necessary or general or specific conditional, and the major being one of the two possible propositions and the minor being absolute necessary. So its productive moods with respect to modality are eighty-four, resulting from multiplying the two minors by thirteen majors on one occasion, and multiplying six majors by eleven minors on another occasion according to the first condition, and dropping eight of them according to the second condition, as is well-known in the number of considered simples.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage explains the two conditions that govern modal syllogisms in the second figure. The first condition is disjunctive: either the minor premise must be one of the two 'permanent' propositions (i.e., necessary or perpetual), or the major must be among the six propositions whose universal negatives convert by simple conversion (i.e., the so-called 'six' that include necessary, perpetual, etc.). The second condition is also disjunctive: either neither premise is a 'possible' proposition (general or specific), or the minor is one of the two possible propositions and the major is absolute necessary or conditional (general or specific), or the major is one of the two possible propositions and the minor is absolute necessary. Based on these conditions, the total number of productive modal moods in the second figure is calculated as 84. This number results from first combining two possible minors with thirteen possible majors (26 combinations), then separately combining six majors with eleven minors (66 combinations), giving a total of 92 before dropping eight combinations that are excluded by the second condition, leaving 84. The commentator al-Attar clarifies the disjunctive nature of each condition, and al-Dasuqi notes a refinement about quantifying the major premise.

Key Terms
دوام (dawām): Permanence; a modal property of a proposition that holds at all times, often synonymous with necessity. [Note: In modal logic, 'dā'im' is sometimes distinguished from 'ḍarūrī'; here it refers to necessary or perpetual propositions.]
السوالب المنعكسة (al-sawālib al-munʿakisa): Universal negative propositions that convert by simple conversion (e.g., 'No A is B' to 'No B is A'). [Note: Stable.]
ممكنة عامة وخاصة (mumkina ʿāmma wa khāṣṣa): General possible (contingent) and specific possible (possible as opposed to necessary); types of modal propositions. [Note: The distinction between 'general' and 'specific' possible is standard in later logic; earlier logicians sometimes used different classifications.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The minor is permanent.

C) The minor is possible general.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The modality conditions for the second figure are disjunctive, offering multiple valid premise type combinations.

?

?

Calculating the Number of Productive Modal Moods in the Second Figure
Author states the two modality conditions and derives the number of productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the number of productive modal moods in the second figure by applying the two modality conditions and the given numbers of minors and majors.

Core Idea
The 84 productive moods emerge from combining premise types under the two disjunctive conditions.

After stating the modality conditions, the passage shows how they lead to a specific count of 84 productive moods. The calculation involves counting combinations of two types of minors and 13 types of majors (under the first condition) and then 6 types of majors and 11 types of minors (under the first condition), totaling 92 before dropping 8 by the second condition. Understanding this arithmetic reinforces the logical constraints imposed by modal logic and demonstrates how systematic counting works in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the total combinations before conditions
The passage mentions two separate multiplications: 2 minors × 13 majors = 26, and 6 majors × 11 minors = 66. These represent all premise pairs that satisfy the first condition. Add them to get 92 total initial pairs.
The numbers 2, 13, 6, 11 come from the modal classifications of propositions (the 'considereds simples' in the terminology).
2
Apply the first condition implicitly
The first condition already determined which minors and majors can be paired. The 2 minors correspond to the two 'permanent' propositions; the 13 majors include those that are convertible universal negatives plus others. Similarly, the 6 majors and 11 minors come from another subset. These pairs already satisfy the first condition by construction.
The details of why these specific numbers are chosen are beyond this lesson, but they relate to the types of modal propositions.
3
Apply the second condition to eliminate combinations
The second condition further restricts the pairs. It eliminates 8 combinations from the initial 92, leaving 84. The text says 'dropping eight of them according to the second condition'.
The exact 8 eliminated are those where the premises violate the second condition's alternatives (e.g., both premises possible, or impossible combinations).
4
Verify the arithmetic
Compute: 2 × 13 = 26; 6 × 11 = 66; 26 + 66 = 92; 92 - 8 = 84. Confirm that 84 is the final number of productive moods.
This arithmetic is straightforward; the challenge is understanding why the initial numbers are as stated.
5
Reflect on the significance
The number 84 is not arbitrary; it encapsulates all possible valid modal premise pairs in the second figure. Such counting ensures completeness in logical systems, a hallmark of Islamic logical methodology.
This skill of counting valid argument forms is also used in other figures and in evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ، فَيُشْتَرَطُ فِيْهِ شَرْطَانٍ؛ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْن : الشَّرط الأوَّل : أن يكونَ (إِمَّا مَعَ دَوَام الصُّغْرَى) بأن تكونَ الصُّغرى إِمَّا مَعَ دَوَامِ الصُّغْرَى، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ الْكُبْرَى مِنَ السَّوَالبِ الْمُنْعَكِسَةِ. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهَا لَيْسَتْ مُسَوَّرَةً بِالسُّورِ الْكُلِّيِّ) فَالْأَوْلَى أَنْ يَقُولَ الشَّارِحُ : بِأَنْ تَكُونَ مُسَوَّرَةً بِالسُّورِ الْكُلِّيِّ. العطار (قَوْلُهُ: وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ ; فَيُشْتَرَطُ فِيْهِ شَرْطَانٍ ; كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْن) يَعْنِي : أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ أَمْرَانِ ; أَحَدُهُمَا : مَفْهُومٌ مُرَدَّدٌ بَيْنَ كَوْنِ الصُّغْرَى إِحْدَى الدَّائِمَتَيْنِ، وَكَوْنِ الْكُبْرَى مِنَ الْقَضَايَا السِّتِّ الَّتِي تَنْعَكِسُ سَوَالِبُهَا الْكُلِّيَّةُ بِالْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي، وَثَانِيهِمَا : مَفْهُومٌ مُرَدَّدٌ بَيْنَ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ مُمْكِنَةً عَامَّةً وَلَا خَاصَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ الصُّغْرَى إِحْدَى الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ، وَالْكُبْرَى ضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ أَوْ مَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ أَوْ خَاصَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ الْكُبْرَى إِحْدَى الْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ، وَالصُّغْرَى ضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ، فَضُرُوبُهُ الْمُنْتِجَةُ بِحَسَبِ الْجِهَةِ : أَرْبَعَةٌ وَثَمَانُونَ؛ حَاصِلَةٌ مِنْ ضَرْبِ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ فِي ثَلَاثَةَ عَشَرَ كُبْرَى تَارَةً، وَضَرْبِ سِتِّ كُبْرَيَاتٍ فِي إِحْدَى عَشْرَةَ صُغْرَى تَارَةً أُخْرَى بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطِ الْأَوَّلِ، وَإِسْقَاطِ ثَمَانِيَةٍ مِنْهَا بِمُقْتَضَى الشَّرْطِ الثَّانِي عَلَى مَا هُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ فِي عَدَدِ الْبَسَائِطِ الْمُعْتَبَرَةِ.
And as for modality, two conditions are required in it, each of which is one of two things: The first condition: that it be (either with permanence of the minor) i.e., the minor be one of the two permanent [propositions], or the major be among the universal negatives that convert. al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'because it is not quantified with a universal quantifier') so it is better for the commentator to say: 'that it be quantified with a universal quantifier'. al-Attar: (His saying: 'And as for modality, two conditions are required in it, each of which is one of two things') means: two things are required with respect to modality; one of them is a concept that is disjunctive between the minor being one of the two permanent propositions, and the major being among the six propositions whose universal negatives convert by simple conversion; and the second is a concept that is disjunctive between: nothing from the two premises being general or specific possible, and the minor being one of the two possible propositions and the major being absolute necessary or general or specific conditional, and the major being one of the two possible propositions and the minor being absolute necessary. So its productive moods with respect to modality are eighty-four, resulting from multiplying the two minors by thirteen majors on one occasion, and multiplying six majors by eleven minors on another occasion according to the first condition, and dropping eight of them according to the second condition, as is well-known in the number of considered simples.
Source pages p. 798

Commentary
This passage explains the two conditions that govern modal syllogisms in the second figure. The first condition is disjunctive: either the minor premise must be one of the two 'permanent' propositions (i.e., necessary or perpetual), or the major must be among the six propositions whose universal negatives convert by simple conversion (i.e., the so-called 'six' that include necessary, perpetual, etc.). The second condition is also disjunctive: either neither premise is a 'possible' proposition (general or specific), or the minor is one of the two possible propositions and the major is absolute necessary or conditional (general or specific), or the major is one of the two possible propositions and the minor is absolute necessary. Based on these conditions, the total number of productive modal moods in the second figure is calculated as 84. This number results from first combining two possible minors with thirteen possible majors (26 combinations), then separately combining six majors with eleven minors (66 combinations), giving a total of 92 before dropping eight combinations that are excluded by the second condition, leaving 84. The commentator al-Attar clarifies the disjunctive nature of each condition, and al-Dasuqi notes a refinement about quantifying the major premise.

Key Terms
بسائط (basā'iṭ): Simple propositions, the basic modal types considered in the classification (e.g., necessary, perpetual, possible general, possible specific, etc.). [Note: This term is used in the context of counting 'considered simples'.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 92
C) 84
D) 13

Key Takeaway
The count of productive moods is derived by systematically applying modality conditions to reduce the possible premise combinations.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Two Modal Conditions for Productive Syllogisms
Explains the two modal conditions for syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two modal conditions (perpetuity/conversion and possible/necessary/conditioned) to determine whether a given modal syllogism is valid (productive).

Core Idea
Modal syllogisms require two conditions: either the minor is perpetual or the major's negative is convertible; possible premises need a necessary or conditioned partner.

The earlier lesson covered the non-modal conditions for syllogism. Now, we turn to modality. The author states two conditions, each offering alternatives. Understanding when a syllogism with modal premises is valid is crucial for theological proofs. This lesson breaks down the first condition (perpetuity or convertible negative) and the second (possible with necessary/conditioned), clarifying their scope as refined by al-Dasuqi.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two conditions
Note that the first condition applies when no possible (mumkina) premise is present; the second applies when a possible premise is used. Each condition is a disjunction (either-or).
The conditions are not both applied to the same syllogism; they are alternative cases.
2
Apply the first condition
Check whether the minor premise is perpetual (dā'ima) – meaning necessary (ḍarūriyya) or absolute perpetual – OR the major premise is a proposition whose negative is convertible (i.e., the major is one of six types: two perpetuals, two generals, two specials).
The major can be affirmative or negative; 'convertible negatives' means the negative counterpart converts, not that the major is negative.
3
Apply the second condition
If a possible (mumkina) premise is present, it must be paired either with a necessary proposition (absolute) or with a conditioned major (mashrūṭa, general or special).
The necessary includes both essential and descriptive necessity, covering the two conditioned types.
4
Verify with commentator's clarifications
Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'necessary' entails perpetuity, and 'perpetual' is weaker than necessary. The major in the first condition is general, not restricted to negative. This ensures the conditions correctly identify the 84 productive moods.
The phrase 'negative of the major' is a genitive construction; it does not require the major to be negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ . وأما بحسب الجهةِ، فيشترط فيه شرطان؛ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما أحد الأمرين : الشَّرط الأوَّل : أن يكونَ (إِمَّا مَعَ دَوَام الصُّغْرَى) بأن تكونَ الصُّغرى ضروريَّةً أو دائمةً، أَوِ انْعِكَاسِ (سَالِبَةِ الكُبْرَى)، بأن تكونَ الكبرى من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب وهي سيَّة: الدائمتان، والعامتان، والخاصتان . (وَ) الشَّرط الثَّاني : (كَوْنُ الْمُمْكِنَةِ مُستعملة، إِمَّا مَعَ ضَرُورِيَّةٍ، أَوْ كُبْرَى مَشْرُوطَةٍ) عامة، أو خاصَّة. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : ضَرُوْرِيَّةٌ) أي : مُطلقةً؛ أي : والضَّروريَّةُ تَستلزمُ الدَّوام . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ دَائِمَةٌ) أي : مُطلقة، وقولُهُ: (أو دائمة)؛ أي: غير ضرورية. (قَوْلُهُ : سَالِبَةِ الْكُبْرَى) من إضافةِ الصَّفةِ لِلموصوفِ؛ أي: الكُبرى السَّالبة . (قَوْلُهُ: بِأَنْ تَكُوْنَ الْكُبْرَى ... إلخ) أعم من أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة؛ خلافاً لما يتبادرُ من المصنف. (قَوْلُهُ : الْمُنْعَكِسَةِ السَّوَالِبِ أي: القضايا التي سوالبها منعكسة؛ أي: الَّتي يصح عكس سواليها . (قَوْلُهُ: وَالشَّرْطُ الثَّانِي ... إلخ) اعلَمُ أنَّ الأمرين المشتمل عليهما الشَّرطُ بحسب الجهة لم يتواردا على محالّ واحد ؛ إذ أولهما : فيما إذا لم يكن في القياس ممكنة . وثانيهما : فيما إذا كانَ فيهِ مُمكنة، ولؤ قال المصنِّفُ : معَ دوامِ الصُّغرى أو انعكاس سالبة الكبرى؛ حيثُ لا ممكنة، وإلا ؛ فلا بد معها من الضَّرورةِ مُطلقاً، أو كبرى مشروطة؛ لكان أوضح. (قَوْلُهُ : مَعَ ضَرُوْرِيَّةٍ) أي : سواء كانت الضَّرورة فيها ذاتية أو وصفيَّة، فيشمل المشروطتين . انتهى . وفي بعض التقاييدِ قوله: ضروريَّة؛ أي: مُطلقة.
Chapter: On Syllogism. As for according to modality, two conditions are required in it, each one of which is one of two things: The first condition: that it be either with the perpetuity of the minor (i.e., the minor is necessary or perpetual), or with the conversion of the negative of the major (i.e., the major is among the propositions whose negatives are convertible, and they are six: the two perpetuals, the two general [conditionals], and the two special [conditionals]). And the second condition: the possible proposition is used, either with a necessary [proposition] or with a conditioned major, general or special. al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'necessary' means absolute; i.e., the necessary entails perpetuity. His statement: 'or perpetual' means absolute, and his statement: 'or perpetual' i.e., not necessary. His statement: 'negative of the major' is an attribution of the adjective to the described; i.e., the negative major. His statement: 'that the major be... etc.' is more general than it being affirmative or negative, contrary to what appears from the author. His statement: 'the convertible negatives' i.e., the propositions whose negatives are convertible; i.e., it is valid to convert their negatives. His statement: 'and the second condition... etc.' Know that the two things included in the condition according to modality do not apply to a single locus; for the first is when there is no possible [proposition] in the syllogism, and the second is when there is a possible [proposition] in it. If the author had said: 'with the perpetuity of the minor or the conversion of the negative of the major, where there is no possible; otherwise, then with it there must be necessity absolutely, or a conditioned major' it would have been clearer. His statement: 'with a necessary' i.e., whether the necessity therein is essential or descriptive, so it includes the two conditioned [propositions]. End. And in some notes: his statement: 'necessary' i.e., absolute.
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya commentary that refines the conditions for syllogism with respect to modality (jiha). The original sharh text states that there are two conditions, each being one of two alternatives. The first condition is that either the minor premise is perpetual (dā'ima) – meaning it is either necessary (ḍarūriyya) or perpetual – or the major premise is a negative proposition that can be converted (i.e., its negative counterpart is convertible under the simple conversion rule). The major must be one of six types: two perpetuals, two general conditionals, and two special conditionals. The second condition is that a possible proposition (mumkina) must be paired either with a necessary proposition or with a conditioned major (mashrūṭa). The hashiya by al-Dasuqi clarifies key points: 'necessary' here means absolute necessity, which entails perpetuity. 'Perpetual' is distinct from necessary; it is a weaker modality. The phrase 'negative of the major' is a genitive construction where the adjective 'negative' is attributed to 'major'. The major in the first condition can be either affirmative or negative, contrary to what the original author might have implied. 'Convertible negatives' means propositions whose negative statements can be converted (i.e., if 'No S is P' is true, then 'No P is S' is true). The second condition's two alternatives are not both applied simultaneously: the first part applies when no possible proposition is in the syllogism, the second when a possible is present. The commentator suggests that the author could have phrased it more clearly by separating these cases. Finally, the necessary proposition in the second condition includes both essential and descriptive necessity, thus covering the two conditioned types. This refinement ensures that the productive moods are correctly identified, which are traditionally counted as 84, derived from multiplying the possible minors and majors under these conditions.

Key Terms
دوام الصغرى
انعكاس سالبة الكبرى
ممكنة
ضرورية
مشروطة
ضروب منتجة

Key Takeaway
The two modal conditions are disjunctive and apply to different cases: either the minor is perpetual or the major's negative converts (when no possible premise), or a possible premise pairs with a necessary/conditioned major.

Modal Conditions and First Two Moods of the Second Figure
Author gives modal conditions and first two moods of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct modal combinations for possible premises in the second figure and construct the first two moods (Cesare and Camestres).

Core Idea
Possible premises need a modal partner: necessary or conditional, depending on position.

In modal syllogisms, not every combination of premises yields a valid conclusion. This passage lays down the conditions for using a possible premise (mumkina) in the second figure. It then presents the first two valid moods of this figure, which always produce a universal negative conclusion. Understanding these conditions helps you avoid invalid arguments in modal logic, a key tool in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal conditions for possible premises
The passage states: if the possible premise is the minor, it must be paired with a necessary (daruriyya) or a conditional (mashruta) premise. If it is the major, it can only be paired with a necessary premise. Notice the asymmetry: the major has stricter requirements.
A possible premise expresses contingency (e.g., 'it is possible that...'). The conditions ensure that the modality does not break the syllogism.
2
Recognise the first mood (Camestres)
The first mood has a universal affirmative minor and a universal negative major, yielding a universal negative conclusion. In the passage: 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B' therefore 'No J is A'.
This is the AEE mood in the second figure (Camestres).
3
Recognise the second mood (Cesare)
The second mood has a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, also yielding a universal negative conclusion. In the passage: 'No J is B' and 'Every A is B' therefore 'No J is A'.
This is the EAE mood in the second figure (Cesare).
4
Note the universal requirement
Both moods require both premises to be universal. The passage explicitly says 'so that the minor and major (both universal) produce a universal negative'. This is essential for validity in these moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في القياس
فالممكنة إن كانت صغرى؛ لا تستعمل إلا مع ضرورية، أو مشروطة عامة، أو خاصة.
وإن كانت كبرى؛ لا تستعمل إلا مع ضرورية فقط؛ لينتج) الصغرى والكبرى (الكليتان)؛ أي: الموجبة والسالبة (سالبة كلية) كقولنا في الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية: كل (ج) (ب)، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (ج) (أ)، وهذا الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل.
وفي الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية: لا شيء من (ج) (ب)، وكل (أ) (ب)، فلا شيء من (ج) (أ)، وهذا هو الضرب الثاني منه.
Section: On the syllogism.
The possible (premise) if it is a minor, it is only used with a necessary, or a conditional general or specific. And if it is a major, it is only used with a necessary only; so that the minor and major (both universal) produce a universal negative, i.e. the affirmative and the negative (universal negative). Like our saying in the affirmative universal minor with the negative universal major: Every J is B, and no A is B, so no J is A. This is the first mood of this figure. And in the negative universal minor with the affirmative universal major: No J is B, and every A is B, so no J is A. This is the second mood.
Source pages p. 800

Commentary
This passage is from the section on the categorical syllogism (qiyas). It first states the conditions for using a possible (mumkina) premise in a syllogism: if the possible premise is the minor, it must be combined with a necessary (daruriyya) or a conditional (mashruta) premise; if it is the major, it must be combined with a necessary premise only. Then the author presents two valid moods of the second figure. In the first mood, the minor is universal affirmative and the major is universal negative, yielding a universal negative conclusion. For example, 'Every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal, therefore no human is a stone.' The second mood has a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, also producing a universal negative conclusion. For example, 'No inanimate object is an animal, and every human is an animal, therefore no inanimate object is a human.' These two moods are the first two of the second figure, which requires the major premise to be universal and the minor to be either affirmative or negative accordingly. The conditions for possible premises ensure the validity of modal syllogisms.

Key Terms
الممكنة
ضرورية
مشروطة
كل (ج) (ب)

B) A necessary minor

D) A necessary major

Key Takeaway
Possible premises in the second figure require specific modal partners: a necessary or conditional minor, but only a necessary major, and both moods produce a universal negative conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Identify the mood and verify it matches one of the two given in the passage.

The Syllogism
