The Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure and determine the conclusion based on the quantity and quality of the premises.

Core Idea
The second figure yields only negative conclusions: universal when both premises are universal, particular when they differ.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the second figure requires the middle term to be the predicate in both premises. Here, the author enumerates the four valid moods, showing how the combination of universal and particular premises determines the quantity of the negative conclusion. You will learn to recognize each mood by its pattern and apply it to real arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Confirm that the middle term is the predicate in both premises. In the second figure, the middle is the predicate of both the minor and major premises.
The author's examples use أ, ب, ج as variables. Here, ب is the middle term.
2
Check quantity and quality of each premise
Determine if each premise is universal (كل/لا شيء) or particular (بعض/ليس بعض), and affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة).
The four moods arise from combining universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative premises.
3
Apply the rule for conclusion
If both premises are universal, the conclusion is universal negative. If they differ in quantity, the conclusion is particular negative.
The conclusion is always negative because the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
4
Match to traditional mood names
The first mood (universal affirmative minor + universal negative major) is Cesare; the second (universal negative minor + universal affirmative major) is Camestres; the third (particular affirmative minor + universal negative major) is Festino; the fourth (particular negative minor + universal affirmative major) is Baroco.
These names are from the Latin tradition; the author does not use them but they help in memorizing.
5
Verify with an Islamic science example
Take the first mood example from the commentary: 'Every man is an animal; no stone is an animal; so no man is a stone.' Replace with an Islamic legal example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; no nabidh is forbidden; so no intoxicant is nabidh' (if nabidh is not intoxicant). However, ensure the example is correct; a better one: 'Every act of obedience is rewarded; no act of disobedience is rewarded; so no act of obedience is an act of disobedience.'
Always check that the terms are properly ordered and the syllogism is valid.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
فالممكنه إنْ كانَتْ صُغرى؛ لا تُستعمَلُ إلا مع ضروريَّة، أو مشروطة عامة، أو خاصَّة.
وإن كانت كُبرى؛ لا تُستعمَلُ إلا مع ضروريَّة فقط؛ لينتج) الصُّغرى والكبرى (الكُلِّيَّتَانِ)؛ أي: الموجبة والسالبة (سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ) كقولنا في الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية : كلُّ (ج) (ب)، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب) ، فلا شيء من (ج) (أ)) ، وهذا الضّرب الأوَّل من هذا الشكل .
وفي الصُّغرى السَّالبة الكُلِّيَّةِ مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية: «لا شيء من (ج) (ب)، وكلُّ (أ) (ب) ، فلا شيء من (ج) (أ)»، وهذا هو الضَّربُ الثاني منه.
(وَالْمُخْتَلِفَتَانِ فِي الْكَمْ أَيْضَاً سَالِبَةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ)، فقوله: «والمختلفتان» عطفٌ على قولِهِ : «الكلِّيَّتَانِ»، وقولُهُ: «سالبةٌ جزئية» عطف على قوله : سالبة كلّيَّةٌ»، فيكونُ مِن بابِ العطف على معمولي عامل واحدٍ. والحاصلُ : أنَّ الصُّغرى والكبرى إمَّا متَّفقتان في الكم بأنْ يكونَا كليتين، أو مختلفتان في الكم بأن تكون إحداهُمَا كلَّيَّةٌ والأُخرى جزئية. فإنْ كانتا مُتَفقتين ; فالنتيجة سالبة كلّيَّةٌ كما مر ، وإن كانتا مختلفتين; فالنتيجة سالبة جزئيَّةٌ ، كَقولنا فى الصُّغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السَّالبة الكلِّيَّة: «بعض (ج) (ب) ، ولا شيء من (أ) (ب)، فبعض (ج) ليس (أ)»، وهو الضَّربُ الثَّالث.
وفي الصُّغرى السالبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية : «بعض (ج) ليس (ب)، وكلُّ (أ) (ب) ، فبعضُ (ج) ليس (أ)» ، وهو الضَّرب الرَّابع . واعلَمْ أنَّ الضُّروبَ المنتجة من هذا الشَّكل بحسب الواقع أربعة كما ذكرت بأمثلتها ، لكنَّ القياس يقتضي ستَّةَ عشرَ كما ذكرنا في الشَّكل
Chapter: On Syllogism. The possible [premise], if it is the minor, is not used except with a necessary [premise], or a universal conditional, or a particular conditional. And if it is the major, it is not used except with a necessary [premise] only, to produce. The minor and major (the two universals) i.e., the affirmative and negative, [yield] a universal negative, as our saying in the minor universal affirmative with the major universal negative: 'Every C is B, and no A is B, so no C is A.' This is the first mood of this figure. And in the minor universal negative with the major universal affirmative: 'No C is B, and every A is B, so no C is A.' This is the second mood of it. (And the two differing in quantity also [yield] a particular negative.) So his saying 'and the two differing' is conjoined to his saying 'the two universals', and his saying 'a particular negative' is conjoined to his saying 'a universal negative', so it is from the chapter of conjunction upon two objects of one operator. The summary: the minor and major either agree in quantity by being both universal, or differ in quantity by one being universal and the other particular. If they agree, the conclusion is universal negative as mentioned; if they differ, the conclusion is particular negative, as our saying in the minor particular affirmative with the major universal negative: 'Some C is B, and no A is B, so some C is not A.' This is the third mood. And in the minor particular negative with the major universal affirmative: 'Some C is not B, and every A is B, so some C is not A.' This is the fourth mood. Know that the productive moods of this figure in reality are four as I mentioned with their examples, but analogical reasoning requires sixteen as we mentioned in the [previous] figure.
Source pages p. 801

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first notes a rule about possible premises: a possible minor premise combines only with necessary or conditional premises, while a possible major combines only with necessary premises. Then he explains that when both premises are universal (in quantity), the conclusion is universal negative. Two moods result: first, minor universal affirmative with major universal negative (Cesare); second, minor universal negative with major universal affirmative (Camestres). When premises differ in quantity, the conclusion is particular negative. This yields two more moods: third, minor particular affirmative with major universal negative (Festino); fourth, minor particular negative with major universal affirmative (Baroco). The author also notes that out of sixteen possible combinations of premises in the second figure (four moods in each figure?), only these four are productive. The examples use the letters (ج, ب, أ) which are conventionally explained in the margins: e.g., (ج) for man, (ب) for animal, (أ) for stone or rational being. In the first mood: every man is an animal, no stone is an animal, so no man is a stone. In the second: no inanimate is an animal, every man is an animal, so no inanimate is a man. In the third: some animal is a man, no stone is an animal, so some animal is not a stone. In the fourth: some animal is not a man, every rational being is a man, so some animal is not rational.

In a second figure syllogism, the minor premise is 'Some acts of worship are obligatory' (particular affirmative) and the major premise is 'No act of worship is invalid' (universal negative). What is the conclusion?
A) No acts of worship are obligatory.
B) Some acts of worship are not invalid.
C) Every act of worship is obligatory.
D) Some acts of worship are not obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The second figure's four moods all yield negative conclusions, with the quantity (universal or particular) determined by the premises' quantities: universal when both premises are universal, particular when they differ.

The Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Author explains the four productive moods of the second figure and their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure categorical syllogism and explain why only four are valid.

Core Idea
The second figure's productivity is constrained by two key conditions: quality difference and universality of the major.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism initially seems to allow sixteen possible combinations of premises. However, only four yield a necessary conclusion. In this passage, the author explains that two conditions—difference in quality between minor and major, and the requirement that the major be universal—eliminate twelve combinations, leaving the four valid moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
Find in the Arabic where the author states that the minor and major must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major must be universal. These are the filters that reduce the sixteen possibilities.
The conditions are 'ikhtilāf al-ṣughrā wa al-kubrā' and 'kulliyyat al-kubrā'.
2
Apply the conditions to reduce the 16 possibilities
Start from the total 16 combinations (4 qualities × 4 quantifiers). First, subtract 8 because the premises must differ in quality. Then subtract 4 more because the major must be universal. Four moods remain.
This counting is derived from the text: 'ashrata thamāniyah' (eight) and 'arba‘ah' (four).
3
Recognize the conclusion patterns
If both premises are universal, the conclusion is universal negative. If they differ in quantity, the conclusion is particular negative. Verify with the two examples given in the passage.
4
Memorize the two explicit examples
The third mood: Some (ج) is (ب), No (أ) is (ب) → Some (ج) is not (أ). The fourth mood: Some (ج) is not (ب), Every (أ) is (ب) → Some (ج) is not (أ). These are Festino and Baroco respectively.
Note that the author uses Arabic variables (ج، ب، أ) to stand for terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He says: 'both universal' is a conjunction of his statement, and his statement 'particular negative' is a conjunction of his statement 'universal negative', so it is of the type of conjunction on two objects of one agent. The conclusion: the minor and major are either agreeing in quantity such that both are universal, or differing in quantity such that one is universal and the other particular. If they agree, the conclusion is universal negative as passed; if they differ, the conclusion is particular negative, as in our statement in the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major: 'Some (J) is (B), and nothing of (A) is (B), so some (J) is not (A)', which is the third mood. And in the particular negative minor with the universal affirmative major: 'Some (J) is not (B), and every (A) is (B), so some (J) is not (A)', which is the fourth mood. And know that the productive moods of this figure in reality are four as I mentioned with their examples, but the syllogistic schema requires sixteen as we mentioned in the first figure, except that the condition of difference of minor and major eliminated eight, and the condition of universality of the major also eliminated four, so the productive moods remain four. Then these moods only produce by khulf (reductio ad absurdum) or by conversion of the major.
Source pages p. 802

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by explaining a previous statement about the conjunction of terms, then moves to the core: the minor and major premises can either agree in quantity (both universal) or differ (one universal, one particular). If they agree, the conclusion is universal negative; if they differ, the conclusion is particular negative. He provides examples: the third mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The fourth mood has a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, also yielding a particular negative conclusion. The author then clarifies that in reality there are only four productive moods in the second figure, even though the raw combinations of premises (considering quantity and quality) would be sixteen. Two conditions reduce this: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), which eliminates eight; (2) the major premise must be universal, which eliminates another four, leaving four moods. Finally, he states that the validity of these moods is established either by khulf (assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction) or by converting the major premise (changing its subject and predicate). This is a standard exposition of the second figure's moods and their proof methods.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-ḍarb): A mood of a syllogism, i.e., a specific combination of premises based on quantity and quality. [Note: Stable term across logical texts.]
الكم (al-kamm): Quantity of a proposition: whether it is universal (كلّي) or particular (جزئي). [Note: Standard term in logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The second figure has only four productive moods, determined by the conditions that premises differ in quality and the major is universal.

Proving Second Figure Moods by Khulf and Conversion
Author states the proof methods for the second figure moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate how khulf (reductio ad absurdum) and conversion of the major premise prove the validity of second figure syllogisms.

Core Idea
Khulf and conversion are two independent methods that establish the necessity of the conclusion in second figure moods.

The author states that the productive moods of the second figure are proven either by khulf (assuming the contradictory of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction with the premises) or by converting the major premise (changing its subject and predicate). In this lesson, we will work through these proof methods step by step, using one of the example moods from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of khulf (reductio ad absurdum)
To prove a conclusion via khulf, assume its contradictory (the opposite proposition). Combine this assumption with one of the original premises. If this new pair leads to a contradiction with the other premise, then the original conclusion must be true.
This method is mentioned in the passage as 'al-khulf'.
2
Apply khulf to the third mood (Festino)
Take minor: Some (ج) is (ب), major: No (أ) is (ب), conclusion: Some (ج) is not (أ). Assume the contradictory: Every (ج) is (أ). Combine with major: No (أ) is (ب) → No (ج) is (ب). This contradicts minor: Some (ج) is (ب). Therefore the original conclusion holds.
The contradiction arises between the derived universal negative and the original particular affirmative.
3
Understand the conversion method
Convert the major premise (universal negative 'No A is B' becomes 'No B is A'). With this new major, the syllogism becomes a first figure mood (Ferio), which is already known to be valid. Thus the original mood is valid.
Conversion of a universal negative is simple conversion (E → E).
4
Check the applicability of conversion to other moods
For the fourth mood (Baroco), the major is universal affirmative 'Every A is B'. Conversion by limitation yields 'Some B is A'. This converted major with the minor gives a first figure mood (Darii) that is valid. Thus Baroco is valid by conversion.
Conversion of universal affirmative is by limitation (A → I).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He says: 'both universal' is a conjunction of his statement, and his statement 'particular negative' is a conjunction of his statement 'universal negative', so it is of the type of conjunction on two objects of one agent. The conclusion: the minor and major are either agreeing in quantity such that both are universal, or differing in quantity such that one is universal and the other particular. If they agree, the conclusion is universal negative as passed; if they differ, the conclusion is particular negative, as in our statement in the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major: 'Some (J) is (B), and nothing of (A) is (B), so some (J) is not (A)', which is the third mood. Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by explaining a previous statement about the conjunction of terms, then moves to the core: the minor and major premises can either agree in quantity (both universal) or differ (one universal, one particular). If they agree, the conclusion is universal negative; if they differ, the conclusion is particular negative. He provides examples: the third mood has a particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The fourth mood has a particular negative minor and universal affirmative major, also yielding a particular negative conclusion. The author then clarifies that in reality there are only four productive moods in the second figure, even though the raw combinations of premises (considering quantity and quality) would be sixteen. Two conditions reduce this: (1) the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), which eliminates eight; (2) the major premise must be universal, which eliminates another four, leaving four moods. Finally, he states that the validity of these moods is established either by khulf (assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction) or by converting the major premise (changing its subject and predicate). This is a standard exposition of the second figure's moods and their proof methods.

Key Terms
الخلف (al-khulf): Reductio ad absurdum: proving the conclusion by showing its negation leads to a contradiction. [Note: No shift; consistent usage.]
عكس الكبرى (ʿaks al-kubrā): Conversion of the major premise: swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth (with appropriate restrictions). [Note: In this text, it refers to the conversion of a universal affirmative or negative, as only those produce valid results in this context.]

The Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure and Their Proof Methods
Enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure and their proof methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure and determine which proof method (khulf, conversion of the major, or conversion of order plus conversion of the conclusion) applies to each.

Core Idea
Only four moods in the second figure yield conclusions, each requiring a specific proof method.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism is powerful but restrictive. Not all premise combinations produce valid conclusions. The logicians derived that only four moods are productive, and each can be proven using one of three methods: reductio (khulf), conversion of the major, or conversion of order followed by conversion of the conclusion. This passage from the Tadhheeb Sharh explains these moods and their proofs, a critical tool for any scholar of logic needing to validate arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the elimination process
The matn states that two conditions reduce the 16 possible combinations to 4: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. Work through the elimination as al-Dusuqi explains: eight eliminated by quality condition, four more by universality condition.
2
List the four productive moods
Based on the conditions, list the four moods: (1) universal affirmative major with universal negative minor, (2) universal affirmative major with particular negative minor, (3) universal negative major with universal affirmative minor, (4) universal negative major with particular affirmative minor. Note that these correspond to specific combinations.
Al-Attar provides an alternative acquisition method.
3
Identify the three proof methods
The matn mentions three methods: khulf (reductio), conversion of the major premise, and conversion of order followed by conversion of the conclusion. Understand each: khulf assumes the contradictory; conversion of major turns the major into a first figure; conversion of order swaps premises.
Al-Dusuqi clarifies which method applies to which mood.
4
Map methods to moods
Match each method to the specific moods: khulf works for all four; conversion of major works for the first and third moods; conversion of order then conversion of conclusion works only for the second mood. The fourth mood is only provable by khulf.
Al-Attar confirms these assignments.
5
Work through an example
Take the first mood: affirmative universal minor (e.g., 'Every human is rational') and negative universal major (e.g., 'No stone is rational'). Prove the conclusion 'No stone is human' using either khulf or conversion of the major. Repeat for another mood.
Use Islamic examples to internalize the logic.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ الأوّل، إلا أنَّ اشتراط اختلافِ الصُّغرى والكبرى أسقط ثمانية، واشتراط كلّيَّةِ الكبرى أربعةً أيضاً ، فبقيتِ الضُّروبُ المنتجة أربعة . ثمَّ هذه الضُّروبُ إنَّما تنتج بِالْخُلْفِ ، أَوْ عَكْسِ الكُبْرَى ، أَوْ عكس التَّرتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ عكس النَّتِيجَةِ؛ أَمَّا الخُلفُ .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : اخْتِلَافِ الصُّغُرَى ... إلخ) أي: في الكيف. (قَوْلُهُ : ثَمَانِيَةٌ) لأنَّهما إما موجبتان وفيهما أربعة؛ لأنَّ الأُولى إمَّا كلِّيَّةٌ أو جزئية، والثانية كذلك، والحاصل من ضرب اثنين في اثنين؛ أربعة، وإما سالبتان، وفيهما أربعة أيضاً، فالحاصل أربعة في كلّ، فالجملة ثمانية.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَاشْتِرَاط كُلِّيَّةِ الْكُبْرَى أَرْبَعَةً أَيْضاً) أي: لأنَّ الكُبرى إذا لم تكن كُلِّيَّة، بلْ كانت جزئيَّة ؛ فإمَّا موجبة أو سالبة، فإذا كانت موجبةً؛ فالصغرى سالبة وهي؛ أي الصُّغرى : إمَّا كلِّيَّةٌ أو جزئية، فهاتان صورتان، وإذا كانت سالبة؛ فالصغرى موجبةٌ وهي ؛ أي الصُّغرى : إمَّا كلّيَّةٌ أو جزئية، وهاتان صورتان، وحينئذ فالجملة أربعةُ صور . (قَوْلُهُ : فَبَقِيتِ الصُّرُوبُ الْمُنْتِجَةُ أَرْبَعَةً) وذلك لأنَّ الكُبرى الكلية إما موجبة، وحينئذ فالصغرى سالبة، وهي إمَّا كليَّةً أو جزئية، فهاتان صورتان. وإما أن تكونَ الكُبرى كلّيَّةٌ سالبة، وحينئذٍ فَالصُّغرى موجبة، وهي إما كلِّيَّةٌ أو جزئيّة، فهاتان صورتان وحينئذ فالجملة أربعة صور . (قَوْلُهُ: إِنَّمَا تَنْتُجُ أي : إِنَّما يُستدلُّ على إنتاجها نتيجة صادقة في جميعِ الصُّورِ بالخلف . . . إلخ . (قَوْلُهُ : بِالْخُلْفِ) هو إثباتُ المطلوبِ بإبطال نقيضه .
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : إِلَّا أَنَّ اشْتِرَاطَ اخْتِلَافِ ... إِلَخ) اقتصر هنا على طريقة الإسقاط، وأما طريقةُ التَّحصيل ؛ فهو أنَّ الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةَ الكُبرى تنتج معَ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ السَّالبتَيْنِ، والسَّالبةَ الكُلِّيَّةَ الكُبرى تنتج معَ الصُّغْرَيَيْنِ الموجبتَيْنِ.
(قَوْلُهُ : بِالْخُلْفِ) هوَ يجري في ضروبه الأربعة مطلقاً
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ عَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى) وهوَ يجري في الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ والثَّالث .

أَوْ) عكس (التَّرتِيبِ ، ثُمَّ) عكس (النَّتِيجَةِ)؛ أَمَّا الخُلفُ
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ عَكْسِ التَّرْتِيبِ أي : مع عكس الصُّغرى أيضاً كما يظهرُ من كلامٍ الشَّارحِ الآتي.
(قَوْلُهُ: ثُمَّ عَكْسِ النَّتِيْجَةِ) راجع لعكس التّرتيب؛ أي: ثم بعد أن تعكس الترتيب، وتأخذ نتيجة ذلك العكس؛ تعكس تلك النتيجة.
(قَوْلُهُ : أمَّا الْخُلْفِ... إلخ) اعلَمُ أنَّ الخلفَ يجري في الضُّروبِ الأربعة، وأنَّ عكس الكُبرى يجري في الضّرب الأوَّل، وهو المركَّبُ مِن موجبةٍ كلّيَّةٍ صغرى، وسالبةٍ كلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى. وفي الضَّرْبِ الثَّالث: وهو المركَّبُ من موجبةٍ جزئيَّةٍ صُغرى، وسالبة كلّيّةٍ كُبرى، وأنَّ عكس الترتيب، ثمَّ عكس النتيجة يجري في الضَّرْبِ الثَّاني فقط، وهو المركَّبُ من سالبة كلّيّةٍ صُغرى وموجبةٍ كلِّيَّةٍ كُبرى، فتدبَّرُ .
العطّار
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ عَكْسِ التَّرْتِيبِ ثُمَّ عَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ وَهُمَا إِنَّما يجريانِ فِي الضَّرْبِ الثَّاني لا غير .
Chapter: On the Second Figure
But the condition that the minor and major premises differ in quality eliminates eight, and the condition that the major is universal eliminates a further four, so the productive moods remaining are four. Then these moods produce only by khulf (reductio), or by conversion of the major, or by conversion of order, then conversion of the conclusion. As for khulf...

Al-Dusuqi: (His saying: 'differ in quality'...) i.e., in quality. (His saying: 'eight') because either both are affirmative, yielding four possibilities (first universal or particular, second likewise, product 2×2=4), or both negative, also four, total eight. (His saying: 'condition that the major is universal eliminates a further four') because if the major is not universal but particular, it is either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, then the minor is negative, and the minor is either universal or particular, two possibilities. If negative, then the minor is affirmative, and the minor is either universal or particular, two possibilities. So total four. (His saying: 'so the productive moods remaining are four') because the universal major is either affirmative, then the minor is negative, either universal or particular (two possibilities), or the universal major is negative, then the minor is affirmative, either universal or particular (two possibilities), total four. (His saying: 'produce only') meaning that their producing a true conclusion in all cases is demonstrated by khulf, etc. (His saying: 'by khulf') it is establishing the desired by refuting its opposite.

Al-Attar: (His saying: 'except that the condition of difference...') He confined here to the method of elimination. As for the method of acquisition, it is that the affirmative universal major produces with the two negative minors, and the negative universal major produces with the two affirmative minors. (His saying: 'by khulf') it runs in all four moods absolutely. (His saying: 'or by conversion of the major') it runs in the first and third moods.

Or conversion of order, then conversion of the conclusion. As for khulf...
Al-Dusuqi: (His saying: 'or conversion of order') i.e., together with conversion of the minor as will appear from the commentator's statement. (His saying: 'then conversion of the conclusion') refers to conversion of order; i.e., after you convert the order and take the result of that conversion, you then convert that result. (His saying: 'As for khulf...') Know that khulf runs in all four moods, and that conversion of the major runs in the first mood, which is composed of an affirmative universal minor and a negative universal major, and in the third mood, which is composed of an affirmative particular minor and a negative universal major. And that conversion of order then conversion of the conclusion runs only in the second mood, which is composed of a negative universal minor and an affirmative universal major. So reflect.

Al-Attar: (His saying: 'or conversion of order then conversion of the conclusion') and these two only run in the second mood, no other.
Source pages p. 803

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) on a matn discussing the productive moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first states the conditions that reduce the number of possible mood combinations to four, then introduces the methods of proof for these moods. Al-Dusuqi and al-Attar provide detailed explanations.

The matn begins by addressing the second figure (qiyas al-awwal likely refers to the first of the remaining figures after the first?). It specifies two conditions: the minor and major premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and the major premise must be universal. These conditions eliminate eight and then four combinations from the total sixteen possible, leaving four productive moods.

Al-Dusuqi explains the elimination: without the quality condition, there are sixteen combinations (4×4). The quality condition removes eight: four where both are affirmative and four where both are negative. The universality condition on the major removes four more: those where the major is particular. The remaining four are: (1) universal affirmative major with universal negative minor, (2) universal affirmative major with particular negative minor, (3) universal negative major with universal affirmative minor, (4) universal negative major with particular affirmative minor. These are the only valid moods in the second figure.

Al-Attar notes that the matn uses the method of elimination, but one could also directly list the productive moods: a universal affirmative major produces with the two negative minors, and a universal negative major produces with the two affirmative minors.

The matn then states that these four moods produce conclusions only through four methods: khulf (reductio ad absurdum), conversion of the major premise ('aks al-kubra), conversion of order ('aks al-tartib), and conversion of the conclusion ('aks al-natija). Al-Dusuqi and al-Attar specify which method applies to which mood.

Khulf works in all four moods. It works by assuming the opposite of the desired conclusion, combining it with one premise to derive a contradiction with the other premise. For example, take a valid second figure syllogism: Every human is an animal (major), No stone is an animal (minor), therefore No stone is human. To prove by khulf, assume Some stone is human (negation of conclusion). From Every human is animal, we infer Some stone is animal, which contradicts No stone is animal. Hence, the assumption is false, proving the conclusion.

Conversion of the major premise applies in the first and third moods. In these moods, the minor is affirmative and the major is negative universal. Converting the major (e.g., No A is B becomes No B is A) yields a first figure syllogism. For example, from Every human is animal (minor) and No stone is animal (major), converting the major gives No animal is stone, then with the minor we get No human is stone, which then converts to the original conclusion. The conversion of the major is valid because a universal negative converts to itself.

Conversion of order then conversion of the conclusion applies only in the second mood, which has a negative universal minor and an affirmative universal major. Conversion of order swaps the premises, then conversion of the conclusion yields the desired result. For example, from No stone is human (minor) and Every human is animal (major), converting order gives Every human is animal (as minor) and No stone is human (as major), which is a first figure syllogism with conclusion No stone is animal, but the desired conclusion is No animal is stone? Actually, careful: The second mood is negative universal minor and affirmative universal major. The conclusion should be a universal negative. By converting order, the premises become a first figure syllogism producing a conclusion that then needs conversion to match the original. The commentary states that conversion of order together with conversion of the conclusion works only for this mood.

The passage clarifies a common misunderstanding that all possible combinations in the second figure are productive; in fact only four are, and each requires a specific proof method.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a method of proof for the second figure moods according to the passage?
A) Khulf
B) Conversion of the major
C) Conversion of the minor
D) Conversion of order then conversion of the conclusion

Key Takeaway
The second figure has only four productive moods, each provable by specific methods: khulf (all), conversion of major (first and third), conversion of order+conclusion (second), and khulf only for the fourth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the second figure syllogism: 'Every Muslim is mortal; No angel is mortal; therefore No angel is Muslim.' Identify its mood and state which proof methods can be used to validate it.
?
Construct a valid second figure syllogism on a topic from fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicating drink is haram; No water is haram; therefore No water is an intoxicating drink' is not second figure because the middle term is 'haram'? Actually correct: 'Every intoxicant is haram; No water is haram; therefore No water is an intoxicant' is second figure with negative major? Let's adjust: 'Every intoxicant is haram; No water is haram' is major negative, minor affirmative universal? Major: No water is haram (universal negative), Minor: Every intoxicant is haram (universal affirmative) -> that's first figure? Wait, second figure has middle term predicate in both. Here middle 'haram' is predicate in minor and subject in major? Actually, minor: every intoxicant is haram (haram predicate), major: no water is haram (haram predicate), so middle is predicate in both, that's second figure. But the major is universal negative, minor is universal affirmative, conclusion should be universal negative, but 'No water is an intoxicant' is valid? Check: every intoxicant is haram, no water is haram -> no water is an intoxicant (valid, second figure mood Cesare). So that works. Then prove it using conversion of the major or khulf.
